THE RED PAGE.
A. Man who Fought for Humanity.
I.
EOBERT GREEN INGERSOLL was
born at Dresden, a township in
the state of New York, U.S.A., on
11th August, 1833. He died, it appears, in
New York eity, on 22nd July, 1899, in the
66th year of his age. As an orator, he
was one of the most notable of the century ;
as a man, he was among the greatest that
America has given to the world.
Ingersoll's father was a Congregational
minister, bigger-brained than many minis-
ters, yet strict in the observance of doctrine,
and held within narrow bounds by his
church adherents. “ Every church mem-
ber,” wrote Ingersoll, “ with a creed, like
a club, in his hand, stands guard over the
brain of the minister.”
Of his mother, Ingersoll could never speak
without emotion. She was a woman of
passionate sympathies, and her son believed
that he owed her all his best.
After school-days, Ingersoll studied law
and practised in the lesser country towns
gradually gaining reputation. When the
Civil War came he threw up everything to
follow the army ; served through the whole
campaign ; and became a colonel of cavalry
At the close of the war he re-took his pro
fession, and threw himself into politics as an
ardent Republican. He was made At
torney-General of Illinois in 1866, anc
gradually acquired national fame as a law
yer and orator. His anti-Christian addresses
were the most effective ever delivered: at the
opposition and obloquy they gained him he
laughed serenely, and his life turned enemies
into friends. He never descended to per-
sonalities. “I oppose Catholicism,” he said,
* ‘ not Catholics ; Presbyterianism, not Pres-
byterians ; Christianity, not Christians.
When I see a man with rheumatism or
religion I pity the man, and hate the rheu-
matism or religion.”
In middle life Ingersoll returned to New
York, where for many years has been his
home. As a lawyer he was employed in
special cases as consultant or pleader, anc
reached the topmost rank of his profession.
He made money fast, and spent it liberally—
according to his creed.
It is as an opponent of religion that
Ingersoll is best known ; but his opposition
to religion was only one phase in a full and
harmonious life. Before all things Ingersoll
was human—and humane. He preached
the Gospel of Love. His charities were
boundless. The purity of his private
life silenced slanderers. The happiness of
his private life was such as falls to the lot of
few.
He surrounded himself with beauty—
according to his creed ; opening his brain to
books, music, art, friends—all the best that
life could give him. He made a visit to
Europe, but the offer of high diplomatic
preferment could not persuade him to go
there permanently. “I am unhappy away
from my country,” he confessed, and
When I have been in other countries I have saic
to myself, “After all, my country is the best ! ’
And when I came back and saw the old flag
flying, it seemed as though the air, from pure joy,
had burst into blossom.
Ingersoll died, as he had often expressed
a wish to die, before the decay of age had,
mastered him. He was, indeed, so spend-
thrift of his energies, he embraced Life so
fully, that the serene old age which he ad-
mired could not be his. In recent years it
was plain that his constitution was breaking
up, and he narrowed the sphere of his
external life, reserving himself to his home.
Personally Ingersoll was of the middle
height and broad, with a strong neck sup-
porting a grand head, showing a perfect oval
of face and brow. In his prime he looked
splendidly vital, the embodiment of physical
and mental force. Few such men are born,
and the country which bears them may well
be proud. They are foremost soldiers in the
liberation-war of humanity.
11.
After hearing Ingersoll speak, it was easy
to believe enthusiasts who called him the
greatest orator that ever lived. If the
essence of oratory resides in power of
statement and persuasion, in dominating
and convincing an audience, in flooding the
dullest facts with poetry, lifting the meanest
statement by the aid of illustration and
argument to the semblance of eternal truth,
and in doing these things sincerely, referring
everything to the highest standards of
honour, of integrity, of patriotism, that the
orator knows, then Ingersoll was as great an
orator as ever lived. He was irresistible,
and no man could be more. His hearers
hung on his words : they were always simple
and intelligible, even when Ingersoll rhe-
torically decorated them with the imagery of
which lie was such a master. And when he
rose, as lie could rise, to the height of a lofty
theme, he ma'de a permanent addition to the
stored riches of eloquence. His speech at
the Soldiers’ Re-union at Indianopolis in
1876, when he reviewed—as was his favorite-
method —a series of incidents in the past,
transmuting old memories into the keenest
emotion, is one of the intellectual treasures
of America. “The past,” he said,
The past rises before me like a dream. Again
we are in the great struggle fur national life. We
hear the sounds of preparation—the music of
boisterous drums the silver voices of heroic
bugles. We see thousands of assemblages, and
hear the appeals of orators. We see the pale
cheeks of women, arid the flushed faces of men ;
and in those assemblages we see all the dead whose
dust we have covered with flowers. We. lose,sight
of them no more. We are with them when they
enlist in the great army of freedom. W e see
them part with those they love. ... We see the
wife standing in the door with the babe in her
arms, standing in the sunlight sobbing. At the
turn of the road a hand waves—-she answers by
holding high in her loving arms the child. He is
gone, and forever.
We see. them all as they march proudly away
under the flaunting flags, keeping time to the
grand, wild music of war —marching down the
streets of the great cities—through the towns and
across the prairies—down to the fields of glory, to
do and to die for the eternal right. . . .
We are at home when the news comes that they
are dead. We see the maiden in the shadow of
her first sorrow. We see the silvered heads of old
men bowed with the last grief.
These heroes are dead. They died for liberty—
they died for us. They sleep in the land they
made free, under the flag they rendered stain-
less.
In cold print the utterance is too ornate,
too elaborate. Ingersoll’s love of rhetoric
was not guided by the best taste. He spoils
the climax in the last paragraph by adding
“ under the solemn pines, the sad hemlocks,
the tearful willows, and the embracing
vines.” There is for readers a too liberal,
too intentional selection from the arboreal
catalogue. But delivered in a voice of flutes
and trumpets, backed by the o,.dor's im-
pressive personality, his magnetic gesture,
his thrilling eye—and to an audience which
matched every sentence with memories, the
magnificence of the speech was flawless.
And even in cold print there are few pic-
tures so touching as that most intimate and
pathetic picture of the man who goes
to fight for his country, and the wife—
The wife standing in the door with the babe in
her arms, standing in the sunlight sobbing. At
the turn of the road a hand waves—she answers
by holding in her loving arms the child. He is
gone, and forever.
111.
It is easy to understand that advocates of
the current theologies writhed under Inger-
soll’s tongue. As an iconoclast he united
the force of Bradlaugh to the wit of Vol-
taire ; and withal he was so true, so manly,
that his bitterest opponents could not help
admiring him. Beside Ingersoll, Talmage
was only a windy mountebank ; even
Beecher, with all his fine emotion, seemed
sometimes shallow compared with him.
For Ingersoll buttressed his rhetoric with
reason ; his wit and sense make his
speeches first - class literature : the ordi-
nary orator’s, like Gladstone’s, are vapid
and tame when reduced to print. He sapped
the citadel of dogma with laughter, yet
exploded its fortifications with shrewd logic
which could never be forgotten. He did
not fear to be “ undignified ” ; he had no
reverence for the rules of dullards; he
spoke as he felt, and used ridicule and
sarcasm to make a way for his arguments.
He fought to win. Conceive the effect of
this upon a popular audience—
The children of Israel in the wilderness lived
on manna. There was not a change in the bill of
fare for forty years, and they knew that God
could just as well give them three square meals a
day. They remembered about the cucumbers,
and the melons, and the leeks, and the onions of
Egypt, and they said:. “Our soul abhorreth this
light bread.” Then this God got mad—you know
cooks are always touchy—and thereupon He sent
snakes to bite the men, women and children. He
also sent them quails in wrath and anger, and
while they had the flesh between their teeth He
struck thousands of them dead. He always acted
that way, all of a sudden. People had no chance
to explain—no chance to move for a new trial—
nothing. I want to know if it is reasonable He
should kill people for asking for one change of
diet in forty years. Suppose you had been board-
ing with an old lady for forty years, and she never
had a solitary thing on the table but hash, and
one morning you said : “My soul abhorreth hash.”
What would you say if she let a basketful of
rattlesnakes upon you ? Now is it possible for
people to believe this ? The Bible says that their
clothes did not wax old, they did not get shiny at
the knees or elbows ; and their shoes did not wear
out. They grew right along with them. The
little boy starting out with his first pants grew up
and his pants grew with him. Some commenta-
tors have insisted that angels attended to their
wardrobes. I never could believe it. Just think
of one angel hunting another and saying : “There
goes another button. ” I cannot believe it.
And Ingersoll's epigrams flew like bullets to
their mark—
. A creed is the ignorant past bullying the en-
lightened present.
The parasite of woman is the priest.
The first doubt was the womb and cradle of
progress.
E very church member bears the marks of collar,
chains and whip.
The church has been a charitable highwayman,
a profligate beggar, a generous pirate.
If I rob Mr. Smith, and God forgives me, how
does that help Smith ?
I am in favor of the taxation of all church pro-
perty. If that property belongs to God, he is able
to pay the tax. If we exempt anything, let us
exempt the homes of the widow and the orphan.
IV.
As a thinker, Ingersoll had little origin-
ality, and even little scope. But he had a
tight grip on the handful of things he knew ;
and as a commentator and expounder he has
never been surpassed. His mind turned
everything it touched to gold ; as soon as he
looked at it, the most barren statement put
forth buds and blossoms of imagination.
Though no poet in the ordinary sense, Inger-
soll had a fine poetic faculty, especially on
the side of form. His matter was often essen-
tially commonplace ; but he so decked it
with imagery that it became transfigured.
He was a master of rhetoric—not, indeed,
in the highest ■ sense, but for the popular
uses of an orator. When he spealks of Napo-
leon in Russia, he says that “the infantry of
the snow and the cavalry of the wild blast
scattered his legions.” Of the country
autumn he said that
The withered banners of the corn are still, and
gathered fields are growing strangely wan, while
death, poeticdeath with hands thateolourwhatthey
touch, weaves in the autumn wood its tapestries of
brown and gold.
The expression is highly poetic, though the
idea is ordinary. In this passage, too, is
seen the strong rhythm which runs through
much of Ingersoll’s prose —not always im-
proving it. His style alternated from
rhythmic to colloquial, and from colloquial to
epigrammatic. Wherever he could he used
an antithesis ; wherever he could he
sharpened the antithesis to an epigram.
It never can be necessary to throw away your
reason to save your soul.
No inan with any sense of humour ever founded
a religion.
A first cause is just as impossible as a last effect.
‘‘ Blasphemy ” is what a last year’s leaf says to
this year’s bud.
The spirit of worship is the spirit of tyranny.
The intellect has no knees.
Every creed is a rock in running water.
Humanity sweeps by it.
Faith is a lullaby sung to put the soul to sleep.
Few men have been patriotic enough to shoulder
a musket in defence of a boarding-house.
The throne and altar are twins, vultures from
the same egg.
Hundreds of such sayings are scattered
through Ingersoll’s writings—the most in-
cisive writings of America. But theological
odium has been potent to suppress his fame :
it is not respectable to refer to him in the
magazines, and the average man’s opinions
are moulded by the conventional critics.
V.
The best side of Ingersoll was his
manly side—the side which stood up for
women and children, friends and country,
exalted love,, extolled the religion of
humanity in the lines of Burns—
To make a happy fireside clime
For weans and wife :
That’s the true pathos and sublime
Of human life.
He gave the homeliest advice to his hearers,
always with a gay vein of humour.
When one of your children tells a lie, be honest
with him ; tell him you have told hundreds of
them yourself. Tell him it is not the best way;
you have tried it. Tell him as the man did in
Maine when his boy left home : “John, honesty
is the best policy: I have tried both.” Just be
honest with him. Imagine now, you are about to
whip a child five years of age. What is the child
to do ? Suppose a man, as much larger than you
as you are larger than a child five years old, should
come at you with a liberty-pole in his hand, and
in a voice of thunder shout: “Who broke that
plate ? ” There s not a solitary one of you who
wouldn’t swear you never saw' it, or that it was
cracked when you found it. When your child
commits a wrong take it in your arms, let it feel
your heart beat against its heart, let the child
know that you really and truly and sincerely
love it. Yet some Christians, good Christians,
when a child commits a fault, drive it from the
door and say, “Never do yob darken this house
again.” Think of that ! And then these same
people will get down on their knees and ask God
to take care of the child they have driven from
home. I will never ask God to take care of my
children unless I am doing my level best in that
same direction. But I will tell you what I say
to my children : “Go where you will; commit
what crime you may ; fall to what depth of degra-
dation you may ; you can never commit any crime
that will shut my door, my arms, my heart to you;
as long as I live you shall have no more sincere
friend. ” Do you know, I have seen some people
who acted as though they thought when the
Saviour said “Suffer little children to come unto
me, for of such is the kingdom of heav.en, ” that he
had a raw-hide under his mantle and made that
remark to get the children within striking dis-
tance.
Or again—
Give the children a chance. Don’t try to teach
them something they can never learn. Don’t
make that poor girl play ten years on a piano
when she has no ear for music, and when she has
practised until she can play ‘ ‘ Bonaparte Crossing
the Alps,” and you can’t tell after she has played
it whether he ever got across or not.
Or again—
They say woman brought all the trouble into the
world. I don’t care if she did. I would rather
live in a world full of trouble with the woman I
love, than live in a heaven with nobody but men.
The man who has really won the love of one
good woman in the world, I do not care if he dies
in the ditch a beggar, his life has been a success.
How impotent were pulpit revilings to injure
a man who felt and spoke like that ! And
Ingersoll was continually expressing such
sentiments in such phrases. His anti-
religious writings are the lesser part of his
published work. He never wearied of praising
the common man, the labourer, of pointing
out how his lot could be bettered, and yet
how in, many ways his lot was enviable.
YI.
As a writer, Ingersoll is handicapped by
his oratory—the whole of his writings were
first spoken, and the cumulative rhetoric of
the orator is often too extravagant for
literary taste. It is noticeable that Inger-
so , en . repeated himself, putting the same
thought in different forms, or using the
same passage in different speeches ; and it
will be a pity if he has not edited his work
foi a final publication. So far as known,
with the exception of one or two addresses’
he has been content to think of himself as
orator, and leave the perpetuation of his
literary fame to others. Noticeable, too,
is it that all Ingersoll’s vital ideas
were exploited, and usually thrown into
their best form, before he was fifty.
For the last twenty years he has been
brilliantly repeating himself. When his
fund of youthful impressions and reflections
was exhausted, he found few others of value.
All that he could do was to add grace or force
to the expression of his garnerings in the
past. Yet, with every drawback made, In-
gersoll is a very considerable literary figure.
His essay on “ Shakespeare ” is too enthusi-
astic to be critical ; but it is perhaps
the most vital short appreciation of
Shakespeare extant. His “ Defence of Free
Thought is a most moving and strenuous
appeal. Even his political addresses are
memorable, apart from their oratorical value.
For Ingeipjoll was a good patriot; he be-
lieved that America was the greatest country
in the world, and that it could be made
greater ; and there is universal value in his
praise of the sentiment of devotion to the
Flag—that fluttering emblem which is so
little, and means so immeasurably much.
And, in social life, he proved his patriotism
was not merely a sentiment. He was a
practical philosopher, who wrote that
The best form of charity is extravagance : the
prodigality of the rich is the providence of the
poor.
And this is a concentration of Ingersoll’s
teaching—
The place to be happy is here ; the time to be
happy is now ; and the way to be happy is to
make others happy.
“Let us make Heaven on Earth," said
Ingersoll, and it is a final summing-up of
human philosophy. You cannot go beyond
that.
VII.
And so this brilliant, loving, honest man
reaches the end lie had so often pondered.
No words can more fitly shrine his memory
than those which he himself spoke over the
grave of Courtlandt Palmer—his friend less
great in accomplishment, no less noble in
character.
My friends : A thinker of pure thoughts, a
speaker of brave words, a doer of generous deeds,
has reached the silent haven that all the dead
have reached, and where the voyage of every life
must end.
In the monotony of subservience, in the multi-
tude of blind followers, nothing is more inspiring
than a free and independent man one who gives
and asks reasons ; one who demands freedom and
gives what he demands ; one who refuses to be
slave or master. Such a man was he to whom we
pay the tribute of respect and love.
He preserved the realm of mind from the inva-
sion of brute force, and protected the children of
the brain from the Herod of authority.
He investigated for himself the questions, the
problems and the mysteries of life. Majorities
were nothing to him. No error could be old
enough—popular, plausible, or profitable enough
—to bribe his judgment or to keep his conscience
still.
He knew that, next to finding truth, the
greatest joy is honest search.
He was a believer in intellectual hospitality, in
the fair exchange of thought, in good mental man-
ners, in the amenities of the soul, in the chivalry
of discussion.
He denied the supernatural—the phantoms and
the ghosts that fill the twilight-land of fear. To
him and for him there was but one religion—the
religion of pure thoughts, of noble words, of self-
denying deeds, of honest work for all the world—
the religion of Help and Hope.
Facts were the foundation of his faith ; history
was his prophet; reason his guide; duty his
deity ; happiness the end ; intelligence the means.
He knew that man must be the providence of
man.
He did not believe in Religion and Science, but
in the Religion of Science —that is to say, wisdom
glorified by love, the Saviour of our race—the
religion that conquers prejudice and hatred, that
drives all superstition from the mind, that
ennobles, lengthens and enriches life, that drives
from every home the wolves of want, from every
heart the fiends of selfishness and fear, and from
every brain the monsters of the night.
He lived and labored for his fellow-men. He
sided with the weak and poor against the strong
and rich. He welcomed light. His face was ever
towards the East.
By the grave of man stands the angel of silence.
No one can tell which is better—Life with its
gleams and shadows, its thrills and pangs, its.
ecstasy and tears, its wreaths and thorns, its
crowns, its glories and Golgothas, or Death with
its peace, its cool and placid brow that hath
within no memory or fear of grief or pain.
Farewell, dear friend. The world is better for
your life —The world is braver for your death.
Farewell ! We loved you living, and we love
you now.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T J. Livingston, whom the Kingston
Govt, secured to move the Address-in-Reply in
S.A. Assembly, has the further distinction of
being the first man to export frozen mutton from
South Australia. He remembers, too, that he
lost money by the transaction. But the province
gained.
The providence that guards
drunken men. Recently, in Elizabeth-st., Sydney,
saw an aged man, who, judging by his appearance,
was smitten with palsy, afflicted with corns
and bunions, twisted with rheumatism, and hard
of hearing, try to cross between two trams, from
opposite directions and another following on. A
friendly conductor, with wonderful presence of
mind, seized him by the collar and pulled him
into the carriage. And did the ancient one fall
down and worship him ? No. He sat in the
doorway of the tram and libelled the conductor’s
mother, and, when the tram stopped, hobbled off
to report him. And the conductor smiled plea-
santly through it all.
Home.
The blue Pacific waves ran clear
In ridges to my feet,
And from their range, and from the gums
Gushed odours keen and sweet.
Squat in the sheoak’s golden flame,
The billy boiled too soon,
Por all was golden drowsiness
That golden afternoon.
I fell asleep and saw myself
Within an ancient room
Of English grange, a white-haired man,
Lit by a ruddy gloom ;
And in my heart a whisper went,
A sense of great release,
I knew that I was home again,
With life and death at peace.
The toil was over—God, how strange !
The tears were on my face—
I need not wander for my bread
From homeless place to place ;
B it here a breathing space I had
Unknown to fate, my foe,
Before I went the outland way
That mortal men must go.
I looked around the panelled walls
And saw dim portraits there
Of noble knight and noble dame
And girlhood sweet and fair;
And in old earven cabinets
Were letters., faded, sere,
With folded tresses, long dead flowers,
And mark of falling tear.
Old perfumes stirred before my hand,
Old heart-breaks bled anew ;
The pink was there, the daffodil,
And, for remembrance, rue ;
My spirit touched the unseen dead
Behind the written word.
The heart touched and wept,
- - -And spirit weeping heard :
Grief for unforgotten hours
Beyond recall for aye,
Regret for lonely youth and love
November tears in May :
A note of joy rose in the sound,
Of coming home at last,
My name was soft on waiting lips
Loved in the ancient past.
My spirit with her sense of rest
Behind the brightening veil,
Had pity on my body’s grief,
For her made weak and pale ;
And as the winds grew to their pitch
Grappling like deadly foes,
She gave unto my mortal part
An hour of deep repose.
A.nd by the winter fire I sat,
Remembered faces came,
By love and death made wondrous sweet,
Of purest spirit flame ;
The hour beside the ruin grey,
The kiss by summer night,
So beautiful, so sad, were seen
In delioate clear light.
Children, unborn, but passionate
With mournful loveliness,
Smiled through their tears, and she was there
Who did my long years bless ;
And all my simple pilgrim gold—
Old print with passing gleam
On ivied tower, old summer walks
By haunted mount and stream.
The flame paled on the hollied crest,
And on the mistletoe,
And something whispered in my heart
’Twas time for me to go;
Aside my spirit, seraph-fresh,
Stood looking on the clay
Chastened for service of the soul,
Then turned towards the Day.
I woke ; and to my dreaming eyes
The hour was as before ;
But still I knew my life was changed.
Then and for evermore.
The wistful passion in my breast
Is not for earthly home,
But I must seek a City still
Wherever I may roam.
Tas.
JAMES HEBBLETHWA E.

A Baby Uonl
Did-you ever see one? It is as playful and
hapxless as a kitten. You might have one
with you for weeks and not suspect danger.
But, beware! Some day it will surely
spring, and the fight for life is at hand.'
It is just so with a cough. Yoii may carry
one with you for weeks and not think of
danger. But the danger is there. Some day
the fight will be on, and it is a question which
will win, Consumption or You.
Ayer's
Cherry
Pectoral
promptly cures all baby coughs: but it can-
not always cure the full grown coughs of the
most advanced stages of disease. Yet even
here it will bring eomfort and ease. Then
nse it early for acute colds, coughs, bron-
chitis and all pulmonary troubles.
Beware of cheap Imitations. .. See that the
name, Ayer’s Cherry Pectoral, is blown in the
"Class Of each bottle. Put up in large and small
bottles.

“ D.J.” : Was in a Moore-street (Syd
ney) office t’other day, when a sudden increase
in the steady downpour aggravated two gentle-
men. They looked like gentlemen,,.though I pre-
sently decided that they were only coves. They first
contemplated the wall of rain with curses. Then
to the telephone: “ That you, Joe ? Haw—well,
bring my umbrella down at once ; and, Joe, don’t
let it get wet.” Both gents were arrayed in
waterproofs and leggings, and altogether suffi-
ciently protected against the elements to have
gone to the Pole. About twenty minutes later,
Joe, a delicate-looking lad, appeared with the
brolly carefully wrapped in brown paper, and as
far as possible tucked under his coat. He had no
overcoat, and the water bubbled from his cracked
boots and dripped from the sleeves and the edges
of his garments. His aristocratic master ignored
all that, and briefly directed him to go with Mr.
, and bring back the “ gamp ” for the boss
himself. Said the other man: “Be d , I’m all
right; he’s wet through now, and will catch his
death.” Replied the first gent.: “Really, my
deah feller, you amuse me. He don’t feel it.
He’s accustomed to it; it rolls off him like water
off a duck. Haw, haw ! ” The other hesitated,
and then said with a smile: “Oh, well, if that’s the
case, I’ll go.”. He plunged into the deluge, with
the thinly-clad and unprotected Joe running
behind him to fetch back the brolly when done
with. Last I saw of Joseph, he was struggling
over the face of this democratic country with the
water pouring out of his boots and his pockets
and his hat, and his clothes plastered to'him, and
his share of the umbrella-footing was nil.

Two Impulses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He looked down afc her as she knelt at his feet,
buttoning his boots with deft fingers. Rising,
she reached on tip-toe, and adjusted his tie, and
touched his hair caressingly.
“ What a pity ! ” he murmured.
She looked up wonderingly.
“ What a pity!” he repeated. “ All those little
tricks for making a man happy —no woman I
ever met knew quite so much ; where did you
learn them ?—wasted on every drunken brute you
see. They ought to be kept for one who could
appreciate them—and one only ! ”
She looked up again. “ Who’d appreciate me? ”
He was a man of moods. “/ would ! ” he said
suddenly. “I would”; and he looked into her
eyes.
Next day he married her.
* * * *
In three months she tired of it all. He was so
good and kind—but . He never hinted at the
past—not even when the little tricks that he was
to appreciate, and did, had one by one disap-
peared. Shortly afterwards she disappeared, and
where he found her he left her.
His friends wondered why Follet went to the
bad so quickly. He lost his billet, then his
friends, then his self-respect. In four years he
drifted into a tattered, disreputable, beer-soaked
loafer, and even the memory of that night he
found her had faded. But as he sank, she rose.
She was now in the height of her profession—the
exclusive property of a very rich man.
* * • *
One night she found him, and knew him. Her
carriage knocked him down, and frightened him—
that was all. He was drunk, dirty, ragged and
muddy. But she knew him.
“Jack!” A scream—then she dung to him,
sobbing.
“Jack ! oh, Jack ! ”
A drunken curse, and a blow.
• * • *
Now all the little tricks are his, and his only.
But he, being merely a common drunken brute,
does not appreciate them.
A.J.R.

A jury at Georgetown (Q.)took nine hours
to consider before they arrived at a verdict. After
about five hours they asked the Judge to be allowed
to partake of a little refreshment. Judge refused
promptly, and satirised the sluggish jurymen.
Would not allow them to go for a walk (under
police-escort), and so escape for a few minutes the
impure air of the jury-room. What a farce to
coerce men into unanimity in this ridiculous
fashion ! Why not revive the old English fashion
under which a Judge could cart the jury all over
the country until they agreed ? It would be just
about as sensible.

Bendigo Independent has been jumping
hard on the local mission-system of one-Chow-one-
girl, and recalling the notorious New York
scandals due to this sort of thing. Where anent
a Celestial bard :
Any white woman’s body, so they’d have us allow,
Is worth ten times as much as the soul of a Chow—
’Tis a doctrine they never can bring to agree
With the tenets of faith that were taught at Judeel
These sanctified damsels who show us the way
To warble our hymns and (apparently,) pray—
They have given their hearts and their souls to the Lord—
So why should their bodies not go “ by the board ?”
On my word as a heathen, I solemnly swear
That if I can “ nially ” my teacheress fair,
The great Joss of my fathers I’ll strictly eschew
And worship whatever she wishes me to I
Thus each “fattee white gel,” with her ravishing charms,
Serenely may rest in her Chinaman’s arms,
Content in the knowledge her yielding has scored
One precious gem more for the crown of the Lord.
In the sight of the Lord a man’s soul is a gem,
Whether be he descended from Japhet or Shem,
Which is why His poor handmaidens do what they can
To rescue the soul of the poor yellow man.
Though the body be fouled, yet the spirit is pure—
And the body must perish, the spirit endure—
Therefore seek not of that which is mortal in ye,
But piump for the soul of the Heathen Chinee !
SIN FAT, "Clistian."

SATURDAY, AUGUST 5, 1899.
Westralia’s Hesitation.
Westkalra has referred the Federal question to a
select committee of Parliament, with instructions
to doze over it till not later than sth September.
After that it will present the results of its deep
sleep to Parliament, and Parliament will act or
otherwise. There is no attempt on the part of
the old sandgroping element, which still controls
the Legislature though it forms only a small por-
tion of the community, to disguise the fact that
it is against union. It doesn’t like new things ;
it doesn't like to be disturbed ; it loves to lie and
sleep away the long languid days in the middle of
the road and not have to wake up to find the foot-
marks of a hurrying world all over its cuticle.
And it desires to know why anybody
should trouble over anything, and why the
old things that were good enough for its
forefathers are not good enough for it. Also,
it wants to know for certain whether, if
it federates, the Commonwealth Government
will guarantee to build a transcontinental railway
to Perth. It wants to know about some other
things also, but it wants to know about that rail-
way with a very great want indeed.
• * * *
The Federal Constitution offers a good many
things to Westralia, if the Sandgroper would only
consider it apart from his usual narrow prejudice
against the East., It offers him, to begin with,
eight times as much representation in the Federal
Senate as his population fairly entitles him to, and a
fraction more representation in the House of
Representatives than he is fairly entitled to —
which means so many more members to barrack
for the coveted railway. It gives him this if he
comes in now; only Original States are entitled
to equal representation in the Senate. The Bill also
offers another special concession; clause 95 provides
that for five years after the beginning of the
Commonwealth, Westralia shall have the right to
charge duties on the produce of all the other pro-
vinces while they must admit the produce of Wes-
tralia absolutely free. Timber is Westralia’s third
largest export, and this means that the timber
trade of the Eastern provinces, now so largely
monopolised by Maoriland, will be, to a great
extent, handed over to Westralia instead, for
Maoriland timber will be a duty-burdened article
from outside, and W.A. timber will be a duty-
free artiole from inside the Commonwealth. Join-
ing the union now means that Westralia will still
have a chanoe to develope its coal industry for
five years, by shutting out the coal of the other
provinces per medium of a heavy import tax,
while it can sell coal to the adjacent South Aus-
tralian market free of all tax, and thus secure a
valuable export trade. It can keep on protect-
ing its own grain against that of the Itast
for five years, and yet it can export gram to
the East free of duty. And Westralia has to
consider the solemn fact that, in two years from
now, if it progresses as it did last year, it will not
only be able to supply itself with grain, but will
have a surplus for export, and to be guaranteed
the free run of all the markets of the Eastern
States with that surplus, while itself protected
against the competition of the Eastern States in
years when they have a surplus, will give it no
small advantage.
* * * *
And this is only the beginning of the special ad-
vantages which the Eastern States offer to the
pampered West. During the period of five years
in which Westralia is to be protected against the
competition of its neighbors, while they are not
protected against it, Westralia has a chance to
become with a rush one of the great manufacturing
provinces of Australasia. Any person who starts
a new industry or puts up a new factory in
Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, S. A., or Tasmania
will have the free run of these States as a market
for his goods, but he won’t have the free run of
Westralia. And any one who puts up a new
factory in Perth will have the free run of all the
States as a market for his goods, while for five
years he will be protected against all competition
in his own market. For five years it will pay
better to start a new industry in W.A. than in
any other province, and, by the end of that time,
Westralia’s manufactures and agriculture should
have gained a start which will keep them abreast
of all eastern competition.
* * * *
At present Westralia imports from the other
provinces a very large quantity of goods —spirits,
beer, and tobacco' —which pay excise duty, tinder
the provincial system these duties are part of the
revenue of the provinces where the goods are pro-
duced —the excise on the beer, spirits, &c., which
Westralia imports from Victoria or N.S.W. goes
to swell the public income of Victoria or N.S.W.,
and not a copper of the money reaches the Wes-
tralian Treasury. Under the Commonwealth Bill
(clause 93, section 1) all this excise revenue will,
for at least five years, be paid to Westralia, and
will furnish it with a new and very respectable
source of income so long as it continues a large
importer of exeiseable good from the East. And
by the end of that time Westralia, being protected
for five years against the competition of the other
provinces while the other provinces are left
absolutely open to West Australian competition,
will probably be a beer and spirit exporting
province, and independent of eastern supplies.
« * * *
As regards the coveted transcontinental rail-
way, there are two sides to that question also. The
dominant political party in Westralia at present
inclines to stand out of the union unless it receives
a guarantee that the Federal Government will
build the much-desired line. It proposes, in fact,
to make that line the price of its adhesion to the
Commonwealth. If Westralia comes in now it
gains the advantage of being an Original State—in
other words it will have a far larger number of
members in the Australian Parliament to cry out
day and night about the urgent need for an
across-Australian railway than it is likely
to have if it joins later on. The State
which comes in later on is liable to be admitted
merely on the basis of representation according to
population, which might leave W.A. with one
senator instead of six. If Westralia comes in it is
almost certain to gain the powerful support of
N.S.W. and S.A. for its line—a railway from
Sydney through Broken Hill to the West would
suit N.S.W. admirably, and the connection of
Adelaide with the West would probably suit S.A.
down to the ground also. And, in any case, the
Commonwealth will be forced to construct a
transcontinental line—probably more than one
some day for defence purposes. But if Westralia
stands out, then the defence of W.A. won’t be the
Commonwealth’s funeral, and it will be under no
such obligation. And if Westralia refuses to
come in unless it gets its great railway guaranteed,
a large section in the East will simply reply that
it will be very glad to leave W.A. out. The com-
mercial class in the East is none too well pleased to
have a partner who is allowed for a number of years
to shut out its goods by a border tariff while it can’t
shut out that partner’s goods in return. And the
large States, which view with'a certain jealousy
the preponderance of the small States in the
Senate, won’t grieve any too much if there is one
small State the less.
* * * *
If the West becomes an Original State of che
Commonwealth, it should have a considerable in-
crease of revenue for the first year or two, at all
events; it will still have its customs duties—
foreign and interprovincial—-and it will collect, in
addition, a good deal of excise revenue which now
goes to other provinces. It should have no serious
decrease of revenue for at least five years —till its
interprovincial duties are abolished. Even after
that the loss of revenue is estimated by its own
authorities at some <£300,000 per annum—not
much more than it lost last year without Federa-
tion. This would leave it with a very much larger
revenue per head than any other province.
And there is no guarantee that it won’t lose
a much larger proportion of its customs
revenue if it doesn’t federate. The new majo-
rity is beginning to kick furiously against
Westralia’s revenue tariff; and while the
Federal majority (if Westralia federates) may
build an Australian tariff that will reduce W. A.’s
customs revenue by £300,000 a year, there is no
possible guarantee that the local majority (if Wes-
tralia doesn’t federate, and when the goldfields
can no longer be deprived of fair representation)
will not construct a tariff that will reduce the
customs revenue by £750,000 a year. Hitherto
the sandgroper minority, which wants its agricul-
tural and other industries protected, has made the
tariff. Very soon the majority from the East,
whose main industry—gold-mining—doesn’t need
any Protection, will make it instead ; and there is
no knowing with what results.
* • * *
Federation offers Westralia its first ehanee of
absolute manhood suffrage. Also its first chance
of an Old Age Pension system—Old Age Pensions
are one of the subjects transferred to the Federal
Government, and the democratic majority from
N.S.W., Victoria, and S.A. is certain to force the
matter on and get the aged Sanlgroper his pen-
sion at the earliest possible date. (The Sand-
groper, as a man who generally lives to a great
age, has a special interest in this matter.) It will
almost certainly bring about a measure of direct
taxation to make up for the abolition, after five
years, of the W.A. border customs—in other
words, the great English, South Australian,
Victorian, and other land and mining and trading
and banking syndicates which now own most of
Westralia will have to pay a larger _ share ot
Westralia’s expenditure. It gives the big, empty
province a fair chance of getting its transconti-
nental line built at the cost of all Australia, while
it is dead-certain that the rest of Australia won t
pay a single copper towards that line if W.A. doesn t
federate. It will quite possibly save W. A. from
having its industries crushed some day by I ree-
trade. A Federal Government, with all the nidus-
tries of Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Adelaide
to protect, will never go in for Freetrade, while the
mining element in Westralia, when it gets on top,
might do so. A mining community is just the
one which Freetrade suits well enough. Ihe
sandgroper in general isn’t ail animal of dis-
course looking before and after ; yet he basil t to
look very far before him to see that there are
many good cash possibilities for him in I ederation.
Land Grant Kail way Swindles and
Other Swindles.
Tasmania has been giving away private railway
concessions of late with a generous hand, and its
Parliament is just now considering some more
matters of the same description. At present
it is busy giving a syndicate power to build
the Great Midland and West Coast Railway, 100
miles in length, and making the syndicate the
very handsome present of 200,000 acres of land by
way of encouragement. Tasmania’s reason for
making this ghastly foolish bargain is that it can t
afford—or thinks it can’t—to build the new wes-
tern railways itself, and it has the strange bee
humming in its brain that the said railways will
cost the country less if built and owned by a private
syndicate than if built and owned by the State.
It is such a grotesque idea that any com-
munity gifted with the power to see through
a large hole in the daytime (it being provided
that there is no obstruction in the _ hole) should
know better. But every Australasian State has
been seized, at one time or another, with the de-
lusion that in some way the community saves the
expense of a railway (that it gets the railway for
nothing, in fact, or at least gets it a great deal
more cheaply) by letting a crowd of private
boodlers build it than by doing the work on its
own account. In fact, some of them have that
crazy inspiration permanently.
* * * *
As a very simple and obvious matter of fact,
the average privately-owned railway must cost
the community vastly more than the publicly-
owned one, and Tasmania, by gridironing its
West Coast with syndicate land-grant lines, is
throwing heaps of its hard-earned money into the
sea. Assuming that the 100 miles of the Great
Midland line costs .£lO,OOO a mile for construction
and equipment, the Tasmanian State could build
it for <£1,000,000. It would do the good old bor-
row in London, and if it offered per cent, it
could get £1,000,000 net; that is to say, it could
sell the loan at a premium which would cover all
expenses. All the railway would have to provide
under Government ownership would therefore be
working expenses and £32,500 a year interest. If
the people are charged enough in freights and
fares to cover these outgoings—well and good. If
they are not, they are charged the difference in
some form of direct or indirect taxation. Either
way, the community pays the above amount and
no more.
* * * *
The private syndicate doesn’t build the line any
more cheaply than the State; it advertises for
tenders in the same way, and accepts the lowest
tender in the same way, and probably the
same contractor does the job. But it has
multitudes of extra expenses, from which the
State is free. It spends money shoving its Bill
through Parliament. Very often the syndicate
which gets the concession sells it to a company
for £lOO,OOO or £250,000 or £1,000,000 of clear
boodle according to the size of the job—which
simply means that it sells a certain portion of the
Tasmanian people to the co. for the purpose of
being squeezed, just as the monarchs of the
Middle Ages used to raise the wind by selling a
rich Jew or two, who seemed as if they would
crush well, to some speculative subject. This
necessarily increases the cost of the railway.
Then the private co. has to maintain an expensive
office in Tasmania, and generally another in Lon-
don (the State needs no office in London, and a
new railway makes only an imperceptible addition
to the cost of the already-existent railway offices);
it has to pay directors (the State needs no direc-
tors) ; it hires a manager (the State can run an extra
railway, or two, or three, or six, extra railways,
with the manager it has already) ; and it requires
enough rolling stock to cope with its traffic at the
busiest season (the State in many cases only re-
quires a little new rolling stock, and meets the de-
mands of extra busy periods by shifting cars and
locomotives from other lines where the closing of
the wheat or wool season has left things tempor-
arily slack).
* * * *
Then the syndicate has company-flotation ex-
penses to pay, also brokerage, advertising, and
multitudes of other charges, from which the State
is exempt. By reason of all these matters the line
which costs the State £1,000,000 seldom costs the
private syndicate less than £1,500,000. The pri-
vate co. generally borrows all that it can of this at
5 per cent, (the Emu Bay Railway Co., of Tas-
mania, is raising the wind at 5 per cent., and the
Wellington and Manawatu Co. pays the same
rate, while the Midland Co. of Maoriland paid no
less than 6 per cent, on its borrowings). Also, it
wants a 5 per cent, dividend, or more if possible,
on its own capital—the Silverton Railway Co.
charges enough to make a 50 per cent, dividend on
its capital. Therefore the private co. aims to make
the line pay working-expenses and at least 5 per
cent, on about £1,500,000 (£75,000 a-year), against
the State’s burden of working-expenses and 3|; per
cent, on £1,000,000 (£32,500). And the people of
the country where the line is located have to
supply the money in each case. It is as much the
people’s burden when a private syndicate finds the
capital as when the State finds the capital. The
million which the State would pay for the line ap-
pears in the statement of the public debt, and the
£1,500,000 which the syndicate pays does not,
but it is only a difference on paper. The people
owe the money either way, because they have to
pay the interest either way. The only difference
is that, when a private syndicate builds the line,
they have to find twice or three times as much
interest, and they pay the difference, partly in
higher rates and partly in land grants.
* * * *
Probably the Midland Railway will pay under
private management. Probably also it might not
pay (on paper) under State management. But the
circumstances are not the same. When the private
co. builds it, the State throws in 200,000 acres of
land (2,000 acres for every mile of line, or one-
eightieth part of the whole country), and the
splendid revenue which this estate will produce
when the railway has opened it up will go into the
railway co.’s revenue and make the line payable.
If the State built the line, it would get the revenue
from this land instead of the syndicate getting it,
only the money would go into the land receipts
instead of among the railway receipts, and the line
wouldn’t look payable. But if the State chose to
build the Midland line itself, and set apart the same
200,000 acres as an endowment for its own line
and paid the revenue received from these 200,000
acres into the railway treasury, then it could make
the line a great deal more profitable than the co.
can possibly do, and it could afford to charge 25
per cent, less fares and freights than the co. will
be able to do. When the results achieved by a
small private line with a gift of 200,000 acres of
land, are set against the results achieved by the
State on a similar line without the 200,000 acres
State management may not look any too profit-
able. But if both lines are put on the same basis,
then State management is immeasurably cheaper
for the people who have to find the interest.
There is no railway in Australasia which a private
co. can build and work at a profit which the
State, on similar conditions, can’t build and
work at an equal profit with 25 per cent,
lower charges. _ For the line that costs
the State a million generally costs the co.
£1,500,000 by reason of all its extra expenses';
and the State can get the money at 3| per cent.,
while the co. pays 5 per cent.
* * * «
Wherefore, the moral is that private railways
in Australia are a fraud and a robbery, and the
province which goes in for them doesn’t know
enough to take shelter under an ordinary wooden
verandah when it rains.
In the Wake of the Atacama.
After Mr. Sam. Smith’s exposure of the facts
in N.S.W. Assembly on 25th July, will the
Govt, insist on the passage of the Navigation
Bill this session ? Will it abolish the Marine
Board this session? —and take care that no
taint of the old rottenness pollutes the new
authority which is to supersede the Board ?
If the Govt, is too sluggish or too faint-hearted
to do its duty, will the Assembly compel it to
do its duty —this session ? Or will the session
pass, and coffin-ships still be permitted to leave
N.S.W. ports; perished steamers still be per-
mitted to trade in Sydney harbor ; boilers still
be left in charge of uncertificated men and boys
—to the certain peril and probable loss of valu-
able human lives?
* * * *
The Bulletin asks every member of Parlia-
ment who feels the responsibility of his posi-
tion to take an effective personal interest in
this matter, and to actively press for reform!
It asks Mr. Barton, Mr. Wise, Mr. Ashton,
Mr. Ewing, and a score of others who are
capable of thinking of something beyond party
warfare, not to let the matter drift, but to
press it at once to the desired conclusion.
Every gale on the coast, every breeze almost,
makes some of the vessels which trade about
these shores a menace to the community. Some
of them are unsafe to journey upon Sydney
harbor in, even in the calmest weather. At any
moment the marine death-roll may be length-
ened —for want of effective supervision of ships.
And to every member of Parliament who has
power to mend things the responsibility will
come home.
* * * *
What did Mr. Smith say? He said what
The Bulletin has been saying for years ; what
the Evening News and Telegraph proved
again the other day by fact and illustration
he said that the Marine Board was a negligent
and incompetent authority ; that its surveys
give no guarantee that a ship is sound and
seaworthy-—as the loss of the , and of the
, and of the prove ; that its certifi-
cates of soundness and seaworthiness are good
until a little more strain than ordinary comes—
a little more sea, a little more wind—and then
they are death certificates.
• * » *
Mr. Smith produced in Parliament portions
of perished plates, and perished girders,recently
taken from certificated and uncertificated
vessels—plates which had rusted away to a
sixteenth of their proper thickness; plates
from below the water-line so thin, so full of
holes, that they could be broken to pieces with
the lingers ; and he implored the Government
to end the state of things which permitted such
vessels to leave the wharves with crews and
passengers—sometimes hundreds of passengers,
on board. He gave ocular evidence of his
statements—so strong that Mr. See, a ship-
owner himself and in sympathy with ship-
owners, did not attempt to controvert it. He
could not controvert it.
• * * *
It is true that, since The Bulletin’s article
condemning the Marine Board for the loss of
the Atacama and 13 lives; since Premier
Reid poured new blood into the Marine Board ;
and since the repeated efforts of the Republic to
leave Sydney Harbor without a certificate of sea-
worthiness were frustrated, ship-owners have
begun to realise their situation. No doubt some
of the worst vessels have beenmended. Since the
agitation for the remorseless overhaul of all
vessels trading from and about N.S.W. ports
took shape, Mr. Smith asserts that the owners
of all sorts of rotten and neglected tubs have
rushed the docks for repairs. It is thus that
he was able to produce his perished plates, and
to affirm that the Marine Board has licensed
ferry-boats through whose sides, below water,
you could shove your fist —ferry-boats carrying
sometimes big crowds of excursionists.
* * * *
But the community is not yet safe—-not by a
long wav. As soon as ship-owners’ alarm is
over, things will fall into their old groove ; and
the unnecessary loss of human life is as sure as
the first big storm—or the first small accident.
Reform must come at once ; and care must ie
taken that no members of the old Marine Boat
are allowed to hamper it. The new authority
must be independent ; and its officers mus
independent—they must not hold their 1 e
on condition of placating interested ship-
owners.
*** * .
The Bulletin suggests that the public them-
selves should move in a matter in which they
are so vitally interested ; and it suggests
Cardinal Moran and his religious antagonists
that, instead of squabbling over missions
the natives of Fiji and Samoa, they should
devote their energies to the work °f sau &
Australian lives—every day endangered white
conditions remain as at present. I hen j
would really earn the public gratitude.


The Bulletin has mentioned no names in
this statement of the case, although Mr. Smith
mentions them in his speech. But everyone
who journeys on Sydney harbour, or who
permits his family to do so, should digest the
facts and allegations in Hansard of July 25.
The daily press dares not fully report the
speech, thanks to the scandalous state of the
N.S.W. libel law.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Brisbane Telegraph’s “Gentleman.”
Brisbane Telegraph has another anti-Federal
ghost-yarn to tell :
A gentleman who knows something about the Com-
monwealth Bill asks the publicans and brewers of
Queensland if they are aware that under the Bill the
duties on spirits may be increased and the excise duties
doubled, without the local Parliament having a voice in
the matters. They do not know it now, but will hear it
by-and-by.
Another gentleman (who perhaps knows more
about the Commonwealth Bill than the above
gentleman) rises right here in this paper to ask
the publicans and brewers of Queensland if
they are aware that, under the Commonwealth
Bill, the Federal Parliament can reduce their
oppressive spirit duty of 145.-a gallon to 12s.
as in Victoria, and their import duty on beer in
bulk of Is. 3d.-a-gallon to 10d., as in Victoria,
despite the frautie resistance of the oppressive
Brisbane Parliament. Also, that it can reduce
Queensland’s 3d.-a-gallon beer excise to 2d., as
in Victoria ; and the 125.-a-gallon spirit excise
to Bs., as in Victoria; and the ls.-per-lb.
tobacco excise to 9d., and the 25.-per-lb. cigar
excise to 9d.—both as in Victoria. The Bris-
bane Legislature, which has refused per-
sistently to do these commendable actions it-
self, won’t be able to prevent it—providing
Queensland federates. If Queensland doesn’t
federate then Brisbane Parliament can keep
the duties at the present high rate and still
make the life of the Queensland brewer and
publican and tobacco-grower and manufacturer
a misery. But as for the Federal Parliament
doubling the Queensland duties—well, in that
case it would have to raise the duties all
round, for all duties are to be uniform. And
N.S.W. and Victoria show no desire to pay ill
4s. per gallon excise on their whisky—especially
Victoria, which has been accustomed to pay Bs.
Of course, the Federal Parliament could in-
crease rates—just as the present Queensland
Parliament could pass a law for boiling every-
body alive. But it won’t do it. It will level-
down Queensland’s unspeakable excise charges
to something near a civilised level, and give
the Queensland manufacturer the show which
his local Legislature has never had sense enough
to afford. The South, which has been accus-
tomed to something like a sane and decent
tariff—not the awful kind of thing which sits
on the head of local industry in Queensland —
will want to argue the point anyhow.
The Federal Plural-Vote Lie.
A curious lie raised by the Queesnsland paro-
chialists is t o the effect that the Federal Consti-
tution doesn’t really provide for one-man-one-
vote. Clauses 8 and 30 declare that, whatever
the Federal franchise may be, nobody shall vote
more than once at elections for either Federal
House. But clause 41, by way of safeguarding
the woman suffrage, says :
No adult person who has or acquires a right (only one
right, be it observed.—Ed. B.) to vote (not to vote more
than once, but only to vote.—Ed. B.) for the more numer-
ous House of the Parliament of a State shall, while the
right continues, be prevented by any law of the Common-
wealth from voting (not from voting twice or six times,
but merely from voting.—Ed. B.) at elections of either
House of the Parliament of the Commonwealth.
And some parochial liars up north are trying
hard to explain, despite the twice-repeated
enactment that nobody shall vote more than
once at any Federal election, and despite the
fact that clause 41 simply conserves the right
of every State elector to vote (not to have a
dozen votes) at Federal elections, that this is a
proviso by which the plural voter is to be pre-
served in the Commonwealth. It is a poor,
small, one-eyed lie—but the Queensland paro-
chialists haven’t invented a good lie of their
own yet. The only really decent lies in the
Federal campaign, and there were some very
good ones, came from N.S. W.
A Story about MTntyre.
The long-delayed trial of lawyer MTntyre
came to pass at Melb. Criminal Court last week,
with the long-expected result that the prisoner,
having been found guilty of fraud by a unani-
mous jury, was let out on bail to await a pro-
bable quashing of the verdict by the Full
Court. For the Full Court is learned in the Law,
and the Law, which is said to be a h’ass even
when it looks most like a rogue, seems to have
been specially designed to keep dishonest law-
yers and trustees out of prison. The Law loves
trustees because they are very apt to be lawyers
also. Alex. Grant MTntyre, for instance,
was both a lawyer (he is still on the rolls)
and a trustee. As a trustee he defrauded
young man Cogdon, his brother-in-law, by
converting to his own use a valuable property
—to wit, a mortgage over land—which his con-
fiding relative had entrusted to his keeping.
The details of the case can be taken as read.
It will suffice to say that MTntyre’s own
counsel protested against the trial of his client
for criminal fraud in these words: “ There
could be no doubt but that MTntyre was
amenable under the law relating to trustees.
As far as criminal law was concerned, it was
patent that . . . the document which Cogdon
had signed had created MTntyre a trustee,
and as such he could not be prosecuted
under the criminal law, even if he had
converted the property entrusted to him to
his own use.” Chief-Justice Madden solemnly
remarked that “this was the real difficulty in
the case.” MTntyre’s defence was, firstly,
that he acted for Cogdon as a brother-in-law
rather than as a solicitor, and that if he did act
as a solicitor he was not amenable to gaol, in-
asmuch as Cogdon had created him trustee of
the property which he (MTntyre) embezzled,
stole, stuck-to, converted to his own use, or
what you will. The guilt of MTntyre was
Tearly proved. The fact of his having stolen
much more money than the average thief who
gets (say) three years penal servitude for a first
offence, was a fact that “ stuck out a mile ” all
the time, but the Law seemed so favorable to
MTntyre that the Chief-Justice spoke of him
as a man who ought not to be tried by a
jury at all. His Honor said, however, that
“he would take advantage of the presence of
the jury to have certain special questions
answered by them.” The special questions
were put, and the answers were to the effect
that M'lntyre had converted Cogdon’s pro-
perty to his own use in the dual capacity of
lawyer and trustee, and not in the capacit}' of
a brother-indaw. Whereupon Justice Madden
said, “ the logical sequence of the finding, in
his opinion, was a verdict of not guilty ,” but lie
gave the jury the option of expressing its
real opinion of M ‘lntyre in one or two words.
The jury foreman expressed it very neatly—
“ln the matter of law we are in the hands of
his Honor. On the matter of the deed , we
unanimously find the accused guilty.” After
which the privileged doer of the guilty deed
was released on bail as before, and the whole
question was reserved for the Full Court, and
if the Full Court upholds the conviction of this
lawyer-trustee The Bulletin will be pleasantly
astounded.

Interpreting the Constitution.
Some learned and fathomless pessimists in the
anti-Federal interest are moaning considerably
on the bar about the alleged excessive powers
entrusted by the Commonwealth Bill to the
Federal judiciary. As one Jenkinson, M.P.,
complains in Gympie Truth (Queensland) :
The Federal High Court is to be the sole interpreter
of the Constitution of the Commonwealth and also that
of the several States.
Jenkinson and other critics complain that this
is very, very dreadful, and that the same sys-
tem has made the U.S. Supreme Court an awful
tyranny. Yet if they have any better plan to
propose they keep it so very d irk that not a
hint of it has yet reached the public. At pre-
sent, the Supreme Court is the sole interpreter
of the laws in all the Australias, and in every
civilised country some similar tribunal does the
same thing. In a few purely barbarous coun-
tries a despot interprets the laws himself with a
club or with his teeth, but these are ex-
ceptional cases. The business of the civil
courts is to interpret the laws, and every law
in every civilised country is subject to the
interpretation of the courts. If they decide (as
the N.S. W. courts did re the original land tax
law) that the law doesn’t say what Parliament
meant it to say, and that it is unworkable and
contradictory, then the law is a dead letter till
Parliament repairs it. The Supreme Court
annuls it, not in so many words but to all in-
tents and purposes, on the ground that it is an
unworkable measure. When the Federal
Constitution becomes one of the laws of Aus-
tralia the courts will presumably interpret it
when necessary, as they do any other law.
Either they must do it, or it must be done by
the policeman, or the bishop, or the pea-nut
man, or the J.P., or the C.P.S., or somebody,
and perhaps the High Court will do it as well
as anybody. The Court can’t enforce the law
without interpreting it, and if it isn’t allowed
to enforce this particular law—well, what is
the good of having any such law at all ? Yet,
as a matter of fact, the Jenkinsons and other
learned pessimists don’t know what they
are talking about, for, curiously enough, the
Federal Constitution doesn’t give the High Court
power to interpret the Constitution at all. It
merely says (clause 76): “ The Parliament may
make laws conferring original jurisdiction on
the High Court in any matter arising under
this Constitution or involving its interpreta-
tion, or arising under any laws made by the
Parliament.” It may do so, but it isn’t bound
to do so, and the power of conferring on the
High Court authority to interpret the Consti-
tution also implies power to take away that
authority if the High Court misuses it. So
there is no analogy between the Australian and
the U.S. position in this matter; and the al-
leged tyrannous control of the U.S. Supreme
Court over the Legislature has nothing at all
to do with the case.

A Voice from the Sunset.
“ T’othersider ” writes to The Bulletin :
The Federal camp in West Australia is brooding mourn-
fully on the situation, wondering if Premier Forrest will
be clever enough to evade the direct issue. The first
move in his game is the relegation of his Bill to a Select
Committee of both Houses, instructed to bring up a re-
port by sth Sept. This is intended to give courage to the
Leg. Council. Members of Assembly are afraid of their
seats, and they will on present appearance pass the Bill
to the people ; but the Council is in the road. In a land
reeking with ignorant pride and fatuous prejudice, the
Council stands supreme for both. It is the holy of
holies of Groperdom. Probably no Upper House in
the world is steeped in such a dense fog of
ten - for -a - penny parochialism. Compared with it
the Upper House of N.S.W. is a hotbed of anarchy, and
the Forty-eight Rich Landlords of Victoria are revolu-
tionaries and dynamitards. So the Federal camp awaits
in gloom, spoiling for a fight, but afraid that the enemy
may not come within striking distance. The Government
actuary has concocted a mess of statistics that would turn
Coohlan green and purple with envy. For clear and
pellucid taradiddles the figures overlie anything that
statistics have ever attempted before. The tariff impost
on food and produce is the basis of the whole concoction.
Each year, as local production increases, the revenue
therefrom is rapidly decreasing. But the W.A.
Coohlan assumes that in the first year of uni-
form duties (two years after the establishment of
the Commonwealth—say, three years from now) the W.A.
tariff on food and produce will bring in the same as last
year. The present rate of decrease is L'60,000 a year, next
year the decrease will probably be £BO,OOO and so on. In
three years the revenue from this source will be very
small indeed, Federation or no Federation. This does
not matter to the Groper Coohlan. He stands firmly on
the quicksand of the 1898 figures, oblivious of the gaping
deficit that already appears in the 1899 figures.
The people of Westralia do not care a rap for
the figures of screech from the anti-federal statis-
tician. They want Federation. On the goldfields the
unanimity of this aspiration is almost monotonous.
Editor Kirwan (Kalgoorlie Miner) says it is practically im-
possible to find a man who is against the Commonwealth
Bill. In Perth, the big meetings have been so one-sided,
that there is no disposition to continue them. The
Federalists compose 99 per cent, of the audiences. The
National League (of parochialists) has driven one well-
intentioned journalist insane, and he has been shipped to
England, after chasing his doctor round the yard with a
pitchfork. This marvellous League has now commis-
sioned the paschal lamb called C. E. Jones, and as paid
lecturer, he addresses small but unanimous bodies of
Gropers, who have not heard the story of the mint sauce.
It is a sick cause that needs C. E. Jones for its stipen-
diary lecturer.
Westralia is particularly favored by the Commonwealth
, Bill, as it gets more than its arithmetical representation
in both Houses. If it joins the Commonwealth as an
1 original State, it secures this minimum of representation
for all time. It may, if it chooses, for five years after the
imposition of the uniform tariff, impose duties on goods
coming from the other States. This will bring extra
revenue to its Treasury, and give extra protection to its
producers. This is not forced upon Westralia, but is
optional. During this period, and for ever afterwards,
the markets of the Eastern States will be open to it, if it
should have produce of any kind to send.
• Constitutionally, Westralia will acquire a democratic
Government, payment of members in both Houses, both
Houses based on manhood suffrage, abolition of plural
voting, abolition of Fatman as boss in federal politics ; it
will practically throw off theyoke of thepolitical bounders
who now run the show ; it will become a factor in a
nation, instead of a joint in John Forrest’s political tail.
In the Commonwealth, Westralia’s fortune would be
established on the basis of good government, instead of
tottering precariously on the shifting sands of Perth
intrigue.

Bureaucratic Blundering.
“ Frank” forwards what looks like a very real
grievance :
(1) Tamworth, a N.S.W. town of some 5000 inhabitants,
badly needed a water supply. Tamworthians obtained
water from a town pump, situated in the centre of
the town ; the well was on a river flat, liable to
pollution from percolation of sewage and other abomina-
tions through the porous soil. Consequently, occasional
outbreaks of typhoid, influenza, and other diseases
ascribed to insanitary conditions, kept the doctors and
undertakers busily occupied. (N.B.— Though the Govt,
water supply scheme has been carried out at enormous
expense the same old town pump is still in requisition !)
(2) At the outset Tamworth City Fathers engaged Mr.
Gipps, C.E., to formulate a scheme. That gentleman
proposed a source of supply from a weir on Cockhurn
River. Total catchment area, about 300 sq. miles. He
submitted plans and specifications of the whole work,
including reticulation of the town estimated cost,
£17,500.
(3) Next, another citizen pointed out some defects in
Mr. Gipps’ scheme, notably the liability to pollution,
through induction of supply from weir to service-reser-
voir through open conduits. He suggested, as an unfail-
ing source of supply, Moonbi Springs, situated at the foot
of Moonbi Range, at an elevation slightly above the site
of the proposed weir. Mr. Gipps cordially endorsed the
suggestion, having noted the Springs while carrying out
his survey. The cost to the council of Mr. Gipps’ survey
and computations amounted to £250.
(4) Then the Government chipped in. The Works De-
partment having suggested a pumping scheme in the
interim (an infernally nasty scheme at that, which was
denounced and discarded) rushed to the fray, infuriate,
as usual, at the resistance offered to its initial design,
and persistently resolved to continue its destructive
career to the bitter end. Officials were sent up to design
a scheme having for its source of supply a flow anywhere
but in the Moonbi locality. But, as Nature has located
there the only available source from which a water-supply
for Tamworth can be economically derived, the task was
simply impossible. Nevertheless, the officials did the
best they could to defeat Nature’s beneficence.
(5) The Parliamentary Committee on Public Works
visited the afflicted borough and took evidence on a
scheme of supply from Moore Creek —catchment area
about 20 sq. miles. Officials swore that this area would
be sufficient, with average rainfall, to supply water-
power to light the town with electricity, to furnish
domestic needs—in fact, to fill the bill as to municipal
requirements. Evidence to the effect that the stream to
feed the proposed reservoir was a miserable trickle at
best, and that the locality had been often dry as a bone,
was contemptuously rejected, because of the ipse dixit
of the unimpeachable officials of the Works— vide Par-
liamentary reports.
(6) The Government scheme was carried out, in spite of
remonstrance by intelligent citizens. The local press not
only lied its best—or worst —to aid official iniquity, but
also suppressed to the utmost of its power all evidence
adverse to the Government design. The result has been
most disastrous waste of public money, intolerable
financial burdening of the ratepayers, and infliction of
insanitary misery. The costly reservoir during the
drought held only a stinking mud-pool which had to be
cleaned out. Five inches of rain fell, and even this
copious downpour only raised the water to lift, in the
reservoir. Where is the 40-h.p. Pelton-wheel which the
officials spoke of in a report that equalled in mendacity
any wild-cat prospectus ever slung at an unsuspecting
public? There isn’t 40-cat-power, nor any power, nor
any flow, to bear out the promises to the borough made
by allegedly-responsible officials, and, through them, by
the Works Department, as inducement to accept respon-
sibility for the cost of a work that is practically inutile.
Worse, it is a "Jwhite elephant of the 'most expensive
description ; for when water is let into the pipes from the
supply reservoir the pressure is higher than the capacity
of the weakly-constructed pipes, which burst and leak
consumedly, and have to be repaired, keeping men con-
stantly employed. Moreover, the water is unfit for
human consumption as a beverage. At one time it was
too malodorous even to bathe in.
Lastly—As there is hardly a scintilla of truth or
accuracy in the reports of the officials, or in the evidence
tendered to the P.C. on Public Works in favor of the
job, what is to result from this blistering expose of the
culminating instance of a long series of bureaucratic
blunders, departmental malfeasance, and the pernicious
existence of an odious Parliamentary excrescence like
the Works Committee, which evidently hasn’t discern-
ment enough to perceive a hole in a ladder until it col-
lides violently with the sides and rungs thereof ?
