The Importance of Averting 1 Simpson.
The Bill which was left unfinished during last
session of N.S.W. Parliament, authorising one
Simpson to build a bridge connecting Sydney
and North Sydney, and the other Bill autho-
rising one Sulman to do something similar, are
both to crop up again this session. These two
persons appear to have got so much in the
habit of cropping up that they, or some others
like them, will probably keep on cropping
till they either get their bridge concession,
or the State, or the municipality, puts a sudden
and violent end to their hopes by building the
bridge itself. Therefore, it is desirable to hit
the public again with a list of the principal
reasons why the State or the municipality
should build the bridge in question, and why
it should reject the offers of the whole Simpson
clan with loathing, and crush Sulman remorse-
lessly :
(1) Bridge-building, according to all Australian prece-
dent, is a matter of State or municipal—not private—
enterprise. All over Australia the State is the bridge-
builder, and it would be bursting up a fine substantial
precedent to let private enterprise begin fooling at this
late hour with things it has practically no experience of.
(2) The State can build the bridge more cheaply than
Simpson can. It hasn't to float a company, or to give
preference-shares to any promoter; or to pay directors’
fees, or manager’s salary, or office rent—its own staff in
the Public Works Department can do all that is neces-
sary. And it doesn’t start as the private enterprise
generally does, by taking the “concession” to London
and selling it to a company for £250,000, wherefore a
bridge that ought to cost £1,000,000 really costs, under the
private enterprise system, at least £1,250,000.
(3) The State, if it must needs borrow, can raise the
necessary £1,000,000 or so at 3 per cent. Simpson’s credit
isn’t as good as that of the State. His eo. will probably
borrow most of the money for construction purposes at 5
per cent., and it will want at least 5 per cent, dividends
on its own money.
(4) Therefore if Simpson, or any other Simpson, builds
the North Sydney bridge, he will have to charge the
public who use that bridge enough to pav 5 per cent, on
(probably) £1,250,000 (fc’62,500 a year), or £1,500,000
(075,000 a-year), where the State would only need to
charge them enough to pay 3 per cent, on £1,000,000
(£30,000 a year). In other words, Simpson would need
to charge twice as heavy a toll as the State would.
(5) There is no evidence that Simpson will supply the
bridge any sooner than the State would do. Private
enterprise is pitiably slow in many cases. Maoriland
handed over the Midland railway concession to a co.
because it couldn’t see its way to build the line for a few
years to come, and the co. fooled round for nine years
and hardly got started. The concession which Tasmania
granted to its Great Western railway syndicate has
already expired and the co. isn’t even formed yet. Tha
whole time has been wasted hawking the concession
round in London.
(6) If Simpson builds the bridge the excessive tolls
which private enterprise will have to charge will presently
raise a crj’ for State resumption. The probable end will
be that the State will pay IJ4 millions or more for a
structure which it could have built for half the money.
(7) If the movement for amalgamating the 41 munici-
palities of Sydney comes off, then the new city of Greater
Sydney will be strong and rich enough to build its own
bridges and make a decent profit out of them—if the
State doesn’t feel like doing the work itself. The new
municipality—if there is any reasonable prospect of such
a thing—should at least get a chance to do the work
before any Simpson is granted a monopoly of this magni-
tude.
The North Sydney bridge proposal should cer-
tainly be laid before Parliament and the Public
Works Committee again this session as a public
undertaking in the hope that it may have
better luck, than it has met with up to date.
And the Committee should be invited to con-
sider, not only the possible profit or want of
profit which the community will make by
building the bridge itself, but the certain awful
loss to the community if Simpson builds it, and
the obvious fact that if the community doesn’t
do it, some Simpson or other is almost certain
to lay his hands on the concession in the long
run. In other words, this public enterprise
needs to be considered in two aspects —as a
possible means of direct profit, and as a certain
means of saving great direct loss arising through
Simpson. The urgent necessity for averting
Simpson hasn’t been properly considered.
Simpson (w r hose bridge hasn’t to run the
gauntlet of the Public Works Committee) will
almost certainly get there some day unless the
State puts its own bridge right on top of Simp-
son and thus ends the danger.
Gaily proceeds the rumpus between Cardinal
Moran and the resenters of his aspersions upon
Protestant missionaries ; and, without offering
an opinion about the merits of the dispute, The
Bulletin has to say that, as a polemic, Car-
dinal Moran has more about him of the clumsy
hedge-priest than of the “Prince of the Church.”
There was also a gross strategical blunder, for
one thing, in the silly and easily-disprovable as-
sertion that the Protestant missionaries wanted
the British war-ships to fire upon the women and
children at the Samoan Catholic mission-house.
When the Cardinal assails another faith, it is
strictly that faith’s “ own funeral,” but when
he insults the common sense of the community
upon a matter of ordinary humanity and manli-
ness, he travels into a different critical category,
seriously damages his cause, and makes himself
personally ridiculous. For the rest, if the Pro-
testant champions have been as blatant and
blatherskity as usual, the Cardinal has also ad-
hered to his crude controversial traditions of
making foolishly acrimonious assertions and
then clumsily trying to evade the issue. Patrick
F. considers, doubtless, that he owes it to the
designation “ Cardinal ” to be keen of thrust
and deft of recovery, but the shillelah is obvi-
ously more native to his hand than the rapier.
Dr. Stawell, lecturing to Melbourne medical
students, condemned the maintenance of found-
ling hospitals, on the ground that the mortality
among infants artificially fed is enormous. The
doctor’s philosophy and morality set out from
the wrong end of the subject he handles.
Foundling hospitals are necessary, and must be
maintained, because, as human society exists,
foundlings will continue to be born. Some may
be killed by artificial diet, but many more will be
killed if there be no hospital to house them, and
they consequently, as likely as not, get no diet
at all. The demand of a new child to be main-
tained falls upon the mother only as an incident
which she may or may not be able to discharge,
but falls upon the community as a duty which
it must discharge with or without the will of
the mother. If foundling-hospital dietary be
not scientifically perfect, the community must
improve it—make it perfect, if possible ; but
the community, because it distrusts its con-
densed milk, must not primarily, by giving no
milk at all, excite mothers to murder the I '' off-
spring.
While all the world, and notably the Rhodes
crowd, has been waxing eloquent and militant
over the Transvaallers’ troubles, the existence
of similar troubles in Rhodesia has been kept
quietly in the background. The Transvaal
Lfitlanders’ chief complaints are with reference
to the questions of franchise disabilities and
monopolistic taxation. Yet they are in far
better case than the people of the country
which sent Jameson to their alleged rescue, and
Rhodes, who grieves so bitterly over Oom
Paul’s alleged tyranny, is a worse tyrant than
Paul. The charter of the British South Africa
Co. nominally provides for universal suffrage in
the election of members of the council which
holds the controlling power in Chartered terri-
tory, but then the suffrage can be used in the
election of four only of the 11 members form-
ing the Council ; the remaining seven are chosen
in the interests of the capital represented by
the co., and are avowedly (as per report) running
the country in the interests of the co.’s dollars.
Then as to taxation : in the Transvaal the
mining industry is taxed to the extent of 14
per cent, of the divs. earned, whereas in
Rhodesia all dividends are taxed in the mini-
mum 33 1-3, and in maximum 50 per cent. The
co. has also mortgages in the shape of con-
cessions on water-supply rights, market dues,
land-leasing, &c., &c., and altogether it seems
to out-Paul ’Oom Paul himself in disfranchising
and taxing all things and all men excepting
those of its own household.
“ G.S.” : Re W.A. Early Closing Act :
A Perth magistrate says it appears to him that, the pro-
vision prev anting employes from remaining on business
premises . ithe day they are due for a holiday will, if
enforced, interfere with the liberty of one lot of sub-
jects, and, if not enforced, with that of another lot. He
suggests as a remedy a compulsory day’s work of 8 hours,
no more, no less, and sa.vs that if there were no such
thing as overtime then there would be fewer men out of
work. Sound sense in this, though I have heard rabid
laborites oppose the latter proposition because it would
interfere with the liberty of the subject. A workman
ought, they said, to be allowed to work as long hours as
he liked, and earn as much as he could. The fellow
workman without employment did not enter into their
calculations. There is another sensible Perth P.M. who
would £ive every free adult a vote and compel him to
i record it under penalty of loss of civil rights for three
. years, maximum.

The “King’s Musketeer” (Henry Hamil-
ton’s version) opened with a blaze of glory at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday, and a vast crowd
assembled to Compare it with Bernard Espinasse’s
version, recently presented by Rignold at the
Criterion. In a purely literary sense, Espinasse’s
edition beat Hamilton’s in several places. The
Australian man’s drama has a tendency to do
things; in the English version people have a
lamentable habit of coining in and stating that
tilings have been done. Tins was so noticeable in
some places that the present scoffer began to think
of writing another “Musketeer” all on his own,
with D’Artagnan and Miladi left out, also Athos,
Porthos, Aramis and Richelieu, but with a suc-
cession of people bursting in to tell the king how
these persons are getting on. Hamilton’s version
also centres round the ill-fated diamonds which
Anne of Austria gave to her gorgeous lover,
Buckingham ; and the frantic journey of the four
musketeers to London to recover them, and thus
establish an alibi. But the whole episode of the
jewels is crowded into one act; the curtain goes
down on the musketeers departing, and it rises
three minutes after to find D’Artagnan bursting
in ten days later with the recovered property, and
telling about the innumerable bloodshed which
had happened on the road. This haste spoils the
episode. There isn’t so much bare shoulder in
Hamilton’s version as in the previous one, where
Miladi’s dress was torn off so liberally to exhibit
the place where her beautiful flesh had been done
brown by the executioner ; but, on the other hand,
the present drama has the more definite ending.
Miladi ends her career with some kind of poison
(possibly carbolic acid) which makes her writhe
horribly, and shriek dismally, and pound herself
to death on the floor. Hamilton’s drama is at
least ten shrieks ahead of its rival and predecessor.
* * * »
The “King’s Musketeer” is gorgeously
mounted, richly painted, richly dressed, and
played by a strong company. Miss Henrietta
Watson —who has developed a striking like-
ness to sister Elizabeth since Australia saw her
last —is a beautiful siren, and her adventuress
is a beautiful, convincing, and expensively-
dressed personage. Miss Eniilie Hughes is an
ideal Queen. The tremendous Augustus Glover
is Porthos—he is the only man on the Australian
stage imposing enough for that mountainous
musketeer, and he is the most absolutely soul-
satisfying person among the male characters.
George Majeroni’s Aramis and Athohvood’s
Mosqueton are close up. Cecil Ward, fine actor
as he is, looks too plump and gentle and affable
for the gaunt D’Artagnan; and Harry Plimmer
is of too copious a build for Athos. ’ Bentley’s
Richelieu is a strong, effective character, with an
occasional tendency to drop into Scotch, and to
allude to the animal which toils in front of a cab
as a “harse.” The author makes Louis XIII. a
cheerful cackling idiot (Espinasse’s version went
one worse, and represented him as a grumpy kind
of burglar or dog-stealer), and though Harry Hill
makes him cackle amusingly, this sort of thing is
rough on a rather respectable king. Still, the
people herein grumbled at would look admirable
in worse company. The Queen and Miladi are so
good, and Porthos is so stupendous, that they
overshadow the surroundings ; besides it is diffi-
cult to live up to the clothes and the
scenic effects. The “King’s Musketeer” is
a good show in a general way, and Bent-
ley*s allusions to a “harse” are not mentioned
in any unfriendly spirit. If it makes the animal
any less jtmipy when one is on him, or any less
unpleasant when one drops off him, then this
writer is willing to call him anything.
* * m *
Tn the short closing season of the “ Belle
of New York ” Albert Whela.n managed to leave
on the sands of dramatic art the biggest footprint
he has made on that shifting substance up to date.
He took the part of Ichabod Bronson, vice poor
Oscar Girard, who was partly dying and partly
dead. For so short a time it probably wasn’t
worth while to think out a new Ichabod, so
Whelan simply borrowed Girard’s character
whole. It was the same to a ham and an
accent and a leer —such an amazing effort
of mimicry that penurious Scotch persons
lost their money betting that Girard’s alleged
illness was only some complicated advertising
dodge, and that he had assumed the new name of
Whelan for purposes unknown. And now em-
bittered Scotch persons are looking for William-
son to point out that the saxpence they lost should
be handed over to them and deducted from
Whelan’s salary.
* w * *
The five nights revival of “Mrs. Tan-
queray ” at Sydney Royal should have been a
great occasion, for the co. put its entire soul into
Pinero’s great tragedy, and the Brough co. has a
lot of soul when the occasion calls for it. The fact
that it was only a moderate occasion is another
evidence of the cussedness of inanimate objects—
the inanimate object in this case being the Sydney
public. The only thing that was notably wrong
with the latest version of “Mrs. Tanqueray” was
Brough himself, as Cayley Drummle. He did
excellent work—but there are at least a dozen
different Broughs, and this was one of the wrong
Broughs. Theone who wouldhavefilled the part to
perfection was the quiet, lazy, cynical man with
the dark moustache, and tlie soft, lazy hat, and
the horizontal manner, who appeared in the
“Summer’s Day.” And instead of this, when
Brough went to the place where he keeps himself
hung up in a row ready for immediate use, he
selected himself wrongly; he took a rather
sketchy, clean shaved, hurried kind of Brough
with light fhair r~ 1 light eyebrows, who didn’t
leave a sharp, clear-out impression on the audience.
Possibly he was late, and snatched himself off the
nail in a hurry, and thus got the wrong person
unintentionally. Cayley Drummle was a good
character, but the other Brough would have
looked him ever so much better.
* * * *
For the rest Mrs. Brough put all her fine
abilities into Paula, and did that tragic woman
admirably, and Joseph Carne, who had the large
contract of being Titheradge’s successor in the
part of Aubrey Tanqueray, did it so well that
Titheradge wasn’t much missed. It is a part
where the quiet, strong man tills the bill, and that
sort of man is Joseph Carne’s best point. Miss
Noble filled her old place as the frozen saint; and
Miss Temple filled hers as the lady of easy Flossie-
dom upon whom George Orreyed had wasted his
bright young nose, and his two waving legs, and
for whom he had dropped all his prospects in the
decanter. There was a new George Orreyed—
Leslie Victor doing the easy character of the
young man who had rashly bestowed his weak
head, and his hiccup, and his tendency to break
things, upon an unworthy object. Also Lovell
had the thankless part of Hugh Ardale, which
didn’t give him the show he deserved. Taken all
round the latest “Tanqueray” was a deserving
performance, and by the time the short season is
over, and the “Brixton Burglary” follows on
Saturday, Sydney will, probably, have just begun
to wonder why it didn’t go oftener.
* * * *
“ The Birthright,” which arose at Sydney
Opera House this week, is a tale of the Emerald
Isle, in which the rise of the curtain discovers a
dining-room with an interrupted dinner-party.
The red-faced attorney, Mr. Quinlan, drunk with
sherry and mad with rage, is shaking his fist, on
general principles, at the retreating figure of Mr.
Walsh, of Walsh’s Hall (Mr. Douglas). Mr. Cut-
liff, senr., soon recalls him to more serious busi-
ness, and tells Mr. Quinlan how in the days of his
burning youth Lord Fitzgerald (his brother) went
through a mock marriage with Dora Dillon, the
usual pretty peasant girl. Mr. Walsh acted as
priest, and poor Dora believed herself really and
truly married. After her son was born, she was
told the truth, and she disappeared. A mutilated
female corpse cast up by the deceiving sea was be-
lieved to be the end of her. Mr, Cutliff is anxious
that Mr. Quinlan, as Lord Fitz’s legal gentleman,
should u»e his influence to get young Bob Cutliff
all the cash and property, as well as the title, and
that the love-boy, Justin, should be fired out to
battle with a cold, cold world. Mr. Quinlan
cheerfully promises to see to it. Out on the breezy
upland above the castle Justin is having a delight-
ful time with Lady Elsie (Miss Billie Howarde),
Lord Fitz’s neice, a dainty little lady of seventeen
who floats about in a white gown and white straw
hat tied with yellow strings in a bow just beside
the little.dimple near her mouth. Justin envies
that bow. Young Bob Cutliff, who wants Lady
Elsie as well as all the cash, all the property and
all the title, finds the two talking of “glorious
things ” in the drawing-room and proceeds to tell
Justm that he is a nobody and that his mother was
worse. He gets nearly strangled for his trouble.
* • * *
Acts 2 and 3 find Justin taking to drink
when Lady Elsie floats in and puts her arms
round him and tells him he can kiss her—if he
likes. And the fool only kisses her once. From
that time out lie lost all public sympathy.
Elsie has to remind him that before the row he
kissed her shoes, and then he does pull him-
self together and declare himself ready to
do so again, and Elsie tells him the mouth is
much more convenient—and he kisses it again,
once. Justin meets his supposed dead mother
talking to somebody in a graveyard, and she blesses
him without revealing her identity. She is living
in a lonely mill, and Mr. Quinlan and the Cutliffs
set fire to it, and it flames up on the stage, and
Dora screams and heaves herself into the river,
and Justin heaves himself in after her. Then Mr.
Walsh, stricken with remorse, decides to tell all
he knows. It is to the effect that when he per-
formed that alleged mock marriage he was a priest
in holy orders, and that the marriage was legal.
Truly, he had retired from active church service
in order to devote more time to the whisky bottle,
but that made no difference. It’s cheap London
Journal sort of literature, but Billie Howarde,
as Lady Elsie, Mr. Walsh, Old Lindsay and Lord
Fitzgerald have a lot of human nature, and play
their parts naturally.
* * * *
The Sydney Lyceum drama here de-
nounced with such frank and unobtrusive objur-
gation last week, has undergone no change
that this paper is aware of. The huge dredge
dredges away patiently, and it is respectfully
recommended as the only dredge on the stage
within the writer’s knowledge. To everything
else in the drama he is as disrespectful as he
knows how to be, and he would be more disrespect-
ful if he was able to.
* * * *
Harry Rickards is making a few farewell
appearances at Sydney Tivoli,“prior to a visit to
Europe and America ; but the great Cinquevalli is
so big an item at the Tiv., that even Rickards’s
departure attracts little notice. Cinquevalli has
lately taken to heaving clothes in the air with his
feet, and they fit themselves on to them when they
come down in a marvellous manner. He is cap-
able of putting on all his clothes this way, likewise
his boots and jewellery ; but there are difficulties
about doing this before a mixed audience. Also
he knows a peculiar trick whereby he can blow
his tailor’s bill backwards off his ear, and it
rebounds from the wall and comes back paid.

Next Tuesday (August 8) all the theatrical
companies of Sydney are combining to give a
great afternoon performance at Her Majesty’s in
aid of the Victoria Consumptive Home. It is a
good cause and deserves support. The pity is
that the old useful consumptive home at Thirl-
mere, so long supported by Mr. Goodlet out of his
own funds—the home that wasn’t fashionable nor
“Victoria,” but just good solid charity—didn’t
have the same sort of thing happen to it -ftener.
* * * *
Miss Helen Hart, the well-known lady
lecturer and sturdy supporter of many good but
unorthodox causes, intends giving a series of lec-
tures in and around Sydney. The first one, on
temperance, will be delivered at Manly on Mon-
day, Bth August.
On Monday night the “Kelly Gang”
clanked into Sydney Criterion. The show that
swam through blood at the Opera House has been
enlarged a good deal. Kennedy is still shot by
the gory Dan ; Fitzpatrick still washes out his in-
sult to Kate Kelly with his own blood ; and Aaron
Sherritt still dies howling. An extra act is inter-
polated, and shows the gay time the Kellys have
when they stick up Glenrowan Hotel. There is a
stranded theatrical co. there, and when the Kellys
get tired of grog they bring in the stranded
theatricals at the point of the rifle, and command
them to be funny or die on the spot; whereupon
the mummers become wildly hilai’ious to save
their lives. After that comes the train-wrecking
attempt, and the place is full of wild horsemen,
and black trackers, and smell of gun and whisky.
*
“The War of Wealth,” at Melb. Royal,
strikes one as being the largest and most promising
melodrama that Bland Holt has set up since “The
White Heather.” It gets away from London and
Paris, for a healthy change, and the plot has
nothing to do with racecourses, baronets, restaur-
ants, adventuresses, genteel poverty, or the “lower
orders.” None of the characters wear spats, eve-
glasses, or coster suits cut saucy over the trotters.
The girl who went wrong before the curtain rose
seems to have been mislaid or purposely omitted ;
and if the drama contains any references to
Walter Baker’s dear old home and loving Dad,
The Bulletin didn’t hear them the other night.
Probably Dad is dead and the dear old home cut
up into building allotments. Baker, in this crazy
romance, is a middle-aged chairman of directors
of the Antipodean Bank (Lim.), Sydney, with a
bachelor residence at North Shore, where every-
body roams at large. Even the night-watchman
from the bank has the entree to the boss director’s
private house, Australia being a democratic
country. Moreover, the whole board of directors
seem deeply attached to the night-watchman’s
daughter, and one of ’em—the villain—is engaged
to marry her next half-year. Meanwhile, this
chosen director desires the watchman to burgle
the bank’s available assets from the strong-room,
to which scheme the honest old hireling reluctantly
consents, for the sake of his child. He wants to
see her happily married to the wicked director on
the proceeds of the burgle aforesaid. But while
the conspirators are plotting aloud, the dear girl
(Miss Ross) lurks behind a tree, and presently she
upbraids her best director, who causes a snaky
corner of North Shore to crumble beneath her
feet. Luckily, she gets caught in a bush half-way
down the cliff, and managing director Baker saves
Miss Ross’s life, although her memory and her
huge hat have got lost in transit. The burglary
happens a month later. The villain (Styan) gets
all the cash and securities, and the acting-manager
(Albert Norman) is left in the iron safe to perish
until Baker blows it open with a bottle of nitro-
glycerine.
* • * *
When Sydney learns that all the reserve
fund and things were abstracted ere the manager
was put into the safe, there is naturally a furious
run on the Antipodean Bank. The hopes of a
furious run on Bland Holt’s drama are built on
this financial panic. It is a fine stirring crisis.
Director Baker (God bless him !) has a friend
(Bland H.), who found a gold mine for him in
W.A., and the first fruits of this mine (.£100,000)
are due at Redfern Station what time the widow
and the orphan, the selfish grocer, the hairy
agriculturist, and creditors in general, are howling
for their filthy lucre at the bank in George-street.
Baker climbs upon the counter and tells them to
have confidence, and Mrs. Holt, who is a faithful
adherent of the burgled institution, dumps a small
bag of sovereigns into her account, but they
simply devour Mrs. Holt’s little bag of loaves
and fishes, and the panic rages worse than
ever. The villain director proclaims the utter
ruin of the bank, and orders the police to shut the
doors, whereupon Bland Holt and party arrive
from Redfern Station and throw boxes of money
through the plate-glass window. The scene went
vigorously on Saturday, and it can be wrought up
to a high pitch of excitement by careful rehearsal.
There’s a last act to the drama in which Baker
and his banking friends wear military garments
because they are Sydney Lancers, or Parramatta
Rifles. If the scene had been laid in Melbourne
Bland Holt would have sentenced them to appear
barelegged as the local Scottish Volunteers.
Brunton’s views of Sydney Harbor, likewise the
solid interiors and the North Shore land-slip, are
first-class items, and the co. put Australian color
into the acting, anyhow. Baker almost rises to
the occasion, Styan is satisfactory, except for his
awful pot-hat, and Bland Holt makes a laudable
endeavor to look like a bulloeky adventurer from
the Never-Never Wayback. Ten other male
characters come in occasionally, and six ladies do
nothing of much importance. Miss Ross. Mrs.
Bland Holt, and Miss Gill have a good many
lines to speak, and they all look nice as usual ;
also Miss Harrie Ireland is a fine spectacular
Bush Girl, and inasmuch as she swoops down
upon the drama in a pair of boxing-gloves and ties
her glorious hair with a bit of string, Miss I.’s ir-
relevant character is thankfully received. The
four ladies in question are all going to get mar-
ried when the curtain 1 drops. Miss Ross recovers
her memory sufficiently ,to know that she hates
the villain, and the heart of the night-watchman’s
daughter is then freely given to the chairman of
the Hoard.

ifc
‘•A Trip to Chinatown,” which broke out
again at Melb. Princess’s on Saturday, has been
already forgiven by The Bulletin. Viewed in
the light of bygone civilisation, this strange
American entertainment was a thing to weep over,
and many of the ancients of 1890 are said to have
been “ off ” theatres for a whole year after witnes-
sing it ; nevertheless, in the dull, despairing light
of to-day, “ Chinatown ” looks quite respectable.
Melbourne has seen “The American Girl ” and
“The Belle of New York,” not to mention a
lamentable pantomime or two, since Harry Conor
first peeled off his vests and took his temperature
on the Princess’s stage. Circumstances have
paved the way for a more or less successful revival
of “Chinatown.” The success will be probably
rather less than more at the end of three weeks,
but as long as the public continue to vote enthusi-
astically for the present bill, the Higgins who
makes these remarks will keep his Humble Nega-
tive to lumself.
* * * *
The 1899 “ Chinatown” co. consists largely
of the same Conor who was popular in less un-
critical days, and deserves to be rather more
popular now. Welland Strong’s humor shows
sundry subtle improvements, whilst his bald-head
shines out amidst the riot, as of old. Brownlow
the beauteous is a comparatively festive substitute
for one of the heavy young men ; and Miss Carrie
Moore, with her sympathetic voice and general
neatness and despatch, makes a valuable Flirt.
Also Carrie’s slender youthfulness helps to bring
the average age and waist-measurement of the
present female cast nearer to the original figures,
as it were, although the fact remains that
neither of these other ladies possesses a gold front-
tooth. The soprano in the original co. was famous
for a gold tooth. For the rest, the comedian who
dozes on a hot stone, is not exceptionally droll,
and little Willie Grow has little else than little-
ness to fit her for the billet; but the new whistling
waiter will be generally preferred to Alf. Lawton.
Lawton’s was shrill and bony. Whistler Brown
is plump and musical, and he creates a surprising
effect of two Browns whistling a duet. “China-
town,” of course, is staged up to its full worth,
and moves rapidly in the first and third acts.
When the venerable Reuben and Cynthia busi-
ness has been hurried up, and the supply of other
encores is judiciously curtailed, the show will give
no particular cause for weariness. First-night
encores at the Princess’s look very well in the
Monday morning newspapers, but as often as not
they irritate 80 per cent, of the audience.
** * *
A smart turn by Spry and Austin, and the
eternal vivacity of Lottie Moore, and a ballet and
many matters which go to make a full programme
are happening at the Bijou. Richard Douglass is

week.
* * * *
Which remark applies to Scherek’s Orches-
tral Concerts at the Town Hall and to John Fuller’s
Myriorama co. The afternoon Orchestra with
Scherek conducting, and the evening Myriorama,
vocalised by tuneful Fuller and his co., keep the
Town Hall warm every Saturday till further
notice. Trustworthy authorities state that Fuller
in early days was never as robust and sweet a
tenor as he is now, and The Bulletin sees no
grounds for doubting the assertion. He sings like
one who has built up his constitution with sugar,
and his Myriorama is a good, honest shillings-
worth. Last Saturday he caught Scottish volun-
teer sentiment on the hop with “ Glasgow ” and
“Doon the Watter,” and this Saturday he will
balance matters by submitting “ Killarney, ” etc.,
to a Celtic and mixed audience.
Sundry Sydney clergymen have beer
smugly holding forth about recentlv-dead Rober.
Ingersoll’s failure to advance agnosticism ! Well,
lie did fail—in a way most parsons unfortunately
Jail to fail in.
“ Well, now,” said a N.S.W. politician to
his constituents at One Tree Pub., “ I have shown
von clearly how the land lies with regard to
Federation. It isn’t very late yet. Is there any
other question you would like, me to say a few
words about?” The audience muttered to itself
for a few minutes ; then a lanky son-of-the-soil
upreared his six feet of eucalyptus. “Well,
Mister, there is a question we’ve had a bit of talk
about among ourselves lately. Do you think the
British nation is the Lost Ten Tribes of Israel ? ”
And the politician gathered himself together and
spoke for an hour about the Pyramids, tree-and-
serpent worship, and the probable routes of early
Phoenicians voyaging for tin to Cornwall ; and
fogged the audience so satisfactorily that it
cheered him for five minutes and then mobbed the
platform to wring his arm off.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
South Australia’s surplus for year ended 30th
June, 1898, is £32,637. This is enough to wipe out the
previous year’s deficit and leave £3965 over.
* * * *
According to the N.S.W. Auditor-General’s re-
port for the year ended June, 1898, the Reid Government
has put up about as bad a record at the Treasury as any
of its predecessors. The A.-G. states that the deficit at
the end of 1891, when Dibbs took office, was £2,557,186,
and that Dibbs left it in 1894 at £2,735,511. This was the
figure at which Reid found it, and at June, 1898, he
had raised it to £2,898,714. Therefore, Dibbs increased
it by £178,325 ; and Iteid further increased it by £163,203.
Bad' as this result is, it would still be a point, in Reid’s
favor that he didn’t go quite so far to the bad as
his predecessor, only that supercilious fat - mail
heaped such scorn on the financiers who went
before him, and bragged with such sickening
persistence that he would never be “associated with a
deficit,” and declared with such brassy cheek that his
deficits were surpluses, that people expected better
things of him. The verdict on Reid, in view of the
Auditor-General’s report, is that he could brag harder
than any other Treasurer, and was less scrupulous than
any other Treasurer, but that he hadn’t a financial virtue
to recommend him.
» * * *
There is nothing gorgeous about the ’urnhle
balance-sheet of the Australian Joint Stock Bank ; still it
can be said that it is better than its immediate predeces-
sor. And that is about the most lavish praise that can be
safely expended upon it. The half-year’s profits to 30th
June, 1899, are reported as £1358 —equal to 4s. fid. per
cent, per annum on the shareholders’ alleged funds (capi-
tal, £1,168,041 ; reserve, £52,000). Some previous re-
turns compare as follows :

The A.J.S. Bank still says nothing about any writing-
down of capital to provide for losses. If it is assumed
that a bank, when all its assets are written down to their
actual present value (especially a bank which gets its de-
posits at so moderate a rate as the A.J.S. pays), should
earn 2j per cent, on its shareholders’ funds, then, on the
basis of last half-year’s earnings, the A.J.S. needs to
write-off, roughly speaking, 11,111,090 to put it on a
sound basis, and reduce its capital to about £lOO,OOO.
The increase in profits is presumably the result of the
new charge of ss. half-yearly on each current account—a
source of income which is proving a blessing to many
hampered institutions.
During the half-year the A.J.S. Bank’s note-
circulation has diminished by £18,400 ; advances have
increased by £197,000 ; cash and liquid securities have
diminished by £362,000 ; and deposits have gone down by
£161,000. The supply of information ends about there.
The A.J.S. lumps old reconstructed A and B deposits and
new deposits together, so it doesn’t appear whether the
new deposits are increasing (through people recovering
confidence in the bank) or diminishing (through people
losing confidence in the hank). It is known that, during
the half-year, some more of the reconstructed deposits
(amount uncertain) have been bought up at a discount of
an uncertain quantity in the £, and that a considerable
but uncertain profit has been made on the deal. The
profit in question doesn’t appear anywhere in the balance-
sheet, nor is it stated what becomes of it. If it is
included among the earnings, and even then the result is
only a visible balance to the good of £1358, the condition
of things must be something unspeakable, but a vague
impression prevails that, the profit made by the repur-
chase of deposits at a discount goes to write off deprecia-
tion. Still, there is absolutely no evidence either way.
Tho biggest asset of the A.J.S. Bank is “bills
receivable, bills discounted, and all other debts due to
the bank and other assets at old valuation's, £6,848,928.”
It would be impossible to give much less information than
that. The state of the profit account, the selling price of
shares, and the knowledge which everyone possesses of
thecollapse in “ old valuations,” are sufficient evidence
that this big asset is, in part, only a paper pretence and
no real asset at all. Presumably the management has
some idea as to how far it is only a pretence and how far
it is real, but it keeps the information very successfully to
itself. The saving clause about “old valuations” is, in
itself, a tolerably straight admission that the A.J.S. Bank
is putting before its shareholders a statement which does
not show how its accounts stand, and a balance-sheet
which, so far as showing the real position of the bank, is
no balance-sheet at all. And there is no law in N.S.W. to
make it present a balance-sheet which will show how it
actually stands.

The half-year just finished is thus the best which the
Ballarat Banking Co. has met with for three years. The
regular 5.4 per cent, dividend is paid, absorbing
£2337 ; the reserve fund is increased by £5OO,
making it £55,000; and the other £lB5, added

and liquid securities ('67,124) is equal to 31 percent, of

The steady improvement in the profits of the
Ausiralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank hasn’t gone on
quite so well of late, the earnings for the six months to
30th June, 1899, being only, £1257. This compares with
some previous half-years, good, bad and indifferent, after
this fashion : _ ■,

The profit is equal to ss. 7d. per cent., per annum on the
shareholders’ funds, and as the A. D. and M. Bank long
since got out of the dissipated habit of paying dividends,
the profit balance is now raised to £OBO5.
The results, however, are not so bad, considering
all the circumstances of the case. The Australian
Deposit and Mortgage Bank reconstructed for the
second time in 1895. It arianged to renew 70 per cent, of
its deposits at 3£ per cent., till 1910. The other 30 per
cent, bore no interest, and were repayable by three instal-
ments in 1896, 1899, and 1901. So the average rate of in-
terest which the bank has to pay for its deposits grows
larger as the non-interest-bearing deposits are paid off.
It was £2 9s. per cent, before the first payment was made.
It was then £2 14s. 6d. per cent. Now that the second
payment has been made, the balance of the deposits cost
£3 Is. 3<l. per cent., and, after 1901, the rate will be £3los.
per cent. The payments now being made are reducing
the amount of profit-earning funds, without reducing the
interest burden, and, under these conditions, the A.D.
and M. is holding its own reasonably well. Dur-
ing the past six months the amount owing to
the reconstructed depositors has been reduced from
£546,712 to £481,510. Other liabilities, apart from de-
posits, have been reduced slightly, and now stand at only
£13,000. The new current-account business has improved
somewhat. The bank has £36.815 in cash and £1,370,000
in various more-or-less realisable securities, mostly mort-
gages, which yield about £1 12s. per cent, per annum all
round at present, but which will probably be worth more
some day. As the institution can pay its wav and hasn’t
to meet the bulk of its deposits till 1910, it can afford to
sit peacefully on these securities and keep them warm,
and, if it sits on them long enough, many good things
may happen. If it had to realise in a hurry, however, the
results would probably be very bad indeed. They are not
the kind of securities to stand being hustled. As for the
dissipated habit of paying dividends, the A. D. and M.
isn’t likely to give wa.v'to that for many years yet.

December, IS9B .. 16,213 |
The 113,879 transferred to contingency account is some
more provision towards the losses which were not fully
covered at the writing-down of 1897. liow much these
amount to, and how long they will continue to swallow
up the profits, are matters of uncertainty, for the final
cleaning-up is necessarily a gradual process. Of the
other £10,900 (»qual to £4 15s. sd. per cent, per annum
on shareholders’ funds), 12500 goes to pay off the debt to
the Queensland Government, £5OOO to reduce the debt to
private depositors, and £2500 to reserve-fund, making it
£9OOO.
The Queensland National Bank is thus £23,879
nearer to making its assets and liabilities balance than it
was six months ago, though it is still a dreadfully long
way off that desirable state of solvency. The situation,
as near as it is possible to estimate, is something like
this:—
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital .. .. £412,644 Cash, advances
Reserve Fund .. 9,000 and other as-
Bills, notes, &c.. 512,616 sets and al-
Govt. Deposits.. 2,156,253 | ieged assets £6,686,343
Private ~ .. 5,062,285 I Deficit (say) .. 1,460,455
£8,152,798 | £8,152,798
The deficit in question is the amount (ss. in the £) which
was professedly written off deposits in 1897 to square the
accounts, and which was partly taken out of the balance-
sheet (so far as private depositors were concerned) and
partly left in (so far as the Govt, account was concerned).
As these amounts have to be paid out of profits, they
still remain a liability, and the above £1,460,455, or
something very near it, is really and truly the
Q.N. Bank’s visible shortage. Also, there is another
shortage (amount unknown, though something like
£380,000 has been suggested), being depreciation of the
£6,686,343 of assets mentioned above, which was not
adequately provided for in I he second reconstruction, and
it is towards this that the £43,564 carried to contingency
account during the last two years is applied. On a rough
estimate the Q.N.B.’s total deficit may be £1,830,000
or so, and it is being written off out of profits just now
at the rate of rather over £40,000 a year, at which pace
the trouble will be over in 45 years if there is no new
crisis in the interval. But there probably will be one.
Anyhow, it would be a vast amount more sensible and
more honest if the balance-sheet showed among liabilities
the whole 20s. in the £ due to private depositors-it
shows the whole 20s. in the £ due on Government de-
posits, and only 15s. in the £ of the money due on pri-
vate deposits—and the shortage was put boldly and
clearly among the assets, with a statement of the amount
written off it each half-year. It would also save much
weary conjecture, and mu h foolish burying of the
ostrich's head in the sand. The r-tate auditor should at
least furnish a complete balance-sheet. It isn’t any busi-
ness of his to conceal the awfulness of the old private
management.
« * • *
The Wilfley concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. This machine has a capacity of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt concentrator made.
It can he easily and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is £ll5.

The C.S. 'Building Society has a curious device of missing
one half-year’s dividend ; it pays a half-year’s dividend at
5 per cent, once a year instead of paying a whole year’s
dividend at 2\4 per cent, once a year. This harmless
little vanity enables it to fancy that it is a 5 per cent,
concern when it isn’t. The co. has £15,171 paid up capi-
tal, 1:1313 contingency fund, £lOOO reserve fund, and
£ll2 protit and loss balance. The great trouble is that it
is too small to be profitable ; expenses are kept very low,
and yet they absorb more than half the profits. The
earnings are not 3 per cent, on the shareholders capital,
so it would be more profitable and a good deal safer and
much less worry to put the State securities.
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc.,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant
this is one of I. and E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

B.H. Pr op. gives about a quarter the informa-
tion that it used to in its half-yearly reports ; yet there is
still good picking in them. Here, for example, are some
figures built up from the balance-sheet to May 31. The
total value of the various plants which the mine has had
is £975,000 : of this £644,000 ha been written off. The
B.H. smelting plants cost £116,571 ; they are now valued,
what is left of them, at £8073. Most of these plants have
been removed to Pt. Pirie ; and presutnabl3 r the value is
included in the item, “ Pirie smelting plant,” £88,360.
The sum mentioned does not therefore quite represent
the full value of the new plants. Other items tell elo-
quent tales of the heavy price which the big co. has paid
for its experience. The amalgamating plant, which (lid
less than a year’s steady work, ran into £39,880; it has
“ depreciated ”to £3370. The same thing happened in
connection with the earlier concentrating mills. Other
balance-sheet items: Reserve fund investments, £125,000
(at which they have stood for a long while now) ; pro-
ducts in transit, £101,716; products on hand, t 262,989.
If the co. were wound up to-morrow it reckons it would
be 440,802 to the good, without the mine and plant.
* • * *
The B.H. Prop. Co. has again paid away more
than its net profits. It paid 3s. per share (£144,000) in the
half-vear to Nov. last, and earned £122,151 ; it brought
down the divs. to 2s. 6d. last half (£120,000), and earned
£107,563. The co. has now 11 furnaces of 120 tons each,
and two of 80 tons each (a furnace’s tonnage is its daily
capacitv under certain standard conditions), which is
greater than any other co’s in the world, and far greater
than this co. ever had before. Formerly with these
furnaces it could have treated 300,00!) tons of its
oxidised ores in a half-year; now it cannot treat
more than 105,000 tons. The concentration of the
sulphides renders them smeitable; but the process
is very slow. This is due largely to the presence
of zinc, which cannot he wholly eliminated ; but it is also
due in some degree to the extreme fineness of the con-
centrated material —the fire does not get a chance in the
caking mass. Besides, the fluxing free lead ores are also
fine now. It is believed that the briquetting plant will
overcome this difficulty in a small degree ; but slow work
will still be unavoidable. The improvement may, how-
ever, allow the present 2s. 6d. per quarter div. to be con-
tinued. The larger concentrating-plant will presumably
he us»d to turn out more concentrates in order to ease off
the demand upon the fluxing oxidised ores ; so that
will not make much difference to profits. It would, in
fact, lessen them if it were not pretty certain that the
new plant will do cheaper and better woik than even th€
section already moving ; and that shows a big improve-
ment on everything that was done down to six months
ago.
Tn spite of its recent enormous expenditure on
new ore-reducing plant the B.H. Prop, does not extract
as much ore as it did before it began to handle sulphides.
In the half-year of ’96 corresponding to the one just
closed sulphides were only being nibbled at ; the total
out-turn was 230,451 tons, of which all but a few tons
was oxidised ore. The profit was £281,735. Last half-
year, with much heavier plant, 202,693 tons ore was
handled, of which 108,693 tons were sulphides ; the profit
(gross) was £139,164. What it is most important to
remember is'that every ton of oxidised ore broken was a
ton of ore to be sent to the smelters ; now every ton of
sulphides broken means only one-fourth of a ton of con-
centrates to be sent to the furnaces, where, moreover,
they can be dealt wdth only at a far greater ex-
pense per ton than was the case with oxidised ores.
No wonder, therefore, divs. have toppled, and the
managers spend anxious days planning economies. And
thev have done much in that way, as workmen very well
know. In six months of ’9l, 129,589 tons of ore, mostly
fairly soft, was raised and smelted for £195,500; in the
half-year just past, 202,693 tons of far harder ore, besides
98,302 cubic vds. of waste, was mined, much of it concen-
trated and roasted, and the whole of the product smelted
for within a few pounds of the same total. Out of the
127,589 tons, bv the wav, the shareholders made £672,124
profit ; out of the 202,693 tons—well, not enough to pay
divs. amounting to £120,000.

Booth’s United Gold Mines is aptly described
by the British Australasian as “a promising N.S.W.
concern.” Its special commissioner has had an interview
with one of the directors. Curiously enough, the direc-
tor’s name is not given. A great yarn of the richness of
the mine is accompanied by pictures and fills 2pp.
Mine just listed on London Exchange ; capital,
only £240,tX)0. Dealings said to have taken place at
about £2, and the fascination of a 100 per cent, premium
is the reason given for saying something about, the show.
Situation—Coolac, near Gundagai. Nine shafts sunk,
proving one block to contain 1,250,000 tons ore, which can
be mined as a quarry at 12s. per ton. “ The entire hill,
in fact, is a mass of gold-bearing rock.” Value of ore,
from Ito 6oz. In one place the lode is 7ft. wide, with no
walls; at another 12ft., with no walls; and at a third
30ft., and still no walls. Ore goes as low as sdwt. to loz.
in rmniense quantities, but No. 1 shaft shows from loz. to
6oz. per ton. “ Our engineers tell us that if we mix the
ore from the hill with the rich ore from the main shaft,
the whole proposition will average loz. per ton. The
value of the 1,250,000 tons already exposed is thus
£5,000,000. Working costs £1 per ton, and “ thus our co.
has an asset worth £4,000,000 sterling. The capital being
only £240,000, the shares on this estimate work out at
nearly £l6 per share.” Calculated only on ioz. per
ton, as this modest director prefers to do until the
crushings prove the higher return, the shares should be
worth £8 each ! All these modest figures are based
on the assumption that the ore will only last 100 ft.
in depth, but. it is anticipated that the hill will prove to be
a mass of gold-bearing rock, in which case the amount of
ore available will be stupendous. Mine equipped with a
40-head-stamp mill, shut dowm for want of water. In-
tended gradually to increase to 100 stamps : and this re-
tiring, unknown, unnamed director sees no reason why
they shouldn’t run 300 stamps like the Simmer and Jack on
the Rand. He does not anticipate any considerable ad-
vance in the price of shares for some months—on which
point writer fully agrees with him. Tell us—How many
people are there in N.S.W. aware of the existence of such
a gorgeous mine ? We have seen assays published from
Booth’s United during the last two years, but have never
heard of a crushing. There is plenty of water there now.
At any rate, £480,000 seems fair value for a mine that
never crushed a ton of ore. After the failure of the Gal-
lvmont, the Gibraltar disappointment, the bottom falling
out of the Wentworth mines, the sudden ending of Tay-
lor’s Reward, and the ignominious fate of half-a-dozen
other N.S.W. gold-mines, owned in London.it will take
something more than the publication of assays and the
“jabber” of anonymous directors to increase its value
beyond the £480,000.

* » * *
The dredging-boom has reached Port Darwin ;
will no doubt make its way across to Papua and Thursday
Island, coming round by way of Westralia. It would be a
joke to start a Coolgardie River dredging scheme. Like
most other booms, it was long incubating and then came
with a mighty rush. Bucket river-dredging has been going
on in Maoriland for the hist 12 years, but we have heard
more about it in the last year than in all the previous 11.
“Johnny” Wallace has been working his centrifugal-
pump system at Yackandandah over 10 years, and has had
a number of dredges working for several years past.
The writer ascribes the dredging-boominnosmall
degree to a remark alleged to have been made last year
byWallace—that he made over £20,000 profit in one year
from one of his dredging claims—the Staghorn. Did he
really say it, and, more important still, did he mean it ?
Whether or not, he could just as truly have said that he
spent over t 50,000 at Yackandandah before he received
that. £20,000. Bucket-diedging in new countries mustneces-
sarily encounter new difficulties, as .speculators in N.S.W.
and Vic. will discover to their cost ere long. Perfection
in Maoriland is the outcome of 10 years’ experience and
experiments to reduce cost and remove difficulties. Ex-
cept on one or two rivers—the Clarence, for instance —the
conditions are entirely different in N.S.W. and Vic., and,
in a great, number of cases, the deposition of tailings will
be so tough a job as to utterly knock out the business.
* * • •
The N.S.W. dredging-boom intensifies. Half-
a-dozen co’s launched last week in Sydney with the
alleged object of building dredges, anil all the shares
allotted before the poor public get a look-in. They, of
course, are expected to come in afterwards—at big pre-
miums, every opportunity being given in that direction.
A good time is here for promoters, brokers, and boom-
sters generallj'. Dredging is a really excellent industry
if conducted with discrimination and the judgment ordi-
narily applied to any other enterprise. Failure iri the
main is, however, a certainty on account of the way
people are going about it. Up to date no yields—in fact,
next to no tests. The game is to peg-out some old,
worked alluvial flat; more fashionable still is it to peg
out miles of a river, no time being given for tests. In
nine cases out of ten no lease is issued, but only a pro-
spectus. Some expert or ottier reports that the river or
flat is suitable for the business, and the public are asked
to wade in. It isn’t customary to require cash—this is
put forward as a redeeming recommendation—the pro-
moters (who take no risk) are satisfied with half the con-
cern in paid-up shares. The game is breaking down by
its own weight.
A reasonable assumption is that 100 schemes are
incubating in N.S.W. Of course, the promoters will not
wait for the building of the dredges ere they launch their
paid-up scrip on to lhe market. Not a word is said about
the difficulties already besetting one well-advanced
scheme, and the troubles that are bound to surround
many others. It is more than probable that when some
of them get to work in the narrow streams of N.S.W. it
will lie expensive, if not impossible, to prevent the
buckets bringing up the same wash over and over again.
Writer has often been struck, on walking into any Sydney
or Melbourne legal-man!’'"'-’s office, by the brass list of
dead co.’s on or near bis r and the long-disused seals
in his office. That forecasts the fate of at least 9-10ths of
the N.S.W. dredging schemes. A few will survive, no
doubt. The great majority will be as dead as Julius
Ciesar this day twelvemonth. In some cases companies
have been floated before the leases are issued, and it is
apparent that it is impossible for the Mines Department
to grant them without fatal injury to leases already
granted further down stream. In others, irreparable
injury will be done to the stream by the enormous quan-
tity of tailings deposited therein and to the surrounding
country. Never before in the history of the Mines and
Lands Department has so serious a question arisen.

Dear Bulletin. —About this dredging bubble. Turon
working eight weeks, tuning up three weekspreviously,and
now wants tuningup again. Visitors gone to see, returned
to-day crestfallen ; yet there is to be a new issue of £6OOO
for a new dredge ! Were the visitors told that Chinamen
scraped the bottom of the Turon 20 times over for every
speck of gold there ? * Mining boom ’—indeed ! Take the
Shoalhaven. In 25 years, 42 companies and syndicates
exploited there, and not one of them pa : d the carriage-
expqnses of the promoters. It is not a gold-bearing coun-
try. No gold in its banks to pay for working, and, as for
the river, the bottom all along is too rocky—take more to
quarry out the rocks than the expected gold would
pay for ; where rocks are not, it is so shallow that you
can walk across—and no dredge could float or work
there. Then take Araluen. Yes, gold used to be there.
So used Chinamen —they scraped there for 50 years, and
are scraping there still. Dredgers can scrape after them,
and get precious little. Something certainly may be left
for a dredge in Araluen, but there is nothing to even buy
tobacco with in the Shoalhaven.— H. Weight.

found the prospectus of the Colombo Dredging- Co.,
which has acquired an area of 96 acres on the Shoalhaven
River near Braidvvood (N.S.W.). It is in 30,000 shares of

will.

Just when the Referendum fight was on
in N.S.W. one of its very biggest labor-unions
was considering designs for a gorgeous new
J ) t a / lller - ; One design in particular pleased the
< ( b° sses >” but unfortunately it bore the motto,
lor Home and Commonwealth.” “Home and
Commonwealth ! ” exclaimed one leader ; “ that
means Britain and this blasted bill !’’ Whereat
there were groans. Of course it didn’t mean any-
thing of the sort; it just meant that that parti-
cular union was for the member’s home and for
the State—for the individual and the general
good. Still they decided to accept the design,
with this amendment of the motto: “For Home
and the Colonies /” And with that idiotic legend
it has gone to the painter’s. Who can wonder at
the disruption of that Labor-party?

greater in Tasmania than any mere Supreme
Court.

Many of Hobart’s old cemeteries (now
used as grazing-lands for butchers' sheep, by the
way) possess some really fine tombstones. The
second-hand dealer has recently been at work,
and, by getting permission from an impecunious
relative of the long-since defunct, and all who are
living of the cemetery trustees, saw a .£4O profit
sticking out. Almost the whole of the monument
had been removed, and the name-plate rubbed
out, when an interfering oldest inhabitant gave
the al.irm. Then it was discovered that the im-
pecunious relative had no legal authority to dis-
pose of the tombstone, which the second-hand
dealer was forced to replace. Thus is Private
Enterprise ever crippled by old-fashioned notions.

The White Star s.s. Co. (now going to take
a hand in the Australasian trade) was the first big
co. to shut down on the flirting-officer evil. Not
a few ships have been lost because officers in
charge were busy philandering. The White Star
captains’ cabins are so arranged that they can only
be entered from the bridge ; which makes it im-
possible for the dear girls to drop in “ fora cup of
tea,” and the other officers have to mess apart
from the passengers, with whom they are further
strictly forbidden to hold any unnecessary com-
munication.

The Bulletin urgently invites the resi-
dents of Manly to carefully read the Hansabd re-
port of the proceedings in N.S.W. Legislative
Assembly of July 25. The proceedings in ques-
tion, which are of the most vital interest to every
Manly resident, and also to every one who ever
visits Manly, are, for well-understood reasons, nob
fully detailed in the daily press—which, as a
matter of fact, gives an utterly inadequate and
misleading report. When the Manly people have
digested Hansard, they will no doubt hold a pub-
lic meeting to demand enquiry.

Encounter Bay district of S.A. is about
the last place in the world one would look for a
hot political fight; for where it is not pleasant
little country villages and tired townships it is
mostly sandy waste. Yet it has had a most ex-
hilarating time; . the place has been a perfect
blaze. And it is just likely that woman has had
a lot to do with it. Tucker and the O’Malley are
both beauty men—Tucker is a brunette with a
lovely mo., O’Malley a blonde with a beautiful
beard, and both of them groom and pose elabor-
ately. How could two such perfect killers appeal
for woman’s vote and influence without hating
one another cordially ?
* * * *
The saintly deacon M‘Turk, wielder of the
plate and general sacerdotal handy - man, has
placed his good character in jeopardy and left
himself open to base insinuations by a very
little oversight. M'Turk returned home late
from a pious festivity with his wife, and was
compelled by unforeseen circumstances to
climb in through the front window to
open the door. He took off his sacred
bell topper, and placed it on the
floor of the verandah beside his
paunchy, familiar brolly, ere under-
taking the task. Then he forgot
all about hat, brolly, and open
window, and went to bed. Next
morning people passing saw these
things, and flew to scandalous con-
clusions. Rumor fled in all direc-
tions, and when the great and good
MMurk came forth in conscious
rectitude, he discovered a jeering,
ribald crowd at his front fence, and
the gingham, the immaculate hat,
and the open window in suggestive
juxtaposition. People were even
ostentatiously smelling the hat for
fumes of drink. M'Turk is still ex-
plaining.

Three young men started
tin - kettling a newly - married
couple near Brunswick, Melb.,
one midnight recently, when the
happy bride got “among them” with a poker,
and dealt it out ” with much enthusiasm. Only
one of the push escaped without a wound. That
is the sort of woman who deserves to be mar-
ried often.
* • • *
The report that Yic. Commandant Holled-
Smith aspires to the Chief Commissionership of
Police bodes no good to the force. In England it
is common enough to give such posts to military
men, but there they have neither the official power
nor the social influence which they possess in Aus-
tralia, nor are the police regarded as a military
force. No British policeman would call the
general public “civilians ” as the Australian trap
does. In fact, the very essence of being' a good
policeman is to be a good citizen. A very good
soldier may make a very bad policeman, and vice
versa. The obedience and steadiness required in
the one do not necessarily imply the discretion and
good temper necessary for the other, and the
military element is responsible for much of the
police blundering characteristic of Australia.
Some practical experience of the beauties
of police-identification methods :
Ed. Bulletin.— Be the man Holt, lately un-gaoled as
wrongly-convicted. I was also arrested in Adelaide, and,
without being told what for, was placed in a row of other
men for “ identification.” It was under gaslight, but in
a shadowy place stood, with a policeman, a man whom
I subsequently found had been assaulted and robbed a
couple of days before, and I distinctly heard the police-
man indicate me as the man to be spotted. The man
accordingly positively identified me as one of the men
who garrotted him. Next morning I was called out
along with Holt and others, and three more prosecutors
were produced to identify their respective assailants.
One lady witness, on having looked carefully at each of
us, exclaimed, “ No, the man is not there,” whereupon
one of the detectives directed her attention specially to
Holt and myself, and pointedly asked her to make very
sure. (My prosecutor afterwards withdrew his identifica-
tion, much to the disgust of the police.)— Victim

At a recent Mayoral ball, the champagne
flowed like water, guests became lively, and, in
a moment of jollity, one gentleman threw a
turkey-leg at another. It landed in the
claret-cup, and the contents of the latter *
completely drenohed the Anglican bishop 1
Apology accepted, but what explanation did
his lordship make when he arrived home?
A bishop should not smell like a wine-
cellar.

A wedding at Hongkong, in June last, and
another in Sydney of more recent date, serve to
recall a tragic incident of the thirties in Illawarra
district. The grandfather of the bride in one in-
stance and the bridegroom in the other, a retiree’
army captain, had among other assigned servants
two women, one Scotch and the other Irish. For
some act of disobedience the captain threatened
to send the pair to the Bench of Magistrates for
punishment; whereupon the ladies attacked the
gallant ex-officer and performed a war-dance on
his body. As a result the captain died. When
making his dying deposition to his brother J.P.,
Capt. Allman, he desired that obliging official to
see that the two women were duly hanged. Which
the J.P. solemnly promised to do. The pair were
tried for murder before Judge Burton, found
guilty, and condemned to death, but Gov. Bourke
flatly refused to hang them until he had referred
the matter to King William IV. In course of
time came Silly Billy’s ” reply reprieving the
ha'd 611 a sentence of three years’

A certain N.S.W. country official is a
dapper little oliap with a hot temper and a
glass eye, and, coming to high words, the other
evening, with a “ commercial ” at the hotel, he
whipped out his glass eye, placed it on the mantel-
piece, doffed his coat, and invited the bagman
“ outside.” “ Right you are,” said the drummer ;
“but let us start level !” And, taking off a head
of hair that rivalled Paderewski’s, he placed it
alongside the glass eye. It was too funny; and,
after a roar of laughter, they resumed eye and
wig, and filled ’em up again.

* * * *
A few years back a N.S.W. resident cut
his wife’s throat. The children were upstairs in
bed and the parents were sitting quietly
down-stairs when the father
suddenly rose and pro-
ceeded to hack at
his wife with a
razor. He then
calmly proceeded
to the police sta-
tion and announced
his crime. The hor-
rified officers rushed
to the woman’s assist-
ance, just in time to
save her from bleeding
lo death. The man was
clearly insane and was
committed to an asylum.
His wife admitted that she
had been in dread of her
husband for some time.
Though in good employment he
was always haunted by the fear
that some day his children
would be in want and that he
would not be able to keep a
roof over their heads. They
were a devoted couple, and im-
mediately on her recovery
the woman began agitating
for her man’s release, which
was granted last January. The
doctors’ opinion of the case was
that the poor man had returned
to his senses, but his life hung on a
thread owing to his terrible re-
morse. His only chance was in
returning to his wife, and
they recommended his dis-
charge conditionally upon
her undertaking the risk
of living with him again.
The Chief Secretary was also to ap-
prove of a moderate allowance being
granted to the family, for it was
thought that if the husband knew that
there was always something between
his children and starvation, his chance
of ultimate thorough recovery would
be much improved. The experiment
is being watched with interest.

Dear Bulletin. —Re priests in
Philippines (B. 24/6/’99). You are wrong
in saying that the natives look upon in-
fusion of white blood as an improve-
ment. Natives and whites alike look
down upon the Srrani (Eurasian), and
though a native girl falls readily to a
white man, the resultant half-caste pro-
geny arrive most unwelcomely.— Yang A.
* * * *
Victorian Justice Williams preaches one
thing and practises another. About a year ago
he delivered a dreary sermon in the Australian
Church on the Criminal Law. Like most of his
non-judicia.l utterances, it consisted largely of
innuendoes directed at his chief, but for the rest
he spoke out strongly against long terms of soli-
tary confinement. One of his remarks was to the
effect that any judge who gave men long and fre-
quent terms of solitary confinement should be
forced to undergo a period of that particular kind
of punishment himself. Also he deplored the
want of uniformity among sentences imposed for
similar offences by different judges. That sounded
all very well from the pulpit of the Australian
Church, but what does this self-labelled humane
judge do when he sits on the bench to sentence
prisoners? During July criminal sittings he gave
one prisoner 90 days’ solitary in the last 18 months
of an 8 years’ sentence ! To another he dealt out
28 days’ solitary in two years, and even in a sen-
tence of six months he gave 12 days’ solitary.
Then, in regard to uniformity of sentences: he
sentenced a man who had pleaded guilty to fowl-
stealing, to two months on each of three charges
in respect to crimes committed at one and the
same time, with a period of solitary confinement.
This was after he had harangued the unfortunate
man for several minutes, during which he in-
formed him that he had been found guilty of a
similar offence at the previous General Sessions,
and sentenced, by another judge, to six months.
That, he said, was a very severe sentence for such
a comparatively small offence, and one which ha
would not have thought of giving him had he
been the judge.

The dispensing of afternoon-tea in churches
on Sundays is seriously discussed in Sydney dis-
senting circles. The expense of the innovation is
expected to be small, as it is calculated that mem-
bers of the congregation will take turns in provid-
ing most of the requisites.
* * * *
Women of the “ unfortunate class ” re-
cently complained to the Bendigo (V ic.) police
that little girls were competing with them, and
lowering the wages of shame in the local
Chinese camp. Enquiry disclosed that the
mother of one child disposed of her daughter to
a Chinese rag merchant for the price of a jam-
boree. It was further elicited that this orna-
ment of her sex was in the habit of
“harboring children,” which means a lot.
Another juvenile Flossie, “rescued” six months
ago, had succumbed again to Mongolian blandish-
ments. She and the other girl before the court
had, in three instances known to the police, in-
duced other little girls to visit the camp and be
“ initiated.” Australia is the country which sends
missionaries to China.

“ Speculum,” to The Bulletin
“Pu.vyaka” (B. 8/7/’99) dogmatises cheerfully. “The
hair-loving female is either a morbid product ora homo-
pithecal reversion.” Might as well say that the smooth-
man-preferring woman is a reversion to the still more
ancestral fish ! Also, the chances are that the average
woman prefers the average man, who is neither too much
Esau nor too much Jacob—a notion which is quite com-
patible with the assumption that if, through causes other
than sexual selection, the a.m. shall become less and less
hirsute, the a.w. will still continue to prefer him. As for
the respective strengths of hirsute men and smooth, such
cases as Sandow’s prove little. Sandow, according to him-
self, was rather a puny youth, and has only reached his pre-
sen t magnificent development by most carefully-regulated
exercises. But he is not a normal man ; he is an artificial
product, whose progeny (if any) will probably (like that
of most other manufactured athletes) he insignificant
physically as well as intellectually. Nature will, in the
m iss, permit only of the norm, and every temporary de-
parture therefrom contains within itself the seeds of re-
version thereto. And the norm, as regards human
strength, is, I take it, the hirsute man whom Sandow
himself acknowledges to be stronger, to begin with, than
the smooth man who is susceptible of a higher degree of
athletic manufacture. Apropos: I have taken much
physiological interest in athletics, and have known many
men, whose brawn was their profession, get into street
rows, but I have never known one who couldn’t be effec-
tively handled by the common, flat-footed policeman,
with just his normal uncultivated muscles to help him.
[Yet the policeman isn’t uncultivated ; he is a trained
athlete, in most British-language countries at least.—
Bulletin postscript.]

More about hair :
Dear Bulletin.—“ Puyyaka” (B. 8/7/’99) says: “Hair,
as an indication of strength, is a fraud. Consumptives
often become magnificently hairy, and the hirsute growth
over diseased localities in the body is well known.” Just
so. But unless that also proves (which it doesn’t) that
the smooth skin denotes the stronger constitution, it is
worthless as an argument, however apparent as a fact.
Further, hair often falls out as the result of disease.
Writer’s head resembled a billiard-ball after an attack of
pleuritis. Also, smooth skin is remarkable as a con-
comitant of physical weakness. Moreover, there is an
aspect of this question which will make “ Puyyaka ” rub
his (possibly smooth) chin and think hard. Are the
hirsute Scandinavian, the huge-moustached Montenegrin,
the full-bearded Briton and German, lower types than the
smoothskinned Chow and the beardless nigger? And,
finally, if the hair-loving female is “either a morbid
product or a homopithecal reversion,’’ there must be a
lot of her, and prolific at that, because the hairy man
occurs as often as the other fellow, up to date. —Ben Sun

Wanted : An artistic conscience. An ex-
cellent chance of giving' some good local artist a
commission, has just been lost by Vic. Govern-
ment. A fine, new cement bridge over the Yarra,
connecting South Yarra with Richmond, is just
finished. The sides of it wanted decorating. So
the dull, unimaginative Public Works Department
imported 12 hideous plaster griffins, all precisely
alike, and has stuck them on the bridge, six on
each side. They are ugly, poor, miserable beasts.
An artist like Longstaff, with
his fine eye for the grotesque,
would have done the de-
partment a set of designs
which would have made
the bridge worth looking
at. But these cheap and
nasty stucco monstrosities
are a discredit to Mel-
bourne. It is so like a
Government Department
to get a dozen things
all precisely alike and
stick them up with
mortar.

In a way-back town, where the stores kept
open till 9 o’clock, employes had met to endeavor
to get the closing-hour made 6. The following
evening into the drapery establishment walked
the local banker and his wife, just before 9 o’clock.
After lingering long over her purchases, the lady
sweetly remarked, “I hear you are starting an
early-closing movement! ” “Not at all, madam,”
promptly responded the shopman; “on the con-
trary, we’re thinking of keeping open till 10 for
the benefit of poor people who leave off work at
3 in the afternoon.”

From Shanghai to The Bulletin :
A word re missionaries, male and female. Last week I
saw that the inland missions of this country, run by the
Methodists and Church of England, received from Aus-
tralia the sura of £3016 for 1896. This does not include
the other churches who collect in Australia “ for the
heathen and our dear brethren laboring in China.” Dur-
ing a recent raining expedition up the Yangtsze Valley, I
found a mission-station near a place called Woolwo, 60
miles west of Nanking. Here there are two white males
and two white females living Chinese-fashion among the
people. They are unmarried, and wear native clothing,
the men having real pigtails. They report three converts
to Christianity last year, and two of those are mission
servants ! Our party of three white men wished accom-
modation, but were refused. We, however, found fair
“diggings” with some Buddhist priests in a temple
pitched on the summit of a 500 ft. hill close by. The
heathen were kind and hospitable, and since that date
we have found that “ the Joss-house” is the best place in
the land to camp in when it is too wet to pitch a tent.
All experienced travellers here give missions a wide berth
just because accommodation is refused nine times out of
ten, and, secondly, because the parsons are so inquisitive.
In one mission where we did succeed in finding lodgings
we found that the Chinese had made much more impres-
sion on the seven inmates than the latter had made upon
the thousands of natives outside. All the household,
three men and four women, dressed in Chinese style, and
never by any chance did anything for themselves that
could be done for them by Chinese servants. The house-
hold is presided over by an elderly New Englander, his
wife being second in command. Two Chicago men and
three young girls complete the show. We retired early
that night, and rising at daybreak, did some prospecting
in the vicinity. Returning at 11 a.m. we found no one
astir except the servants, and from them learned that
“ the Kweitya” (foreign devils) never rose before noon —a
well-established Mandarin custom. This mission returned
“18 converts to Christ ” as the result of last year’s
labors, and apparently they are all employed at the es
tablishment.

All Drapers Everywhere.
It is encouraging to notice in a rpcent number
of Pbarson's Wkkki.v, that the public in Kurland
were appreciating the South Australian invention, the
“ Albatross Lamp Wick.” They endorse all that is claimed
for it, viz., “ that it requires no trimming,” saves oil,

The trend of fashion for the past few years has
been along a course of unusual brilliancy, and for the
season just opening, if one may judge by what prevails at
home, that vain goddess who presides over our fashions
will dispense styles with a lavishness and perfection
worthy of the federal year. Our leading fashionable
drapers are, of course, on the qui vive with plans for
dealing with every variation of style ; but our best infor-
mation, from a well-informed source, hints that the most
magnificent costumes, mantles, blouses, &c., are to be
seen at the well-known Melbourne firm’s, Messrs. Stainer
and Co.’s, Swanston-street, who have this season had the
extraordinary advantage of a special personal selection,
extending over all the noted spheres of refined fashion at
home, and whose parasols, feather boas, robes, and lace
delicacies of all kinds are described, by one who has seen
them, as “ a revelation.”*
