A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 1, 1599.
Mr Dear Myee,—
All very well to sneer at the thought of asking
800 guests to play on your staircase, as the
Equitable Life Assurance Society did on its 40th
birthday, but that style of entertainment has its
good points. For once, every woman was seen
at her best. In a ball-room, a well-dressed
woman gets lost in a crowd of other well-dressed
women. She looks charming in her bed-room,
before she leaves home, when the family gathers
round and says “ How lovely !” Then she goes
to the function, and gets swallowed up, crushed,
robbed of her identity, as it were, in a crowd of
other identities. But on a staircase all is different.
You see a petite creature in white gauze sweeping
down the wide stairs, and her beauty gets a chance
to smite you. In a ball-room she would be un-
noticed. On the staircase she moves apart from
her surroundings, and frock and face have full
play. After that, you may be surprised to hear
that one never saw so many pretty women at a
Sydney show before.
The Equitable broke out brilliantly at this
party. Mr. Carlisle Taylor, Mr. D’Arcy Irvine,
and others received their guests on the entrance-
steps ; which isn’t a too highly successful way of
welcoming people. Women in goloshes and
ballooned-out overcloaks over a turned-up skirt,
want to dash madly straight into the dressing-
room. A lighted portico is always a trial to a
wrapped-up woman on a wet night. She who can
stop calmly and shake hands, and send up a sweet
smile from under a forbidding headgear of wet
black net, is quite a heroine. The feminine part
of. the 800 guests rushed past the hosts, looking
the other way.
His Ex. and Lady Mary Lygon arrived at half-
past eight, and were ushered straight into the
concert-room. Other people found their way
about by printed programmes, that directed to
the joys of different floors. It was a great glitter-
ing Aladdin’s-palace sort of affair. Everywhere
there was something to see, free. On the first
floor, the concert-room ; on the second, Mrs. St.
Hill, a very crack palmist, just from London (by-
the-way, she is no relation to Miss Alexia St. Hill,
who has quite given up palmistry lately for
“metaphysics”): on the third floor, Madame
Raprah, a physiognomist ; on the fourth, a cine-
matograph ; on the fifth, a ball-room and luring
Bavarian band. Refreshments went on every-
where. Therein lay the only blur on this beautiful
spree. Many people went home without finding
out the solid spread down underground in the
Manhattan Tea-Rooms.
The frocking was remarkably well-considered.
Mrs. Willie Mitchell was in cerise satin, slashed-
up over flounces of white chiffon, with short black
chiffon sash. Mrs. Learoyd was ethereal enough
to wear a gauzy white mousseline se soie. Miss
Grace Noble topped a blue-brocade skirt witli a
chiffon bodice, and wore the new black unintelli-
gent flowers on her corsage. Mrs. Ted Rundle
was also in light blue, a lovely combination of
poplin and pearls. Mrs. Harry Levy wore white
net shimmering with silversaquins. Anothersnappy
sequin frock was worn by Mrs. “Nellie Cox ’’Trench,
who is paying a flying visit to her family ; she still
wears her hair & la Lady Carrington, in a thick
fringe on her forehead. English women stick to
this style persistently, led on by the Princess of
Wales, Countess of Warwick, and others. (By
the way, I saw it printed the other day that Lady
Carrington’s hair had gone snow-white). Mrs.
Trench wreathed the latest circlet of pink baby-
roses round her top-knot. So did Lady Macleay,
and several others.
As this Conversazione was about the smartest
thing we’ve had for ever so long, one gathered
many hints and forecasts of fashion. For instance,
the hair was all dressed top-mast high, and well to
the front of tire head. Flags of ribbon, which look
careless, but are carefully wired, waved alongside.
Several smart women tied artless blue or pink
ribbon round the knob, and pinned a tall black
feather straight up in front. Others heaved gardens
over their heads. Paris says that flowers in the
hair must be so many and so widely spread that a
middle-aged flower bonnet results. And every
well-educated skirt is a flat waste below the belt
at the back. The placket-hole is invisible at the
side. Not a gather of any sort must disturb the
flatness of that back-breadth. On a thin woman
it is dangerous, on a fat one it is death—sartorially
speaking.
In the concert room, vice-regal people sat in the
front row quite close to the performers. His Ex.
talked to Lady Bertha, who was in black with
white chiffon sleeves. The little feminine ferment
who was driving everybody to despair, has dis-
appeared lately. She and her mother are on a
visit to their country home, and other girls breathe
again. As for the concert, everyone was in such
a highly-excited state, thinking of the palmist and
the physiogonomist and ballroom, that the artists
caught the flurried atmosphere of the audience,
and didn’t all perform on their best legs. Signor
de Beaupuis played Liszt with a golden touch.
Naturally, his Ketten piece didn’t go well to the
accompaniment of the big band playing the “ Blue
Danube” outside. Stael and Vollmar—of course ;
Vollmar was presented to Lady Mary after-
wards, and congratulated. Miss Beatrice Esmond
I heard for the first time. She is a pretty little
woman, with a lot of history attached. She sang
with a strange lip contortion, but with plenty of
go. She and Madame Lotta Harkness give joint
recitals next month. One utterly-uncooked pro-
duct got into the programme somehow, and tossed
all the largest Horsetralian vowels in your teeth
with a self-complacent smile. Considering all the
other only half-raw singers belonging to us, this
quite “raw” is unforgivable. Mr. Beattie’s round
sweet voice rang out cheerfully in the Toreador’s
song. The concert lasted but three-quarters of an
hour, without one repeat. Yet the programme
was of ordinary length, which shows how well
tilings can lie done under good management, and
without encores.
A performance of indescribable unspeakablencss,
in which a sister played Romeo to another sister’s
Juliet, and another sister played Paris to her other
sisters, punished the boards of the Palace Theatre
on Tuesday evening. “Romeo and Juliet,’’mis-
interpreted by the Maoriland family of play-at-
actors, showed how very long charity suffereth.
Some of the generous patrons, who had come
through the rain to the d.c. and front stalls to
support the deserving Sydney Hospital, sat out
the performance. However, if the hospital bene-
fits in the long-run, the means are forgiven for the
sake of the end. The Greenwoods do not
possess a single R amongst the five or six of them.
Under these conditions, you will understand how
hard it was for anyone to call Romeo or Paris by
name ; and then there were other things.
Speaking of charities, the Grand Fancy Fair
opens on Thursday, August 10th, at the Exhi-
bition Building. I daresay you “ know that all of
yore, ” as placards are everywhere pasted on the city
to tell the tale. Lord Beauchamp has the Souvenir
Book under his charge. An heroic committee
invited him to throw off a little poem as his auto-
graph contribution, but he smiled sadly and
murmured “No more poetry,” or words to that
effect. But Lord Brassey, who has no poetry to
speak of in his past, comes out with a verse of
Browning.
Masonic Ball came off at the Town Hall
last Thursday in an enormous crowd. This hop
always draws record galleries. Draws, too, all
that is weird and wonderful in the way of frocks
and coiffures. Some Aldermanic ladies came out
in toilettes a pork-pie-dream matron might
affect. For instance, there was a fifteen-stoner,
in a pink satin skirt to her ankles, a red velvet
blouse, white satin shoes, paste buckles, and pearl
trimming stuck on everywhere a blank space
offered, and hair done up alongside of a bunch of
cabbage roses with long green stalks ending in
dewdrops rearing out of the roses. But she was
nothing to a twonty-stoner in green fly-net, with
no sleeves, and no neck, and a tower of auburn
hair, surrounded with balconies of red poppies and
pink bows, and paste buckles. A moral lesson in
{esthetics is always protruding from these balls.
Many of the frocks, if you could see them lying on
the bed with nobody inside them, would strike envy
into your heart. But the wearers sidle at them,
shy away from them, never melt into their., and
carry them on their backs as though there was a
great gulf fixed between them. All the same the
hop went off like a cannon-ball. His Ex. and
Lady Mary came after the Masonic ceremonies,
and mixed themselves up in the crowd, generously.
Lady Mary always dances a good deal. His Ex.
contented himself with the V.R. Lancers and
Mrs. Remington (in yellow brocade), wife of the
Grand Master. Nobody in this inside set was
dressed outrageously well. Lady Mary repeated
a solemn grey brocade, with bands of dark fur at
the neck. The Hon. Violet Somerset cooled her
bright cheeks in black satin. Miss Daisy Wright
was the smartest figure on the dais—in black silk
muslin, interwoven with a dull-blue pattern, an
outline of turquoise velvet at the top of the bodice,
and a long black feather in her undulated hair.
Mrs. Teece scattered black-jet trimmings over a
white brocade, and struck yellow notes in her
smartly-dressed head. Mayoress and Miss Harris
were both in beaded black.
By-the-way, Mayoress Harris is to be given a
complimentary tea by lady members of various
charitable committees on which the beaming
mayoressal smile has so often shone. The tea will
be altogether feminine. It comes off at the
“Australia ” on Thursday next.
The Society of Artists is getting ready for its
annual ball, which is to be a plain one this year—
that is to say, not fancy. Mr. Goring-Thomas and
Mr. Leo Dobbin are acting as secretaries to the
hop, and have fixed on Aug; 31st, at Needs’ Rooms.
It seems that a much-talked-of engagement
leaked out through an unexpected hole. It was
not intended for the public ear just yet, but a girl
friend of the bride’s caught sight of the engage-
ment-ring one day, and wormed out the story—
under the seal of dead secresy, of course. The
bride’s family were in recent, and deep, mourning.
They wanted to keep the matter quiet awhile. So
quiet, indeed, that even the bridegroom’s people
hadn’t been acquainted with the news. But the
girl-friend simply couldn’t contain such copy. She
wrote off a detailed account of all she had been
told to a Melbourne paper. Next day, a Sydney
paper reprinted. Then there was consternation.
The man’s family attacked him for not telling
them. The girl’s consumed themselves with
wonder how that paper ever got to know. “ Isn’t
there anyone you told as a dead secret ? ” asked
the bridegroom-to-be. “ No, there was only one
girl I told as the very dead, dead, deadest secret,”
said the bride. “ Humph ! ” said the bridegroom
—who is a widower and knows a thing or two.
At times, Henrietta Watson, in the “King’s
Musketeer," looks like Sister Elizabeth. Shelias
altered considerably since she was here last, and
is much thinner in the face. From a fine, strapping
girl, she has merged into a Potteresque being with
her dark hair puffed round her face like a Beards-
ley being, and shows a. new genius for posing
with her back to the audience, and her hands
behind her. But those marvellous, sloping
shoulders of hers have come back unspoiled, and
she reveals every inch of them in Miiadi’s low-cut
frocks.
On Saturday a big - house welcomed the new
play warmly, but without a single flower. And
the d.c. was clad in black almost to a
woman. The whole of the front row leaned
over out of black trappings, and the flowers in the
hair had to be very gorgeous to brighten up this
sombreness. Theatre gloves are quite defunct.
Long sleeves to the knuckles take their place.
But what an ugly thing is a long, thin, knobby
ann, like a hat-rack, creeping barely out of no
sleeve to speak of, and continuing in wicked
nakedness down to its scraggy finger-tips ! The
best-dressed Society woman in Sydney always
wears short sleeves, and goes gloveless at theatres
and concerts: but then her arms have a natural
covering.
Is Newbury trying to educate the people up to
Beethoven at a shilling a head ; or is he hoping to
translate the classics into popular slanguage ? At
a recent Pop. he had the lights turned very low
for “The Moonlight Sonata.” Friedenthal was
playing. But it was a nasty, unsoulful, ignorant
house. The back seats thought that the dimness
meant they were to kiss their girls, and un-
attached boys set up a hubbub. After some
yelling and stamping the lights were turned up,
and it was found that Beethoven could survive the
strongest light.
Some new songs from Paling and Co. are “A
Fairy Song,” by Wilfrid W. Gibson and Edith
Dick, a light, little, pretty thing, that must not
be executed by anyone over fourteen stone ; “Rosa,
a Spanish Serenade,” by A. C. Bunten, a well-
written song that wails tunefully about hearts and
guitars ; and “ Australian Federation, ” by A. Seal,
Professor, which I scarcely dare call a song nor yet
a hymn, as the title seems to argue against either.
“ Advance Australia ” occurs often, and the music
has a sacred ring, that should recommend the piece
to Y.M.C.A.’s drinking lemonade round a chilly
fire. Paling and Co. also send Sylvia Blackstone’s
new waltz, “Zarona, ” a thing of chords and
crashes, which isn’t up to her other waltzes,
although it was written expressly for and dedi-
cated to Lord Beauchamp. Glen and Co. send
“ As a Bird from its Nest,” poetically frontis-
pieced with a portrait of Amy Castles, which
reads and sounds like an attempt to tell Amy to
say her prayers and be a good girl, and come back
with a big fortune.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
‘‘Hon. Tom Brassey is coming out on a brief
visit to his noble Dad at Government House,
Melbourne, to talk to him seriously re his health,
his habit of falling-off, and other matters. Judging
from some of Gov. Brassey’s recent appearances
in public, it is quite time that his family persuaded
the ex-mariner to take more care of himself.
“Other days, other ways. A present Australian
governor meekly bows his hoary head before the
little tempers of his last-born. Recently driving
in semi-state through metropolitan streets, the
viceregal couple wedged the infant in question
safely between them, ignoring the rebel’s resist-
ance, so as not to invite public scrutiny into her
mutiny. Meantime Mksie frowned her blackest,
doubied-up and straightened-out like a jumping-
jack, and elbowed the parental ribs.
“Pa Brassey has just been informed by cable
of the advent of a new grandchild (daughter
Freeman-Thomas’s second son). Joy-bells would
be ringing had the mannikin fallen to the share
of his Ex.’s only son and Lady Idina Brassey ( nee
Nevill).
“Despite brilliant press notices, the two last
viceregal balls were practically frosts. Numbers
of jteople, usually invited were left out, and of
those who received invitations and accepted
them, only about half responded in person.
At the Friday ball, during one dance only three
sets of lancers were going.
“ Champagne bottles at a certain grand ball had
their labels scraped off. From the streaks of label
still sticking to the pint-bottles on the supper-
table nobody, however astute, could guess the
brand. Was that done by the magnificent
lacqueys because the brand was so-so, or for fear
of jealousy among the many big agents who are in
a social position to be invited ?
“ The latest vagary of fashion: Two well-known
Society girls, in half-mourning for their sister-in-
law, appeared at last Government House ball in
chastened costumes, with narrow black ribbons
round their necks, tied in bows in front, the long
ends falling almost to their skirts.
“ At a novel tea in Melbourne a girl appeared
with an illustrated post-card pinned to her dress.
On the front of the card was a picture of Govern-
ment House. Few guessed the novel intended;
it was ‘Uncle Tom’s Cabin.’
“ One of Melbourne’s really nicest girls is going
to join the Brough Comedy Co. shortly. The
belle is Miss Edith Gavin Brown, a witty and
clever amateur actress who seems built to do things
on the stage.
“A full house welcomed back the ‘Chinatown ’
trippers with seeming glee. On the first night
everyone must have indulged in an extra dab of
make-up. Even Wallace Brownlow seemed to
have overdone his cultivation of a glassy-blue
stage-glance and red-rosebud mouth. In certain
lights, the new girls looked as if they’d been
smelling a rat in the flour-bag, and some of them
affected blue black top-eyelids. Carrie Moore—
pert, pretty, light-heeled—was, maybe, the cutest
she-critter on view.
“ Madame Wiedernmnn sang nine songs at the
Brahms concert in Melbourne last Friday—fancy
nine Brahms songs at a breath, as it were! And
ye amateurs—notice how ‘Madame’ sings, not
only with her voice but with her brains. When
she sings ‘ Wenn ieh bleicli und halt,’ it is bleak
and cold, and you feel a chill running all through
the lino. It takes more than a voice to make a
singer like the Wiedermann. The voice is just the
instrument ; it is the brain of the artist behind it
that makes the singer.
“ Curly-headed Louis Hattenbach, the ’cellist,
appeared for the last time before his departure for
America at a Brahms concert last Friday. An
evening of Bralnns music might be expected to be
rather headachy ; for Brahms is, above all
others, an intellectual musician. But the selec-
tions were judicious, and the concert delightful.
In Brahms’ concerted music, the instruments seem
to be constantly arguing with each other, as in
Beethoven. The argument starts in the allegro
movement; heads are broken, and the combatants
jump on each other in the allegretto ; and in the
final movement they trudge off to the hospital,
arguing all the way.
“In time even the square peg gets to fit its
round hole not so badly. Most municipal mag-
nates die fatted flesh-potters, not excepting those
who began life with yearnings after a diet of rose-
leaves and dew. Lately a team of athletes
arriving from a sister-province were driven from
the train to the local civic stronghold. At that
time of day, the travel-stained, jaded guests
apparently took little joy in the provided ‘cakes
and ale.’ But it was a Horrible Warning to watch
the hosts gormandize, scarcely pausing to snort for
air between their gulps of confectionery and fizz.
“Grandmotherly disciplinarians justify their
advocacy of the rod by reference to V.R.’s long-
ago visit to Belfast (Ireland) with some of her
then small fry. As the royalties drew near
Belfast quay, sounds of smart slaps and youthful
wailings wafted from the cabin-in-ehief told wel-
coming subjects that their Gracious Queen could
wield a corrective slipper as well as a sceptre.
“ Many people here remember Mrs. Brunei
Norntan (Adey Brunei) who elocuted in Australia
a few years ago. She was a niece of Sir William
-Tenner, the Queen’s physician. She had a hus-
band with her—-a mild erratic youth. The pair
now run concerts in London, as ‘ Mr. and Mrs.
Norman-Concorde.’ By last mail arrive pro-
grammes of Norman-Concorde concerts; and
announcements that the said N.-C.’s ‘guarantee
the artistic ability of those they specially recom-
mend.’ The cleverest thing the little woman did
in Melbourne was a delightful imitation of the
manner of a sister elocutionist's pupils, a malicious
bit of burlesque, the remembrance of which sends
those who ever saw it into fits of laughter when
they see one of the said pupils reciting, even to
this day.
“Be those Ballarat Reformatory girls, who ran
away, because, so they said, they didn’t like
‘man’s work, digging wells,’etc. ; some damsels
prefer rough and ready be-done-with-it jobs to
endless, pernieketty household - pottering. I
know a sturdy sixteen-year-old ‘rescued’ from a
supposedly cruel step-father, who made her
split timber, help with the cross-cut saw, See. The
fugitive was put in training for domestic drudgery,
but soon fled back to the bark humpy, where she
went bare-foot, lived on nanny-goat and pumpkin,
and slept under the stars whenever her guardian
got drunk. ‘At the old man’s,’ she explained,
‘ when I ain’t splittin’ 1 lay on the broad of me
back and see how long I can keep on lookin’at the
sun. Here I get no spell. If I ain’t workin’, I’m
jorin’—tel I in’ how old I am, and if I can read, and
whether I’ve ever been to church, and all that.’
“Flats are becoming fashionable in Melbourne.
The big building called Queen Bess Row, formerly
Miss M'Cartncy’s private hospital, is now entirely
taken for lodgings, and contains all manner of
people—families, bachelors, spinsters; most of
whom hurry off in the morning to their various
offices. Occasionally from an attic to the next
floor is heard a cry of “ Can you lend me a loaf,
or a drop of milk?” or “ Wko'n taken my broom
—’pon my word I think it’s a shame,” &c. Every-
one is peaceable on the whole, and all wander In
and out at all times with latch-keys.
"V ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

Aboriginal King Mickey, of Moree, toured
his district lately, and reports that he found
among all his subjects intense loyalty to his throne
and person.
“C. 8.”: Was coming from Brisbane by
boat; Clement Wragge, Queensland’s meteorolo-
gist, was also a passenger. Saw him scrutinising
the heavens. “Is it going to rain?” I queried.
He laughed and said, “Not just yet, but I'M
make it rain as soon I fget to Sydney.” It
rained.

AT THE JUMBUCK AND BOBEE
DEBATING SOCIETY.
MR. BRIGAOW BOGONG : “ Mr. President an’
yen’elmen, my opponent in speakin’ fer Federa-
tion, ses that the United States of America’s
Federal laws was perfect. Well, they ain’t so;
an’ I ask you, gen’elmen, to look at their in-
famous law—the Lynch law.”
He was the only man of whom lawyer
Jellicoe was frightened ; and, on the other
hand, Jellicoe was the one man for whom Rich-
mond had a wholesome respect. The word-
contests between the red-faced, tubby, little
Englishman at the Bar, and the gaunt asthmatical
skeleton who adorned the Bench, were things to
remember.
A voice in the hall asked a Jewish candi-
date, at last Vic. general election, if he favored a
tax on bacon. “ No! ” said the Hebrew, promptly,
“ but I stoutly favor one upon hogs at public
meetings.”
Who is the clumsiest and most mischievous
person just now in the publiceye ? Answer : Car-
dinal Moran, who is making an even more “holy
show ” of himself over the missionary business
than he did with respect to his Federal-Conven-
tion candidature.
Goldfields business makes a fourth Judge
imminent in Westralia. Ex-Vic. politician Penne-
father is Attorney-General, but ’Groper influence
is against him, and Leake’s chances are favored
if he likes the post. If it is to be a non-political
appointment, then F. W. Sayer, of the Law
Office, may go up.
Sergt. Rogerson, the Melbourne policeman
who fought the good fight with two housebreakers
whilst a casual bicyclist looked on and saw foul
play, was an English public schoolboy, and at 21
went to India with a comparative fortune. The
indigo smash left him a “ broker,” and he came
to Melbourne to repair his shattered fortunes in
the foorce.
Allerdale Grainger, M.P., ex-journalist
broker, hauled up by Speaker Coles in S.A.
Assembly, the other day, for accusing him of
being “too dictatorial,” is the John Norton of
S.A. Parliament. Grainger, who stands 6ft. 3in.,
lately said that the S.A. public debt had increased
to 25 millions and the population to 350,000, ‘ ‘ for
which Premier Kingston was entirely respon-
sible.”
Slab of eloquence from gasbag Monk, of
Waitemata, M.L.:
And that head appeals to our imagination as well as to
our hearts—appealing even in the mute eloquence with
which it asserts its claim to a transcendent fame, bring-
ing to our minds the story of a reign that is distinguished
for a towering ascent of British power, and specially
replete with scientific discovery and marvellous invention
—a reign in which the feats of Aladdin dwarf before the
colossus of her people’s wealth, and the poet in the
highest fervor of his dreams but acts the drowsy laggard
in the wake of her nation’s progress. Do you think, sir,
I am wrong in pleading for the replacing of the effigy of
a hc-ad so significant witli transcendent memories upon
the postage-stamps of our colony?
11l connection with the recent cable about
a Russian officer suiciding because the Czar had
reproved him for letting the deceased Czarewitch
cycle unattended, it is wortli while noting the also
recent press-statement that “in the Caucasus
murder has of late years reached an incredible
degree. The land is becoming uninhabitable, so
infested is it with robbers.” Put it all together—
“ Hemorrhage of the throat ” (Ist account); “ bi-
cycle accident” (2ndaccount); “allowing George to
bike alone ” (acompanion could not have prevented
a smash), “and the local prevalence of murder.”
It looks as if yet another Romanoff has been mur-
dered and the thing is being kept quiet.
Not generally known that Chief Justice
Robert Stout has in his veins a mixture of Spanish
and Scandinavian blood. The Iberian strain
comes from an officer who escaped drowning at
the wreck of one of the Armada ships in the North
Sea and found an asylum and a wife among the
Shetlanders, who are pure “Scandies.” This
possibly accounts for the sallow complexion and
coal-black hair of Stout.
The Kipling influence gets into even the
British Wesleyan Methody Conference. A Rev.
F. W. Macdonald has been much paragraphed as
the uncle of Kipling, and the advt. has no doubt
helped him into the office of president, in suc-
cession to the wordy Hugh Price. Hughes.
Besides Kipling’s mother, the Rev. President Mac.
has the wives of knighted artists Burne-Jones
(deceased) and Poynter for sisters.
Wm. Malpas, who stepped out of a boat
at Holdfast Bay, the birthplace of South Aus-
tralia, ran up a tent, and with an associate forth-
with opened business for the English-owned Bank
of South Australia, died in Adelaide the other

“ The reward of valor.” Of the half-
dozen leaders who were gaoled for their part in
the Broken Hill miners’ strike of ’92, the district
now knows only three—Sleath, Ferguson and Pol-
'inghorne. Sleath and Ferguson are in N.S.W.
.Parliament. And now the Labor-party, having at
last secured a majority in the local municipal coun-
cil, provides Polkinghorne with the first public
office available —the assistant-inspectorship of
nuisances!
Thomas Wardell Hammond is dead, at 74.
It was Hammond’s Speckleback which carried
news from Old Junee to Wagga—22 miles in one
hour —when bushrangers stuck up Old Junee
nearly 40 years ago. Speckleback won that time ;
but, a week later, the Ben Hall gang stuck up
Hammond and made him hand over Speckleback
and five other of his best horses. Hammond was
a founder of the Riverina Racing Club, and was
very popular in his district.
Napier (Maoriland) Herald on a good old
warrior who will be much missed when his time
comes:—
The old fire of Richard Seddon, the once lion-hearted,
is dying out with increasing age and the f oils of office,
involving more work than is done by any other half-a-
dozen men in the province. The old warrior is failing
rapidly, and when Russell or anyone else climbs over
him to the Premiership, they will have defeated a weary,
worn-out man. But he dies game, fighting for his head
to the last ditch.

No. I—The Bakes.
Mr. T. Streater writes: “ I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. I am a baker by
trade, which,-as you know, is very hard work, but
I am pleased to say that when I am done of a
night, I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh again, and then I go and enjoy
myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-
Cocoa I used to mess about indoors as tired as
could be.”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted dietetic
value, Dr. Tibbies’Vi-Cocoa is at once digestive
and refreshing, nutritious, and invigorating. This
is the secret of its phenomenal success. It seems,
so far as we can gather, to agree with everybody,
and everybody is agreed in giving it a good name*.
Apparently it is the accepted beverage of a people
who have recognised and appreciated its remark-
able health-giving properties. And, certainly, in
this age of rush and worry, wear and tear, when
the energies of mind and body are so severely
taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers, and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.
As an unparalleled test of merit, a dainty
sample tin of Vi-Cocoa will be sent free on appli-
cation to any address, if when writing (a post-card
will do) the reader will name The Bulletin.*

W. Gr. Brookman, the Boulder millionaire,
formerly ran a jam-factory ; now, someone has
started a jam-factory on his behalf—in a Perth
paper. Brookman has just shifted into a new
residence, and after writing a column-and-a-half
about Brookman’s bronzes, and his parrots, and
(40-guinea) window-curtains, and (100-guinea)
vases, the obsequious scribe winds up thus :
A charming room is Mr. Brookman’s own particular
sanctum or study. It is also hung with dark-tinted
paper, and the carpet is in consonance : also the carved
walnut furniture, roll-top desk escritoire, book-
oase, and cabinets, &c.; while the centre table is
covered with a cloth of Indian make, embroidered
in gold thread. A quaint object in one corner
is a polished walnut barrel on a stand, having silver
hoops, and labelled “ Falstaff.” On opening this is dis-
covered a liqueur-stand, with every requisite for the re-
freshment of the inner man. A novel tobacco case is a
stag’s head in bronze, and novel figures here and there
hold aloft electric globes in colored glass. All over the
house a blaze of light is instantly thrown by the pressing
of a button in the hall. The window-hangings in the
study and dining-room are bordered with guipure d’ap-
plique, of most expensive style, and in the best bed-
room, which is as large as the drawing-room, the
window-curtains are of brocade, lined with satin, and
edged with a foot-wide border of Venetian Honiton
lace, each pair (there are three) costing sixty guineas.
The bed, which looks like the State beds Jin pictures of
kingly palaces, is of the Louis Seize style, and is formed
of solid walnut, carved in high relief, with a grand
carven canopy, from which depends a festooned drape of
brocade to match the window-hangings, and edged with
the same exquisite lace. The brocade is ivory-colored,
the design being clusters of pink roses and foliage. The
bed-spread is of the same material, only cut out in the
shape of diamonds, and appliqued with squares, triangles,
and circles of pieces of hand-made lace, a deep frill of
the same surrounding the whole. This superb bed-
spread was bought for 70 guineas. The linen, towels,
pillow-cases, &c., cost such a fabulous sum that readers
may not credit the fact that exquisite linen diaper, or
damask towels, with ten-inch lace at each end, cost £i
10s. to £7 10s. each, but anyone seeing and handling
them would know that it is the hand-made lace that gives
the value. One sheet, wider than the ordinary sheet, as
long, and bordered with Venetian point, was only 40
guineas, a second specimen being 23 guineas. Pillow-
slips, nearly all lace, are of equal value, according to size,
and the massive bedroom suite matches the bedstead,
being of richly carved solid walnut, while the ewers and
basins are of Bohemian glass, traced with gold.
The bed “ looks like the State beds in pictures of
kingly palaces.” Yet Brookman stands about 4ft.
llin. high, weighs (say) 104Ibs. Also, he carries a
trick walking-stick, into which the barman at the
Australia, when Brookman visits Sydney, pours
matutinally ninepenn’orth of brandy. “It holds
exactly ninepenn’orth,” says Brookman, proudly,
as he screws up the end of his curio.
In the early ’7o’s, G. Republican Dibba
occupied the first floor of premises in Pitt-street,
Sydney, the basement being occupied by a
holy pillar of the church and his six severely
proper clerks, who all taught in Sunday-
school. Among Dibbs’s frequent visitors
was the late John Robertson, and their
interviews always terminated much as
follows, for the benefit of the pious gen-
tleman below. When old Jack reached
the street-door he would pause, return
to the bottom of the stairs (as a man
who had just remembered something)
and shout, “Dibbs! Dibbs, you !
Dibbs ! ” Promptly would thunder down-
stairs, “ Hallo, you old blank !
What the blank are you kicking
up that dash blank row about ? ”
Then the pious gentleman would
shudder and put his fingers in
his ears, and the six proper
young men would look appropri-
ately horrified while the cyclone
oelow would continue its storm of
highly-seasoned remarks and the
tempest above would nobly keep
up its end of the holiness. Finally,
with a tremendous bang of his office-
door, the good man on the base-
ment would signify his righteous
indignation, and old Jack would de-
part chuckling hugely, while Dibbs
guffawed up-stairs.
“ L.”: Truth is stranger than
fiction. In 1835, the writer’s uncle
—Oscar Jones, for the purposes of
this story—an officer in the Bri-
tish army, sold out and emi-
grated, without taking the
trouble to mention where he
was going. The Earth swal-
lowed him up till 1887, in
which year I was taken down
a mine at Temora, N.S.W.,
sight-seeing. At the end of a
long “ drive,” my guide introduced
me to the overseer, who was superintending
some work. “Jones,” said he, “do you spell
it ‘ J.O.N.E.S. ?’ Why, that was my grand-
father’s name.” And it turned out that the grand-
father was the traditional lost Oscar Jones of our
family, who had departed in ’35.
Bert Levy sends The Bulletin some re-
collections of George Gordon, scene-painter and
White Man: —
I spent seven years at the side of George Gordon on
the painting-frame—years teeming with the recollections
of the kindly instructor and true friend. Gordon seemed
to consider every man in the theatre —actor, super.,
stage-hand, or splodger—as under his special care, and
in case of trouble he would inquire all about it; then no
stone would be left unturned to do something for the
sufferer. He was an extremely nervous and sensitive
man, and, as already mentioned in The Bulletin, fainted
on his first (and last) visit to a dissecting-room, and there-
after stuck strictly to landscape. Gordon could not hurt
any living thing, and, calling the splodger (assistant) one
day to remove a big beetle from his palette, was careful
to add, “ But, mind, don’t kill it!” Another time there
strayed into the painting-room a miserable creature of a
cat, Which some of the boys in the property-room had
been “gilding and decorating.” Poor Gordon was
greatly concerned, and, after having the creature washed
and warmed at the stove, sent out to get milk for it.
Gordon had a great admiration for his friend, Phil May,
and whenever Phil came to Melbourne he would sit for
hours in the painting-loft, yarning. Now and again he
would illustrate a remark with the charcoal on any clear
space of the back-cloth Gordon was painting, and Gordon
would paint all around Phil’s sketch before reluctantly
painting over it. I recollect Gordon painting the corn-
field for the first act of “ Dorothy,” and when he knocked
off for the night, the sky was just laid in, and on the
horizon were a sketch of Premier Parkes, a caricature of
Grattan Riggs, and some wonderfully funny Hebrew faces.
Next day, when Gordon came to lay-in the distance,
he could hardly bring himself to obliterate Phil’s work.
Phil was in the painting-room with Gordon on the night
of Fred Leslie’s first appearance in Australia at the Prin-
cess’. He saw Leslie from the flies, and sketched on the
painting-room wall a life-size head. Gordon treasured
that head-sketch for years, and every time the. theatre
was being cleaned he would carefully instruct the
white - washers to circumnavigate May’s sketch.
Two years ago, when the drawing was getting
faint, Gordon took ap impression of it with sized-paper.
Gordon was full of anecdotes of Irving, Bancroft, Toole,
Leighton, Millais, &c. In fact, he was a mine of informa-
tion about art and artists. A Sunday morning’s stroll
with him through the Botanical Gardens was a treat. He
would stop and go into ecstasies over each little view,
and would enthuse, perhaps for minutes, over an old grey
rock. “A whole day’s study in that,” he would say. As
his pupil, I used to make studies of still-life articles
PARADISE PASTIMES.
A quiet game of halo-quoits on Saturday evening.
about the paint-room, but, though I tried hard to please
him, I could never get more out of him than “ Ah ! that's
a little better till one day I ventured to ask if he
couldn’t say something stronger. “Well,” he replied
humorously, “if I say ‘good,’ ‘beautiful,’ or ‘grand,’
what is there left for me to say to AlmaTadema, Bunie
Jones, or Peter Graham ?” He liked his little joke always,
and once, when I was making a study of some fruit, he
found fault with my color. “My boy,” he said, “watch
nature. Don’t take your eyes off nature, and paint only
what you see.” Then, unknown to me, he covered the
fruit with an apron, and I worked on for half-an-hour
before I found it out. While painting, Gordon was seldom
without a cigarette in his mouth, and, as he kept putting
them down after a few puffs and lighting fresh ones, his
leavings used to pretty well keep some of the hands in
smoke. Gordon’s strong point in painting was old-
English architecture, and he positively loved to handle a
fine old interior. Some of his miniature models of stage-
settings are works of art in themselves, and I have often
marvelled at his modelling, say, an old village street,
with bits of coal for rocks, little cardboard bridges, and a
little track of flake-white, altering things again and
again, and lingering over his work far into the night.
George Gordon—to sum up—loved everybody and every-
thing—except whistling, and the modern suburban villa
with bow-windows.
SomeN.S.W. ancients still gazing at the
passing show : Mrs. Carey, of Warra Warra, near
Collector, born in Parramatta, April 21, 1801;

July 25 was the 37th anniversary of the
completion of McDouall Stuart’s historic acr<">-
the-continent ride; on that day he got with Ins
party to the Indian Ocean. Though Stuart, w lib
stands right in the front rank of explorers ai y
where, died as long ago as ’66, only three of 'the
nine who formed his party are dead. P. W.
Thring (second assistant), W. P. Auld, Steph-n
King, Heath Nash, and John McGorrery still
live in South Australia; and schoolmaster Bil-
liott lately went to England. They were tough
fellows whom Stuart chose ; partly on that ac-
count he never lost a life. And, by the way, he
didn’t use a camel either.
A martyr to principle (writes a Bulletin
correspondent) is a Pentridge prisoner who once
starred with the Salvarmy as a reformed criminal.
He is the. only professed atheist on the prison
books and passes the church hour locked up in his
cell, thus missing the highly-valued weekly relaxa-
tion of lifting up the voice in hymns. As the
Reading bard sung:—
We banged the tins and bawled the hymns
And polished up the rails.
Noise relieves prison-tension, and the Pentridge
atheist will probably end in a fit of yelling and
battering his cell-furniture. In which event his
last state will be worse than his first!

“ 0.P.” : Appalling is the indifference of
the Australian station-hand about the abuse to
which his unfortunate stomach is subjected. He
has to gorge tasteless, flabby boiled mutton (with
cancer, hydatids, &0., thrown in) three times a
day; the flour from which his bread is made is
often weevily and rotten through long exposure to
heat; his tea and sugar are largely composed of
black lead and bone-dust; and the black, rank
“ tobacco ” that he smokes incessantly is an abso-
lute horror. Add to these the attentions of the
mercurial pill-man, of the “ poisoner ” or amateur
oook, and of the other poisoner who lives in a tin
house at the cross-roads, and cease to wonder at
the intemperance, disease, profanity, and insanity
that are so inseparable from bandy legs, peeled
nose, and the smell of yolk. These remarks also
Miss AMY CASTLES.
Alba Studios, photo., Sydney.
apply in a less degree to Australians generally.
How are we going to get on without stomachs,
and when will our bleeding country learn to cook
and be sensible ?

“C.JVI.A.” : My crow and cat are dining.
Crow gathers all the pieces of meat his beak will
hold, till only one remains. Felis has this, and
Corres, after eyeing it greedily, runs to fern-box
close by, plants what he has, stalks backhand
pinches cat’s tail till she surrenders her piece,
which crow marches off to fern-box to plant also.
Finds the other lot gone (I had taken it), goes
back to cat, and gives her an unmerciful “doing.”
That crow has a fine sense of what is due to Pro-
perty.
r J * * * *
“ Tongarra ” : 7?e quickness of aboriginal
children (8., 8/7/’99). I taught once, in a border
aboriginal school, about 80 children, ranging from
pure black, through copper, brown, and amber, to
white. Found the full-blacks good triers but
with no capacity, and the half-castes, though on
the whole smart, were curiously weak in memory-
work. Their minds seemed too volatile to be re-
tentive. They were particularly good at writing
and drawing, while in mental arithmetic they
were ahead of the average white child. On one
occasion Mr. Bruford (the Vic. financial authority),
after hearing a class put through some rapid
mental calculations, remarked to me that he had
tried to follow the maze of figures himself, but was
beaten by the pace. The girls were, as a rule,
quicker than the boys. The head-teacher of this
school, by the way, is a Hindoo, and olever, well-
educated, and well-mannered at that.

“F.W.K.”: Ever go fishing for wood?
Not in water, but in air. When there’s no wood
on the ground, and no axe with which to “down”
it, you must get your whaling line, fix on a sar-
dine-tin or stone for a sinker, and heave line over
a small, dry limb ; then walk round until you get
it well tangled above—pull, and down comes your
“ fish.” Arizona Ike is simply notin it with some
of the wood-lassoers in the big river-bends.
“0.P.”: “Ashton Murphy’s sketch,
“ Pulling out bogged sheep,” is not so absurd as
might be thought; in faot, the idea has much to
recommend it, when the harness is away on loan,
or you haven’t any harness-horse. Any horse will
pull a small tail-load, such as boughs for a sheep-
brake, bag of dead wood, &c. Every horse, like
every man, has an inborn dislike for work—
[Nonsense. Lots of men have an inborn love of
work.] —and when strained solely from behind
goes the other way in consequence. With the
collar there is too much “ pressing him to stay,”
as it were. The rope must be tied securely about
the middle joint of the tail, and no more than
half a load attempted. As to cruelty, I have not
been able to ascertain what the horse thinks
about it.

“Dominie”: The growing practice of
abolishing the hour-and-half midday recess in
some N.S.W. country public-schools has only to
be vigorously assailed by local medical men to
make the Dept, interfere. The chief-inspector,
for one, is strongly opposed to all attempts to
shorten pupils’ recess. Let him be medically ap-
proached on the matter, and the grievance will be
soon redressed. School-children in our climate
need abundance of fresh air and time to masticate
their food thoroughly. You should see my rosy-
cheeked young ruffians.
In the old days, when
D station was renowned
for its brumby-runs, the
best horsemen of the dis-
trict would gather there,
honest and dishonest, un-
wanted and “ wanted ”
latter sure of welcome on
arrival and assistance on de-
parture, if necessary. Once,
when a big run was in an-
ticipation, Spifkins, the lo-
cal sub-inspector of police,
determined on a haul. He
had many “wants” to
satisfy, and thought the
time propitious. Accord-
ingly, on the evening of the
meet, he rode into camp
with three troopers, but the
manager, knowing his man,
passed the word : “O.K.—
Don’t leave.” There was a
good supply of rum on hand,
and it was kept moving in
the sub’s vicinity with such
vigor that presently ho geni-
ally d d every official
duty “until the morning.”
That was enough. A man
was promptly told-off to
“keep it up to him,” and
when, 48 hours later, Spif-
kins “came to the surface,”
he was told many things
that he didn’t quite swal-
low. Still, he was half-con-
vinced that he had com-
mitted 17 different crimes
while in a blissful state of
grog, and that nearly all the
“ wanteds ” had seen him
doing them. Anyhow, there
was no help for it—his birds
were flown. Sub-inspector
S. drank himself to death at
a “shanty,” whilst osten-
sibly enquiring into the
death of a subordinate, who had perir jed of
many bootfuls of snakes at the same precic as hole.

“Tramp” : Trudging Riverina last sum-
mer, got talking to a grizzled old carrier, who told
me that, though he had lived within 40 miles of
Albury all his life, he had only once ridden in a
train —from Albury to racecourse —and then was
in such mortal fear of smash-up that he returned
“ per boot.”
* * *
The most comprehensive
cattle run in Rougemont’s country,
N.W. Australia, sweeps in six
million acres ; there is another of
four millions; and Conor, the
Westralian M.P. for the score
or so of Kimberley electors, re-
presents a firm which holds 3g
million acres. Part of Conor’s
holding is in Westralia and the
rest in the N. Territory ; and,
in consequence of Bigjohn’s
tick regulations, cattle may
not now be shifted from one
part of the run to the other.
So Conor, who has hitherto
dealt only with Westralian
politicians, comes over to get
concessions from S.A. —jetties
constructed, and so on. As to
tick, he says there is plenty up
there (in “ the finest cattle
country in Australia ”), but it
doesn’t do any harm.
“ Traralgon ” : Was on
Smith’s-Curse line t’other day.
Travelled in guard’s van. Noticed the train
slowing down without apparent reason. Asked
why. Guard glanced out the window. “Thought
so,” he said. “Steve Murphy is driving to-
day ! This is his paddock. He’s counting the
bullocks ! ”

“0.P.”: “Steff” (B. 24/6/’99), in alleging
that Nelson (M.L.) residents won’t argue, strikes
a peculiarity of Australians generally. The
“ bloke,” as a rule, strongly resents being differed
from, and the rest of him will generally agree, no
matter how absurd the statements, just as if No. 1
was a roaring lunatic, t-hould one listener, how-
ever, after due consideration, go so far as to ex-
press dissent, then the whole crowd will promptly
endorse him also, as if he was lunatic No. 2. Pecu-
liarity is due, I think, to the “ bloke’s ” general
tired desire to avoid any extra mental exertion.

both the benifit av the doubt, an ’ split the diff'r-
ence. Vardict for plaintiff, twinty shillins.
Honors aisy—I mane, divide the costs, if the
law'll allow yez.”

Things are quiet in far Cooktown,
The days are dull and cold,
The festive goat has ceased to skip
Upon the mountain bold.
The Chinamen who run the show
Are tired, and broke, and weak ;
The pub. has got its shutters up—
The barmaid’s in the creek.
No more the sweet, rich scent of rum
Will roll across the plain ;
For cash is gone, and tick is done.
The red blind glares in vain.
A busy city by the sea
Has sunk and settled down,
A goat, two Chinkies and a dog
Now own the blanky town.

One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia —are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Mud gee (N.S.
Wales), lately
had a slice of
luck such as
doesn’t often fall
to the lot of even
the Mudgee Cox-
es. Finding that
he couldn’t ex-
tract a loaded
cartridge from
the chamber of
his Winchester,
he proceeded to
batter it out from
the muzzle-end,
meanwhile hold-
ing the rifle care-
fully trained on
to his bump of
indi vi d uality.
The cartridge ex-
ploded, blowing
the base off the
shell and scorch-
ing the contents
of the magazine ;
but neither bul-
let nor rod came
out through the
muzzle—in
they didn’t come
out at all, and at
time of writing
still hold each
other jammed in
the barrel. The
rod was fortu-
nately inserted
small end down.

Dear Bulle-
tin. —Th e fol low-
ing may help your
“ forlorn ” Papu-
an correspondent
as regards the
prophylactic vir-
tues of fatty food
in beri-beri. Beri-
beri having brok-
en out among the
prisoners (fed al-
most entirely on
rice) in a French
colonial peniten-
tiary, the doctors,
after hundreds
had died, tried the addition of pork to the diet, and
found that the epidemic was immediately checked.
Again, on a vessel conveying coolies from Pondi-
cherry to Martinique beri-beri showed itself only
amongst the Mahometans, whose diet was of rice
and fish. All those on board who consumed
grease (in any form) escaped. Pap.

“Te Bum” : “Pukaki” (B. 24/6/’99) is
wrong about an incision in the bark of the matai-
tree bringing forth a “beer,” and also about same
being sweetish. Not every matai-tree contains
this beer, and I have always had to get near the
heart of the tree with axe or auger to reach this
juice, which will sometimes squirt out to a dis-
tance of yards. It is a good thirst-allayer and
tastes not unlike alum. Bushmen often sweeten
it with sugar, as had probably been done with
what “Pukaki” tasted.
* * * ♦
“ Moa ” : Yes, “ Pukaki ” (B. 24/6/’99), I
have heard of the matai beer, and have drunk it,
but never tasted your amber-colored sap. Have
spent 15 |years in M.L. bush, and always found
matai beer in “shake ” of tree.

“ A.A.B. ” : In Feb., ’96, N.S.W. Abori-
gines Board placed a party of Bushfield blacks
upon a 280-acre block near Bundarra so that
they might work the land to support them-
selves. In June, ’99, 1J acres had been partly
cleared, and 20 cabbage plants were in their
childhood. “ Garden ” fence had been con-
sumed as firewood. Plough and harrow rest-
ing just where left by carrier on arrival.
Seed-wheat, breeding-hens, and “ lone lorn
milch cow all eaten to celebrate turning of
first sod, and, if progress isn’t reported for
another three years, the remaining members
of the Bushfield Aboriginal Clearing Co.
will probably be found in Central Queens-
land killing ’possums with a ploughshare or
knocking one another’s heads in with harrow-
spikes.

N.S.W. eagle for “ spread” was recently shot
at Barrengarry. Tip to tip, 126 inches.
“D.G.S.”: Be snakes (“F.P.,”8. 8/7/’99).
Five months' is not an extraordinary time for a
snake to go foodless. Under ordinary climatic
conditions, most of our snakes have perforce to
do without food for four or five months. Recently
kept a “ tiger,” a “ brown,” and two “ diamonds,
which remained respectively seven, nine, ten, and
eleven months without food. At the end of
eleven months the smaller of the “diamonds
(2ft. long) received a mouse, after which it had
nothing more till date of death, four months after.
Also, snakes do not invariably swallow their prey
head-first (“C.F.”B. 8/7/’99), though they usually
do so. My larger “diamond” had his mouth
torn several times through taking in pigeons
wrong end foremost.
ON THEIR HONEYMOON IN THE WILD WEST.
Brisbane has lately been inundated with
a small flood of bogus silver coins, and the effect
on church collections has been simply disastrous.
Strange that orthodox people should actually
fancy they can thus cheat the Almighty. It re-
calls the Chinese custom of putting spurious coins
instead of genuine ones into the coffins of the dead
—in the belief that the hapless corpse won’t know
the difference.
At Whangamumu, M.L., where a. Captain
Cook catches whales by stretching a titanic net
across a narrow strait between an island and the
main land, nine whales have already been
slaughtered this season. The fish have, according
to Maori tradition, used this strait from time
immemorial in their periodical journeys from
north to south, being attracted to it, it is pre-
sumed, by the facilities its rocky shores afford for
relieving their bodies of barnacles. The strait is
so narrow that when once in, the monsters cannot
turn ; the net, buoyed with barrels, baulks them
and they are speedily killed with harpoon and
lance. Near Whangamumu is the site of an
ancient Maori battlefield. The place is a strict
tapu and the skeletons of over 100 slaughtered
warriors lie just as they fell under the mere
stroke and spear-thrust.

Oscar Girard, the brave and clever come-
dian who died at Adelaide last week, was in
wretch d health when he landed in Australia, but
the lung-hemorrhage, which caused his death,
was brought on through a chill caught while
THE LATE OSCAR GIRARD.
Photo., Talma, Melbourne.
travelling between Brisbane and Sydney. Girard
only played twice in Adelaide, and then almost
broke down on the stage. His last words to the
publio oonsisted of the announcement that his
“pipes were blocked up, and the plumber would
have to be called in.” From the first the doctors
regarded his case as hopeless. No man ever
made a pluckier fight for life.
A DEPARTING DIRGE.
Girls in town and boys out back,
I’ve rolled up my little pack,
And on June’s chill wintry gales
Sail from pleasant New South Wales.
Ere I go—a doggerel song
To bid the whole caboose “ So-long 1"
Saddle-gear and horses sold—
Fetched but scanty stock of gold—
Scanty 11 yet the whole d d lot
Publicans and Flossies got.
Since I in this country landed
Ne’er before was I so “ stranded.”
Now I’m leaving Sydney’s shore
Harder up than e’er before ;
A keen appetite I feel
To taste a bit o’ British veal ;
And let’s trust, across the foam
They have a fatted calf at home.
From duns and debts (once safe on board)
Pray deliver me, oh Lord 1
Here’s the burden of my song :
“ Good-bye, old girl ! Old chap, So-long! ”
Hardest loss of all I find
To leave the good old horse behind.
So-long, “ Cavalier 1 ”
June, ’99.

WOMAN.

You’re a “ necessary evil.”


London, June 16.—Hospital Sunday witnessed a new device for lightening the
sorrows and comforting the souls of sufferers in hospitals.
'Hie electrophone has been fitted to some of the London hospitals.
Last Sunday the Chelsea Hospital for Women and the Dreadnought Seamen’s
Hospital at Greenwich were able to listen to the services being held at some of our
leading places of worship.
Nor was the privilege confined to those sound in limb, for a special arrange-
ment keeps the hearing-cups in position close to the ears, and thus enables
those with broken arms or otherwise unable to move to enjoy the elec-
trophone. There was, of course, a choice of creeds, including
Church of England, Wesleyan, Presbyterian and Jewish.
Thus Canon Fleming was able to sav
belore beginning his sermon : “ While I
am speaking for the sick and suffer-
ing, some of them are listening.”
And while he was speaking to the
listening most of them were suffering.

Wellington, Wednesday.—The remarkable decrease in the birth-rate is
Having an effect on the schools in Otago. A number of teachers have reported that
there seems to be no prospect of an increased attendance for s,ome years, as there
are no young children in their districts, and very few births are reported. In 12
schools the board Las dismissed a teacher from each, and seven others have been
notiiied that the staff will be reduced if there is-no increase in the attendance.”

‘Dreamer” : In a current account at a local
bank I had, previous to 30th Junelast, Is. at my credit. I
decided to close the account some days ago, but the
manager informed me it would cost 9s. additional to do so.
ss for keeping the account and ss. for closing it. Also,
that if I did not close it, it would run on indefinitely and
increase 10s. yearly. I did not close, and I invite opinions,
as to whether the bank can legally recover 10s. yearly if I
cease to do business with it.
* » * *
Ed. Bulletin. — In a recent issue you pub-
lished two paragraphs re the cyanide patent, both of which
begin with a garbled statement. The first states that the
action of the N.S.W. Examiner of Patents in refusing the
Ovanide Co.’s application for permission to amend brings.
N.S.W. into line with Victoria. This is the reverse of
fact, for in Victoria the permission stands as granted by
the verdict of a majority of the final deciding local
authority, the Full Court, while in N.S.W. permission,
has been refused by the administrator of the Patents
Department, the deciding authority in the first in-
stance. It is to be hoped that all obstructions
to the path of the patentees in an appeal to the
Privy Council will be removed in N. S. Wales. In
W.A., Q., and Tas., the amendment of the patent has
been allowed. As to the Transvaal, it ought to be said
that a majority of the Supreme Court disallowed the
amendment, and to obtain even a minority there in such
an issue was a moral victory for the Uitlanders. Again,
it is not pretty generally admitted that the knowledge of
cyanogen’s power to separate gold was common property
fifty years ago. No such thing has ever been admitted.
It was known that cyanogen would dissolve gold, and that
solutions of gold in cyanogen were commonly used in
electrolysis as in the process of plating. No one, until
M‘Arthur and Forrest came along, ever suggested that
cyanogen solutions would separate or disentangle gold
from its ores. Two or three little inventors had suggested
that cyanide and something else might be put in solution
for the purpose, but their ideas were never seriously con-
sidered. M‘Arthur and Forrest were not only the first to
patent the practical method, but the first to absolutely
separate or disentangle gold from its ores by means of
cyanide solutions. — Fred. Walsh.
[The Bulletin is well aware there is no end to
discussion on this matter, particularly among interested
parties. Mr. Walsh admits that it was known before the
Macarthur-Forrest patent that cyanogen would dissolve
gold, and that it was commonly used for that purpose ;
but denies that.it was known that cyanogen would separate
gold. The distinction is a fine one, and the justification
of the distinction is a matter of opinion. Anyway, J. A.
Parkes, known as the inventor of a process for taking
silver from lead, gave good evidence to show he knew
cyanogen’s separating properties as far back as 1852.
Macarthur and Forrest are entitled to all the practical
credit of the invention ; but they didn’t discover the
germ-idea—they only applied it.]
* * * •
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. has notified
Clarence River growers that it will buy cane fit for cutting
before the end of 1901 under rates in present contract
provided that the Federal tariff—if in existence before
that period—shall ensure a duty of not less than £3 per
ton on sugar. In event of reduction of duty below £3,
price of cane will be also reduced 2s. per ton for each £1
of such reduction. No mention, however, is made of any
higher price for cane if the Federal tariff sugar-duty shall
be higher than the £3 per ton on which present contracts
are based, yet it is 20 to 1 that it will be higher. What
about an increase of 4s. per ton of cane if the i eaerai
sugar tariff is £5 a ton ?

Judge Conolly (M.L.) recently ruled that col-
lusive failure to pay calls, in order to enable a co. to go
into liquidation, was illegal. #
If, as would appear, the indefatigable Mr.
Cairnes, B.N.S.W. manager at Parramatta, has discovered
a method of perfectly preserving passion-fruit for oversea
export, another very considerable source of revenue has
been found for Australian orchardists.
• » * *
The B.H. mines’ timber-bills are small affairs
now compared with what they used to be when the
square-set system was alone employed. In 1891, £302,000
worth of timber was put on the mines. Last half-year,
though three times more ore was extracted from under-
ground, the total quantity imported to B.H. was valued
.at £41,085.
* * * *
The successful concentration of Broken Hill
sulphides and the establishment of a market for “ zincs”
have turned attention to some old silver-lead “ shows ” in
:S.A. —the province which, before Broken Hill, had most
success with silver. The cos. abandoned their claims
when they got to sulphides, though in places the silver
contents were above Barrier average. The most pro-
mising occurences are in the hills S.W. of Adelaide, close
to the Melb. railway line.
* * * *
Suoh fine concentrating work can now be done
that, in consequence of the rise in the price of copper,
tailings containing only 2 per cent, of metal are being
profitably worked at old Cornish mines.
A great check might be put upon the littering
of wild cats on the market if there were a law that vendors
should only be allowed ordinary shares (no cash), while
shares issued to the public should be preferential as to
■dividends, up to, say, 10 per cent. —and altogether pre-
ferential as to capital. In other words, vendors’ shares
would carry no divs. till the public’s shares had got their
10 per cent. Out of further profits, vendors would get up
to 10 per cent. —and, if there were any balance, all shares
would participate on equal terms. If the co. went into
liquidation, the public would get first whack at the assets.
Vendors should not get cash, because they claim so hea,vily,
as a rule, and swamp the property ; also because it relieves
them of all responsibility as to the future of the mine, or
as to the lies in the prospectus. If they want to realise,
they can sell their ordinary shares, and this means of
raising money will not interfere with the cash directly
raised by the public for working the mine or whatever it
is. The system would be a real test of the bona-fides of
vendors. The man who swears that his property is a sure
fortune, but won’t take a share in the fortune in payment,
would simply be knocked out.
* * * *
Boulder Brookman’s gift of =£lo,ooo to Adelaide
School of Mines had unexpected results. It set men
thinking of the time, only a very few years back, when
Brookman was handing sugar and jam over the counter,
and of the great things that have come to him through
being a “first robber”; and pious Adelaide has been
leaving home by the first train in order to lie in wait for
a hairy person with a slug sticking out of his pocket, so
that it might be a “first robber,” too. Everything that
has at any time been vaguely suspected of being a mine
has either been repegged or is being shepherded ; and the
gentleman who has been for the last two or three years
carrying vegetables round the suburbs blossoms once
more into “ Captain Tom Tresomething, the well-known
mining man,” or words to that effect. To one of which
“ Captains,” by the way, an awful thing happened lately.
An “ influential syndicate ” had sent him out after a
copper “show.” He wired next day—“ Found property.
Green carbonates showing in outcrop. Forwarding speci-
mens.” The specimens came, with the “ green carbon-
ates” considerably damaged—they consisted of a sun-
dried coating of moss 1
* * * *
“ Spotter ”: Notwithstanding the ill-fortune
English capitalists have experienced in the wild cats of
W.A. goldfields it is astonishing to find how they still
allow themselves to be gulled in connection with the
timber industry. The manner in which the timber mills
are managed (or mismanaged) is scarcely credible Dor
instance, a mill, recently erected employed 11 hands
at the benches, &c., and to guard the interests of the
capitalist the following: staff (management and clerical)
is engaged, viz., a managing director at £l5OO per annum,
a general manager at £6OO, a timekeeper at £lBO, a mill-
foreman at 4 200. For log-hauling two teams are em-
ployed, and for foiling two men, with a boss at a wage of
£lBO per annum to direct them. Take the actual workers
at the mill, their wages in 12 months total £l4OO, whilst
local management costs £2480 ! ! The truck system comes
to the rescue here, and only for the swindle thus worked
on the men few of the timber mills of W.A. could show
a profit.
* * * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents
owners will be advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not be
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
We have examined the foregoing: Aggregate Balance-Sheet, made up to 30th June, 1899, and report that we find
the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances in the General Ledger at Head Office, and to the Balance-
Sheets furnished by the Branches ; these latter being certified to by the respective Managers and Accountants.
We have counted the cash, and examined the bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in on the 30t
ultimo and hereby certify that we have found them correct. The securities held at the Head Office ha\e, at the
request of the Directors, been examined by Messrs. Chambers, Bruce & M‘Nab, Solicitors to the Bank, and we have
seen their certificate to the effect that they are in order.
We have further to certify that in our opinion the said Aggregate Balance-Sheet is properly drawn up, and ex-
hibits a true and correct view of the Bank’s affairs as shown by the books on the 30th June, 1899.
Brisbane, 7th July, 1899.
GEO. FRED. SCOTT, 8.A., F.I.A.Q.
F. A. MULLER, F.F.I.A. (Vic.)

THIS company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring and working, by means of a bucket-
dredge of the latest and most approved type, a gold-mining area of 96 acres situated on the
Shoalhaven River, about six miles from Braidwood.
The property is held by virtue of an application for a Special Lease for gold-mining purposes,
made in terms of the Mining Act Further Amendmeut Act, 1884, the application being approved
and gazetted on 20th January, 1899. It embraces over a mile of the riverbed and beaches,
and varies from four to fifteen chains in width. Mr. Wilson, who had come over from New Zealand
for the purpose of securing a dredging proposition, selected the ground in June, 1898, as the best
available site on the Shoalhaven River.
The reports of Mr. C. E. Richardson, M-E., late manager of the Manorburn Gold Dredging Co.,
Ltd. of Alexandra, New Zealand, and of Mr. John W. H. Piper, C.E., together with a sketch plan
of the property proposed to be acquired, can be obtained at the office of the company.
