POLITICAL
POINTS
Some extremist might fetch up to date Dr.
Lang’s book on “ The Coming Event, Freedom
and Independence for Australia,” published in
London, 1870.
The first Colonial Secretary of N.S.W.,
appointed in 1825, drew a salary of <£2ooo a year.
And the country was poorer then than now. But
it was England that fixed the amount.
The total number of affirmative votes
cast for the Commonwealth Bill is now 335,000;
the noes only total 110,000. An awkward fact to
tack on to that petition to “ the foot of the
throne.”
By its new Electoral Bill, reducing the
Assembly to 32 and the Council to 16 members,
Tasmania takes the lead of the Australian pro-
vinces in framing its local legislation to stand
proportioned to Federal legislation.
The very fact of “ ten-bob ” Unmack
yapping against Federation should be sufficient
inducement to the whole of the bush-workers of
Queensland — i.e., those of them fortunate enough
to have a vote—to support the Bill baldheaded.
Notified in a recent Q. Govt. Gazette
that Premiers Braddon, Kingston, Reid, Turner
and Seddon have been appointed J.’sP. for Banana-
land, John Forrest being one already. Is this an
indication of how the Federal wind blows down
J. R. Dickson’s neck ?
J ames’s motion in favor of woman suffrage
in Westralia was enthusiastically carried in the
Assembly by 17 votes to 6. Westralia has the
smallest proportion of females in the Seven Pro-
vinces, and the female population is proportion-
ately smallest on the goldfields—for the ’tother-
sider generally left his “missus” behind in the
East—and largest in the old agricultural districts.
So female suffrage will greatly strengthen the old
Sandgroper minority when the inevitable redistri-
bution of seats comes along.
SIGNS OF THE DROUGHT.
VOICE FROM THE DOOR : “ Now, then, Alexander,
mar says yer ter ’urry hup out of that there
bath, and not to hempty the water, ’cause there’s
Mary Jane, Amelia, and little Willie to have
’em after you!”
Dalby (Q.) stock-growers lately complained
bitterly because £4OOO worth of stock was refused
admission into N.S.W. for fear of tick. What
sort of yell will there be if Queensland rejects
.r ederation, and finds all its stock permanently
walled out of all the southern provinces by an
inexorable tariff barrier ?
Only one bold attempt was made in Mel-
bourne to hold an anti-Bill meeting— at Colling-
wood, a Democratic centre which recorded 1100
votes against the Bill in 1898. Holman, M.L.A.
Higgins and Hancock addressed the gathering
and announced that the meeting denounced the
B'll by a 500 majority. At the poll the week
after Collingwood voted: aye, 2018 ; no, 231.
No less than 185 business men of New-
castle have petitioned against the Sydney morning
papers getting the gratis early-morning news-
paper train to the North that these rich, tough
mendicants have been begging for. Newcastle
considers that the Sydney papers should pay for
their own train if they want one.
At Johannesburg a meeting of thousands
of Uitlanders cheered wildly the suggestion that
British troops should be sent in to smash the
Boers. They were most eager to fight by proxy.
At Paarde Xraal thousands of Boers cheered the
declaration that they would go out and shoot and
be shot themselves, and do the fighting in their
own proper persons.
Sydney Sunday Shellback will evidently
have to carry its anti-Federal agitation “to the
foot of the throne” on its own, unless John
Haynes and David Storey can be counted in ; and
the said agitation is limping along very painfully.
At present it seems a matter for conjecture if it
will ever reach the foot of Bridge-street on its
way to Circular Quay.
Premier Forrest actually told W.A. Assem-
bly that “ he was awaiting the (Federation) Bills
from Queensland and Victoria to ascertain the
form in which it was placed before these Parlia-
ments. John seems unable to realise that all the
eastern Parliaments are dealing with exactly the
same Bill, and that it must be the same to a word
if there is going to be any Federation.
Premier Turner jumped with both feet on
the suggestion that Minister Taverner should
prolong his sojourn among the dukes and
duchesses by acting as Vic. Agent-General whilst.
Andrew Clarke is away from the London office.
The newly electro-plated Minister of Agriculture
has been instructed to tear himself away from
Britain’s proud nobility and return to work.
The Queensland Geebungs are persistently
barking that Federation means being swallowed
up by N.S.W., which is awkward in view of the
tons of printed statements by N.S. W. Geebungs
that the small-population provinces, of which
Queensland is one, were going to have a real good
time looting Sydney in particular, and all N.S. W.
generally. Verily the way of the snake-yarn man
is hard!
The total votes in the four provinces
which have now accepted the Commonwealth Bill
were, in 1898: For, 214,000; Against, 108,000.
This year the ayes have jumped to 335,000, while
the noes have remained practically stationary.
N.S.W. polled 34,000 more in favor of the Bill
Victoria 50,000, and S.A. 30,000. All in 12 short
months ; increases plainly indicative of the rising
of the tide of Australian sentiment.
It is quite an undertaking to use a
franked envelope in Wesfcralia. One re-
cently sent from Perth to Fremantle is
before us. In addition to “On Her
Majesty’s Service,” the following is
printed on the top right-hand corner :
“ This envelope can only be lawfully used
by Railways Departments (sic) on the
public business. The use of it by public
officials to avoid payment of postage on
postal matter of any kind is punishable,
and involves the letter being charged
double postage. The public are cautioned
against using it under any circumstances.”
Then there is a, large frank stamp with
some official’s initials, a rubber-stamp to
show the branch of the dept., then two
Perth P.O. stamps and, finally, a regis-
tered-letter stamp. .
Through the expiration of leases,
15,000,000 acres in N.S.W. Central Divi-
sion will revert to the State in June, 1900.
Of these, 1,000,000 are classed as first,
3,000,000 as second-class, and the remain-
der stands as poor. The first and second
lots should certainly go to agricultural set-
tlement, and perhaps a portion of the
remainder. Nor, despite some Parlia-
mentarians, should such a gigantic pro-
perty be left wholly to the disposal of an
incapable Crown' Minister like Mr.
Young The issues are big enough for the
entire Cabinet and Parliament.
Nelson declared the other day in
Bananaland Council that black-labor will
undoubtedly be blocked at an early date
by the Federal Parliament. Hence Fede-
ration will be an unmitigated ourse.
Certain alleged Q.- democratic organs
aver, on the other hand, that Federation
will signify the continuance of black-labor. Hence
Federation will be doubly an unmitigated curse.
Minister Chataway’s organ in Mackay denounces
Federation. Chataway himself now does the
double shuffle, and says he doesn’t mind it so very
much, after all. Public feeling in was
too much for him.
Not pleasant for democratic S.A. to reflect
that Bigjohn Forrest defends, or at least palliates
the unequal representation of Westralian elector-
ates by references to S.A. conditions. He draws
a deputation’s attention to the fact that East
Torrens has 14,000 electors and the Northern Ter-
ritory 900, and yet, he remarks, nobody screams at
Kingston. But he merely shows thereby that
Kingston should be screamed at.
The utterly unexpected record Federal-
vote in Victoria was a facer for Melb. Age. That
paper gave no help to the Bill, and, beyond a few
references to the meetings, took but little notice
of the campaign. On the eve of the referendum a
message was sent from the Age office to Sydney
Telegraph that it was not expected the polling
would be as heavy as last year. Therefore the
Age, which never really sympathised with Fede-
ration, and was dragged along at the heels of the
Australian Natives’ Association, can take no part
in the rejoicings over the result.
A curse from Far North :
That naked hypocrite and unparalleled liar, Brisbane
Telegraph, keeps roaring to its readers that the Com-
monwealth Bill is a ghoulish Tory measure framed bv
resplendent boodlers to rob the horny-handed of his
birthright and liberty. Now—to reiterate for the mil-
lionth time—the Bill makes both houses of Parliament
elective on a one-man -one-vote franchise ; gives every
man a chance of political distinction—or the other thing
—by paying members of both Houses, and recognises the
democratic principle of majority rule. And the loath-
some and illiterate Telegraph is the bulwark of pluto-
cracy and the plural vote ; and audaciously advocates the
abolition of payment of members and invariably gets mad
at the mere mention of the horrid Labor-partv. If the
democracy of Piebaidland follows the Telegraph’s advice
it richly deserves whatever happens to it.
TRUE GREATNESS.
S HE: “ The lines of this hand show that you have
never done anything truly remarkable or note-
worthy to make your name ring throughout
the land.”
FARMER BROWN (proudly): “ You’re clean out of
it, mum—clean out of it. I took special prize
for finest pumpkins at the Big Cow Flat
Show!”
Moran, M.L.A., solemnly warns W.A.
not to federate because “as soon as the duties and
bounties were swept away the Collie (W.A.) coal
might find it a hard job to compete with the New-
castle article.” Moran, to all appearance, really
doesn’t know that his province has no coal duty or
coal bounty to sweep away; and the Collie coal
on the spot can beat the coal that has to pay 2000
miles of sea-freight by ss. in the ton any day.
Collie coal would knock Newcastle out of the
W.A. market in a week if the ’Groper had energy
enough to dig it out.
The return of Henry Turley as Federal
and Labor candidate for South Brisbane (Q.) came
as a sickening surprise to the anti-Federalists in
the Q. metropolis. Jones, the Ministerial and
parochial aspirant, was most lavishly backed by
anti-Feaeral funds, and the electorate was de-
luged in his interests with panic pamphlets and
plaintive appeals to the working men to save
themselves from destruction. The deranged
Telegraph backed Jones, and all the provincial-
ists within a radius of a hundred miles were quar-
tered in the electorate, and barracked night and
day for their man. And the result is that the
head-quarters of anti-Federalism in Q. has voted
Federation and taken a Federal Labor-man in
preference to an anti-Fed. Ministerialist.
Hugh Nelson, who used to gibe at the
demand for Separation, and say that such towns
as Townsville and Rockhampton oughtn’t to exist
at all, is stricken with a grievous fit of sympathy
for the lot of the hapless Centralians who are
going to be blighted by Federation. The ex-
Premier draws the attention of Central-district
folks to the fact that their 57,000 inhabitants will
only entitle them to one member and a fraction in
the Federal Lower House —whence all manner of
trouble is predicted. The Centralians, however,
may be comforted by the fact that every other
57,000 folk throughout the length and breadth of
Australia are afflicted with precisely the same sort
of grievance. Nelson’s great alleged grief is that
Central Queensland will get just the same repre-
sentation in the Federal Lower House as any
other place of equal population.
The victory of Turley (Labor-Federation-
ist) for South Brisbane in the first place showed
that the Q. Labor folks vote solidly for their man,
be he Federationist or otherwise. Secondly, it
was a terrible slap in the face for the S. Brisbane
anti-Federalists, who were confident of the easiest
of victories. Then, again, the contest provided a
magnificent joke. Turley protested against Rock-
hampton being appointed a polling-place for S.
Brisbane. The protest was disregarded. At the
close of the local poll each man had received ex-
actly 1168 votes. Everybody was waiting in
breathless expectation for the outside returns.
Suddenly a telegram arrived—nine votes for Tur-
ley from Rockhampton; none for his opponent.
And the Government went home, tore its raiment,
put ashes on its head, and cursed the property-
vote unto seventy thousand times seven.
Carruthers, N.S.W.’s new Treasurer, made
an unspeakable show of himself with his com-
plaint that the Auditor-General “was not right
in reviving these old deficits of years gone by and
publishing them to the colony as a new fact.’’
The Auditor-General, in his annual report,
always states what the total deficit amounts to up
to date ; he couldn’t give a true account of the
finances if he didn’t, and it isn’t a new fact, but
an old, regular fact of long standing. The real
trouble is, that this independent and non-party
authority makes the deficit a great deal larger
than the Reid .Govt., in its anxiety to whitewash
its own financial methods, has always represented,
and he shows it to be really larger now than when
Reid took office. Treas. Carruthers apparently
has a grievance because the A.-G. doesn’t sup-
press facts which look bad for the Reid Ministry.
Premier Forrest’s proposed tax of Is. in th&
L on Westrahan dividends moved Western Mail

be the absolutely unheard-of amount, except in

metropolitan paper probably never heard that
Queensland and Tasmania have had the Is. in the £

(not only on dividends but also on sums carried to.

property S.A. charges Is. in the £ and Victoria
Is. 4d. in the £ income-tax. Over in the West
they actually fancy that Forrest invented tl.o
dividend-tax.
Dear Bulletin.— Gympie (Q.) Truth is
against the present Commonwealth Bill because
it it is accepted by a referendum taken on the pre-
sent restricted Queensland franchise “it will im-
pose a constitution on tens of thousands who can
have no voice in accepting or rejecting.” Yet
suppose a Queensland Parliament (necessarily
elected on the present restricted franchise) passed
a new Queensland Constitution making both
Mouses elective by one-adult-one-vote—that would
also impose on these tens of thousands a constitu-
tion which they would have no voice in accepting
or rejecting. If you give woman the franchise
■^i? U on her a new political system which
she has no voice in accepting or rejecting. The
Gympie paper’s logic is that a person who hasn't a
vote now shouldn’t get a vote unless he had a
vote before he got a vote so that he could say bv
his vote whether he wanted a vote or aot.—T.
Salt.
Speaker Abbott regards it as a sign of tliG
degeneration of NS. W. Parliament that such a
term as “ damned liar ” should have found its way
into Hansard. But Hansard is supposed to be
an accurate record of what goes on in Parliament,
and if Speaker Abbott has let the Assembly drift
into such chaos that members call each other
damned liars without fear of consequences, then the
fact ought to be officially reported. The Speaker
also complains that the habit of interjection has
gone so far that last session one member was
interrupted 47 times in 23 minutes. Hansard
reports that sort of thing also—and quite rightly
—to let Speaker .Abbott see what things have
drifted to under his control. Hansard ought to
go further, and report what members do as well
as what they say. “ Here the hon. member for
Boggabri shook his unclean fist at the hon. mem-
ber for Boggabilla ” mightn’t look well, but it
would at least make the record more accurate.
“CASTLES IN THE AIR.”
“ Buggins ” writes from Perth (W.A.)
Your correspondent, “ T’othersider’s,” remarks may be
permitted to pass as approximately correct, with the
exception of what he says about Vosper. Re this noto-
riety-hunting individual we are assured that he is “doing
a little harm and very much good for the Commonwealth
Bill.” Well, let’s see ? After posing for some time as an
out-and-out champion of the Bill, the erratic owner of
the long hair suddenly confronted the public with a
lightning change into the character of a conditional
Federationist. It is this individual, and not our
native Groper Government, who is responsible for
those demands of a trans-continental railway to
Freeman tie, a dock, and other parish-pump details.
Yosper’s brilliant idea of obtaining these is by petition-
ing the Imperial Parliament to insert the necessary
clauses in the Bill when it goes to 'England! Of course,
all this was right into the hands of the local “ antis”
who welcomed Yosper into their camp with much jubila-
tion. There he remains, and now each issue of his self-
advertisement—the Sunday Times— is full of labored
attempts to justify his position. The cause of Vosper's
break-away from the Federal party is simply offended
vanity. He aimed at being chosen leader of the move-
ment, and when he found his long hair wasn't going to
stream in the van, his colossal self-conceit was so hurt
that he at once set about doing his best to wreck the
movement. He will have to reckon, however, with his
goldfields constituents, who are ardent federationists to
a man, and who, though somewhat nonplussed for the
present by the vagaries of their representative, are not
likely to stand by and see him play into the hands of
their natural enemies—the old Gropers. Vosper’s latest
move was to induce Labor-member Thomas, of Broken
Hill, to come to W.A. to try and prejudice the workers-,
against Federation. However, as the defects in the Com-
monwealth Bill, according to Thomas, are precisely those
concessions to the smaller States which favor Westralia,
his objections are not likely to carry much weight here.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The football-match, England v. N.S.W., on
Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday did not reach the
Standard anticipated, nor was the attendance (14,000) tip
to expectations. Local team played one short during
three-parts of the game, throutrh Galloway retiring with
a badly-injured collar-bone. The match was compara-
tively tame, ami lacked speed. Little more than 15min.
after starting Barton (.V.S.\V.) secured a try, and Spragg
converted : 5-nil. Half-time saw no change. The Eng-
lishmen shone during second-half, and made things hmn
by forcing matters. From a penalty kick, just outside
25, Adamson (Eng.), smin. after resumption, notched 3.
Subsequently N.S. W. was atrain penalised (same player,
O’Donnell, beimr in fault), and 3 more points were re-
corded, though this goal should not have been allowed
through the kicker, Adamson, placing the hall. With
the figures England 6, N.S.W. 5, play waxed warm for a
while till Swannel (Eng.) gained a hard-fought-for try,
and Adamson converted, making totals England 11,
N.S.W. 5. The struggle ceased a little later.' English
backs and N.S.W. forwards did the best work. Britisher
Adamson s goal-kicking was a revelation. Ail three
goals were exceedingly difficult, hut every one went as
accurately over the centre as if fired from a gun, just
skimming the bar in each instance. The visitors have
improved a heap since last in Sydney, and will probably
be still better in the next two test-matches.
* * * •
Their brilliant back division won the Britishers
the game against N.S.W. on Saturday, and apparently
no amount of experience will convince the selectors that
what is wanted in the Australian teams are some fast for-
wards able to swoop flown on said hacks and bustle them
a bit. Men like Nicholls and Bucher can, if not flurried,
always gain 50 or 60.vd. hv a kick, and as there was no
one to flurry them in the N.S.VV. team, they were able to
keep the play well down on the opposite line. And if you
keep the play there the chances to score come t hemselves.
Following-np is a heart-breaking game, but it is a game
that wins a lot of matches.
* • m m
The. long-kicking which has been such a feature
of the English Rugby team during its tour recalls the im-
mense superiority of the local kicking in the Australian
game. . In Melbourne and Adelaide nearly every senior
plater is good for a kick of 60yds. or more on a still day,
anti to one used to the Australian game, the kicking of
average Rugby footballers seems execrable.
* * • •
Young Victor Trumper, in the match against
Sussex last week, earned lasting fame, and pleased thou-
sands of Australian admirers, by topping with 300 not
£!JL Mur,loch ’ s rec °id f°r an Australian in England
(286) notched 17 years ago. Notable that both feats were
accomplished on Brighton ground, which favors quick
run-getting. Trumper has now passed his thousand and
Nobles aggregate (1238), scoring 1279 for 30 completed
innings.
• * * •
IVTelb. Argus, like other patriotic journals, got
itself into a.fangle last week re the heavy scoring against
the Australian Eleven. Said the Aimes man on Saturday t
If our bowling alone were off, our batsmen, having so
much the best of the three-figure scores earlier in the
summer, should at least he holding their own now, I
fear that moans general staleness, from which batsmen,
bowlers, and fieldsmen alike are suffering.'’ It didn’t
need an. Australian onslaught on the Sussex bowling to
make this look ridiculous. Bowler Jones has been rested
from time to time all through the season, and howler
Noble proved that he wasn’t stale by hatting splendidly
fourth test-match, where the Englishmen made
their first noteworthy stand. Also, bowler Truinble
scored well in the same match.
• • • •
With little pars., of this sort Australian cricket
scribes try to discredit the English arrangement of three-
day matches. The Argus man writes Three-day
games serve the weak counties well. A case in point was
a match between Sussex and Notts. Sussex led by 347
to 180 in the first innings, but in the second innings of
Notts, Gunn and Shrewsbury played for the draw, and not
it— the former scoring 150, the latter 114.” Why not say
that they played for the score, which in this case caused
the draw? The two Notts, veterans are notoriously
slower batsmen than the average pro., it is tru.e, but it is
absurd to say that in making 264 runs between them
they were batting against time.
For the sake of argument, let us say, three-day
games serve the weaker team well. A case in point was
the fourth test-match. England led by 372 to 196 in the
first, innings; but in second innings of the Australians
Noble, Trumper, Worrall, and others played to save the
game, and were successful, although the two highest
individual scores were only 89 and 60.
* * • *
One of the most striking coincidences connected
with cricket is that concerning Jack Lvons and recent
test-matches. The service the great siniter has done to
Australian cricket is well known, but in ’94-5, and a<u»in
in ’97-8, he proved a regular Jonah. In the former
season, Lyons was only a shade past his best, having been
oqe of the stars of the ’93 tour in England. lie played in
three test-matches and stood out of two ; Australia lost
those three and won those two I When Stoddart’ssecond
team came along, Lyons was still dangerous, as Victoria
and N.S.W. found, that season. He was given one test-
match and stood out of four. Australia lost that one,
and won those four. Such is luck t
* * • *
“ The smartest thing in the second test-match,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ was
Kelly's stumping of Townsend. The batsman's toe
scratched in and out of the crease like a streak, but too
late. MacLaren was not in good enough form for Jones
the first day; Jessop, being a slogger, has no nerves; Jack-
son with all his side is a clinker, but the others were
palsied, arid Jonah bowled like condensed Sheol. Dar-
ling’s batting is, as expected, hampered by his responsi-
bilities ; Worrall wanted to hit too early ; Hill’s 135 is
■ debited with three chances, but two of them would he
safe to miss disaster 99 times in 100. Trumper was the
gem, and he’ll be near the angels by, the time the averages
are totalled. Noble is earning unqualified praise for his
awful earnestness. It must he said that the press
unanimously gave the highest credit and praise to ‘ Our
Boys ’ for their win.
* * * *
The Hinglish press must be just now engaged
in trying to account for the sudden disappearance of the
“ funk” which seemed chronic with local “ crack ” bats-
men six weeks ago. Cricket scribes at that time held
out no hope of the Australian howling getting “col-
lared,” or the batting “ stuck up ” ever again. The
British Lion, with his tail between his legs, was denounc-
ing himself as a played-out impostor, although-the Aus-
tralians had not shone move brilliantly than some pre-
vious teams. It was their workmanlike way of winning
at the right end of the game that had established the
“funk.” The third test, wherein Hearne performed the
hat-trick on Noble, Gregory, and Clem Hill, infused new
spirit into poor old England, and what with Clem going
wrong in the nose, and Jones getting off color, and a
change in the luck of the toss, the Australians were put
on the defensive. Since July 17 four innings of over
350 runs and six individual “centuries” have been
scored against them. Let us pray for a splendid revival
of Clem Hill and Jones, for they were the fellows who
scared All-England in the first and second tests. The
two South Australians, with Noble, Trurnper, and
Trumble, have done all the good work for the team when
it was most needed. Billowell's. capture of 10 Surrey
wickets for 23 runs was a mere waste of strength, and his
“form” at critical times has been disappointin'', as a
rule.

Why doesn’t Darling give Svd. Gregory a
fortnight’s rest in the pavilion, and tnke'“ three days off”
himself meanwhile? Worrall could skipper for one match.

Ex-sailortnan Young promises to ripen into a
famous cricketer. As a fast left-handed bowler his fault,
at present, is too much leg-break, and as a batsman he
has a free, nautical style, seemingly. When he knocked
up 40 runs for Essex against the Australians, the cable
railed it wild slogging, hut the fact t hat, he repeated the
offence in a test-match suggests a little method in his
wildness. Fora man who was all at sea until a year or
two ago these Young performances look extraordinary.

™e A.J.C’s edict that a licensed bookmaker
shall not have any interest in a betting-shop or office is
on all-fours with its puerile action re country clubs, and
has caused the toe-rag and welching section of Ikeys to
ruh their paws with glee—for does it not almost abso-
lutely leave the Sydney shop-betting Eldorado to them
and their dirty little practices, hitherto only restrained
by the presence in the game of men of standing, all of
whom hut one have now been driven off the field.
Leviathan Oxenham has refused to put up his shutters,
and has consequently forfeited the right to bet at any
meeting held within a radius of 40 miles of Sydney G.P.O.
Oxys’ absence will probabty cause a rush of the
leather-lunged from Melbourne, and thus lessen the
profits of the Sydney ring.

Saturday night last saw a willing “go ” in a
Melbourne hostelry between a racehorse owner and a
certain jockey now “resting.” An argument started
over the way one of the owner’s horses had been ridden
and a difference of opinion led to strong personalities’
The lugger one didn’t wait for the other 10 punctuate his
remarks with a bottle, but promptly knocked him down
twice, finally twisting his neck untii his eves bulged. On
recovery, the jock rushed out and tried to buy a
revolver, but his wild and shaggy appearance led to the
shopkeeper declining to part with a “squirt” at any
price. A further fight, took place in the street on the
parties meeting later, the ex-rider being again badly
worsted. Further developments should be interesting.
>£l2oo in stakes are given at Canterbury
(M.L.) Trotting Club’s Aug. meeting. Which should
make N.S.W. trotting men think of'the possibilities of
the game over here, had only a decent showing been
made in the past.

* * * •
The Vic. Crown will not waste any money in
prosecuting cyclist Corbett for his alleged reckless over-
throw of Bilmartin. Corbett was committed for trial
but the evidence didn’t look nearly strong enough to
sustain the criminal charge.
* * * *
Melb. cycledom is bombs lie] led by the charge
levelled by E. J. Carey, of the Cycling News. He alleges
that the Martin-Walker match, advertised as being run
f ° ra JpKJP'irs'i and a side wager of £lOO (by Martin) to
£5O (by Walker) was in reality only raced for third
“cuts,” each competitor and the St, Hilda club taking an
equal share of the profits. Carey is partly right. No stakes
were put up either by club or men, but the winner got the
larger end of the two-thirds. Anyhow, the revelation
was a very unsurprising one to persons versed in sporting
lore. Purses are invariably faked or inflated, and as the
Martin-Walker match was a squarely-run contest there is
no real exposure at all. Nathless 'the league professes
great astonishment about the news it knew all along and
threatens an enquiry, which will probably end disastrously
for the competitors and the St. Kikla club.

Satisfactory to note that “ Prosper,” the favorite
whom I key Mo had striven hard to diddle, came out of
Vic. Club billiard tournament with colours flying. When
the first prize was handed over to him, he was told by the
secretary that he won it in manly style and had done
“nothing to warrant the demonstration made against
him. The fact of “ Prosper ” Graham being able to win
under the adverse circumstances went far to show why he
was such a “ good thing.” He possesses the nerve
whichits more than half the battle where billiardists of
the Vic. Club class are concerned. All ordinary Ikey Mo
for instance, is a poor perspiring coward when he gets
into “the pit” against a player who has more pluck
than himself. If the other fellow is a cool, determined
customer, with a bit the best of the handicap into the
bargain, Ike can’t make an attempt at a struggle. It was
“ Prosper s ” display of nerve in his first game which told
the books they had laid the winner, consequently
they plotted to shake his nerve, if possible, by ridiculous
protests and wild threats outside of the billiard-room and
by dirty tactics every time he took the floor for his
“ heat.” Everything calculated to interfere with the
bloomin’ favorite was tried in vain. At. the finish
The winner shouted “Fizz!” and said he'd “Nevei
played so well,”
And all that Ikey uttered was the exclamation “ ’Ell 1”

The star of whilom clever but unlucky Sydney
heavyweight, Nick (called “ Mick ”) Doolev, who dis-
puted supremacy with Australian top-notchers—Peter
Jackson, “Paddy ’ Slavin, and Joe Goddard, in turn—
continues to fall. At Kalgoorlie (W.A.), 15th ult., played-
out Tut Ryan beat Nick all the way in as tough a fight as
was ever seen on the fields. Miners suspected a schlenter
and kept away; the house was so poor that the match
would not have taken place but for someone offering £26.
Dooley should quit now. He was an at Sydney
Govt. Printing Office years ago till the fighting fit seized
him.
» * » »
“Old Timer”: Newcastle (N.S.W.) heavy-
weight Bill Green is a “dead ring” for one-time man-
eating Joe Goddard, but only in appearance and build.
There’s absolutely no “devil ”in him. He's as clumsy as
a bear, has no idea of the game, and is too old to be
taught. The match between him and Stewart on Saturday
night was side-splitting and exciting, but two old washer-
women would have shaped better ; yet it was hilled “for
the championship of N.S. W.,” and this in the city that
saw world-famed Jackson, Slavin and Goddard win and
hold the title which embraced Australia. Green, blown
out by his victory over the 3st.-lighter, weak-legged
black,challenged beetle-browed, battle-worn Bill Jennings
to fight at Newcastle. Latter has a particular soft thing
on, or I'm much mistaken. Sandy Ross, jun., hankers
after the job too.

With all their cleverness in pointing, Fitz-
simmons and Julian made one blunder in meeting Jeff-
ries. The ring was 2ft. less than the regulation square.
This gave the heavier man an advantage, as also did
Fitz.’s forcing tactics.

Shinty (hurling) has come to stay (if the law
will let it) as a Sunday afternoon attraction in Sydney.
3000 people, including many women, trained, trammed
and tramped it to Lady Robinson's Beach, on Sabbath
week, to see the “Michael Davitt” and “Robert Em-
mett” men smash heavy clubs, but trippers were “ had,”
as the Emmetts failed to materialise, and the Davitts
played a scratch-match “just for the fun of it,” and so
that nobody should be disappointed. Last day of rest
another huge crowd rolled up at same place and saw
Emmets romp over Tempe and finish up with a wild
“hurro.” Heavy spills were frequent. One burly son of
the “ ould dart ” was literally trampled upon and got up
dazed. Another had his shin opened fully 2in. ; but
these were small happenings How many people would
travel same distance (8 miles from town) to see an inter-
club match at the “ national game?” It's the spice of
roughness—probable wounds and gore—that draws, and
Shinty fairly bristles with these possibilities ; football
and fighting are simply “ not in it ” alongside hurling.
* * * *
A letter from N.S.W. sculler Jim Wray, written
from Boston, says he has given up all hope of inducing
world’s-chainpion Jake Gaudaur to row,; the Canadian
will not be lured. Jim is engaged bringing out a local
crack, and expects to meet Hunter River (N.S.W.) cham-
pion Towns, now in England, as soon as the latter
recovers from his accident. Same communication tells
that Teenier, once a strong claimant for the world s
supremacy, now never sits in a boat.
* * * *
As an evidence of their faith in the prosperity
of Federated Australia Massey-Harris Company Limited,
have, since the decision by referendum in N.S.W., secured
a business property on Bourke-street, between William
and King Streets, Melbourne, and have let a contract for
the erection of a large and commodious office and ware-
house premises. The building will be a handsome two-
storey and basement briek-and-stone structure 51ft. wide
x 315 ft. deep running through the block from Bourke-
street to Little Bourke-street, and fitted with every
modern convenience for handling their large and rapidly
increasing farm-implement and bicycle business. Work
has already commenced, and the premises will be ready
for occupation by November I.*
* * * *
We are requested to inform Bulletin readers
that the management of the Sydney Safe Deposit (in Ash-
street, back of Paling’s, Sydney)’have decided, with a
view to popularising theuse of the safes, tooffer a special
discount of 25 per cent, to persons paying rent for two
years in advance. At present the annual rents in advance
are from two to six guineas, and the new system will
have the effect of reducing the rents to from to 4J
guineas. It has also been decided to lease safes at from
15s. per quarter and 255. per half year, a further innova-
tion.*
Pastor Bevan, of Melbourne, has adoptetv a
Yes-No policy on the tote question. Talking
of the rpachine in competition with Ikky Mo,
the plump shepherd faces both ways : “ It may
be said that the public would not be foolish
enough to risk their money on the poorer
chances which a bookmaker furnishes, hut, as I
have already remarked, the totalisator itself is
the most perfect scheme for finally netting all
the money out of the betting-public,” It doesn’t
seem to have struck the good doctor that any
human cash-taker who makes a steady profit on
his business—notably the hotelkeeper or the
tobacconist—is just as perfect a scheme for
gathering in the public boodle. Ur. Bevan’s
twaddle is pathetic. The tote claims to give
its clients a reasonable gamble on level terms
all round. Take the ease of the occasional
race-goer, who punts .£1 a time with the tote,
and assume that even 10 per cent. com. is de-
ducted. He goes 12 days a year to the races,
which means a total risk of .£72, at the rate of
six events per day. He sinks 10 per cent, of
this —£7 4s.—on the machine. If he gets the
exact amount of luck that is due to him, his
betting entertainment will cost him £144 in 20
years—say about half as much as he has spent
upon moderate drinking pleasures. The tote
is hardly more ruinous than Dr. Bevan’s offer-
tory-plate, which furnishes no fun in return for
a silver-coin contribution.

Among some new and fetching gramophone records
lately sent along from London to agent Sykes, in Me l-
bourne, there is a capital “ turn ” bv old friend Snazelb.
The voice of Snazzy is beard protesting that he doesn’t
feel equal to “The Heart Bowed Down.” He makes
a start, but after singing a few bars, he says lie
will whistle the rest, and his whistling comes out of the
funnel as fresh and larye as life. Then Snazzy siting some
more, and whistles again, and so on. Finally he wants to
know what he will get for his trouble, and lie goes away
disgusted, exclaiming “ What ! only a pint of beer ! ”

Amateur Orchestral Society concert. Wrong. Victoria
Young, who dfebuted at the ’96 concert, of this society,
and again sang for the amateurs in ’9B, was enthusiasti-
cally encored on both occasions. Doubtless if the C.P.
had knowoi that Miss Young (despite her name) is also a
“ true believer” the assertion would not have been made.
The late Grattan Riggs was always bored by a prattler.
The old hoy had a very slow, methodical style of conver-
sation and if casually interrupted would rise fro.il his
chair and abruptly decamp. Riggs, too, was very touchy
on the subject of his alleged ancientness. “ Fcr Go i’a
sake,” he would say to his advance agent, “ if you s ? any
of those pressmen tell them not to call me the vet., ran
Grattan Riggs.”

A bad oversight in the Firm’s representation of
Dumas’story is the failure to account for the reappear-
ance of Miladi. Athos explains that he has carefully
hanged the wife of his bosom, and left her dangling; yet
she bobs up serenely without a word of explanation. ‘ It
.is clear that Miladi was plucked from the tree, but why
hot let her tell the tale of her rescue in a strong
soliloquy?
John Fuller, the sturdy, tuneful tenor who runs the
Myriorama Co., was originally a compositor. He and
John Hancock, the Vic. Labor member, were fellow type-
setters in the same London office.
The world’s remarkable lack of great composers may
have much to do with the sudden fame of Don Perosi,
the youthful Venetian priest—three of his oratorios are
already billed for the English festivals. If there is any-
thing in Perosi’s w-ork, he has the wav quite clear, for
Italy is left wil h poor old Verdi, the sole living relie of a
dead musical day, and mad Mascagni. The Frenchmen
have taken to writing operettas and other transient stage
frirolities ; and the musical glory of Germany seems to
have vanished absolutely since rugged old Wagner held
the fort.
When “ The Firm’s ” opera co. was going to Broken
Hill it remembered that that was the place where the
thermometer played about the 116 degree mark. So it
took out its gauziest gowns—and struck the town shiver-
ing in a temperature below freezing-point. And firewmod
had just been put up to 30s. a ton, too !

Mr. S. A. Palmer has opened a Sydney

“ Floger,” on an important subject:—
Sydney is becoming swamped with women steno-
graphers and typewriters triad to accept employment at
any price ; some, doubtless, through stress of circum-
stances, but the great majority because they are in-
capahles. The reason is that thousands of parents have
an idea that their daughters have only to attend a short-
hand and typewriting school four hours a day for six
months (called a “complete course” by most schools),
and they will he competent to earn a living. To this end
the fee (mostly about ±l7) is, perhaps with difficulty,
scraped together, and the girl enters upon her “course”
with bright dreams of prospective independence. Result
(with a clever pupil), a 40-words-per-minute rate on the
typewriter and 100 ditto in shorthand. Sufficient, doubt-
less, for mere mechanical business purposes ; but far more
than this is needed in a position where a mistake may
cost a big sum, and where a knowledge of business-prac-
tice is indispensable. And what knowledge of the busi-
ness-world and its methods can be acquired during a
six months' course which leaves the girl as it found her,
grievously lacking not only in general information, but
in the commonest educational equipment? Girls iike
this simply keep on getting billets and being “ fired ” for
cotal incompetence, until they are forced into the ranks
of the “ any-pricers.” Competition prevents the adop-
tion by the schools of a thorough business-course of
education, because people will have the cheap and quick
course. Thus business-men are gradually coming to
abjure the petticoated so that, while the
number of employment-seeking girls is rapidly increasing,
the number of openings is steadily decreasing, and the
result cannot fail to he disastrous.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
ttte have examined the annexed Balance Sheet made up to 30th June, 1899, and report that we find the several
VV jf, e ms therein stated to conform to the Balances in the General Ledger, and the Balance Sheets of the respective
Branches of i he Bank collectively.
We have counted the Cash and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Sydney on the 30th
June, 1899, and hereby certify that we have found them correct. We further certify that all Vouchers, Accounts, and
Documents necessary for verifying the Balance Sheet have been submitted to us. ■ - ,
The Cash, Bills and other Securities held at the Branch Banks in New South Wales and Queensland, on the date
of Balancing, have been certified to as being correct by the usual documents, and the figures of the Balance Sheet of
the London Branch have been received by cable.
In our opinion the Balance Sheet is a full and fair Balance Sheet, containing the particulars required by the
Articles of Association of the Australian Joint Stock Bank, Limited, and properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and
correct view of the state of the Company’s affairs, as shown by the Books of the Company.
T. F. 11. MACKENZIE, F.S.I.A.
F. JAGO SMITH,
Auditors.
The Chairman, in moving the adoption of the report, said :
“ My duty is, on behalf of your Directors, to ask you to adopt the Report and Balance-sheet now in your hands, and
before formally doing so I have only a few words to say on that subject. Comparing: the Balance fig-tires with those
for last Half-year, there are increases in the following items—Bills in Circulation, Cash at London Bankers, and in
Bills Receivable and Loans to Customers, which are indications of an expansion of current business. Deposit money
decreased by a small amount, and the Coin has also decreased, but is accounted for by the increase of Bills Receiv-
able, the Current advance business, and payments in discharge of the B. Deposits in terms of Clause 10 of the
Arrangement of 1897. The next thing I ask your attention to, is the good position of the Bank with coin and liquid
assets, showing the absolute safety which we give to those customers who do business with us as depositors or
borrowers. The liability on Current Accounts and New Fixed Deposits, on the Notes issued and Bills payable and
the Interest accrued due to customers—in fact, every claim that can he brought against the New Bank is provided
for to the extent of 76 per cent, in Cash and liquid items as shown in the Balance-sheet, and there is besides further
cover in the shape of Bills Receivable, Bills Discounted, and Cash Credit Advances. This large provision for absolute
safeguarding of the Current business is not, I regret to say, productive of such a margin of profit as in previous
years, but we hope for better employment of our resources with the return of more ptosperous times. Thera are
many signs of a revival in business in view of the recent bountiful rains in some parts of this colony and the advanced
prices for our staple products.”
The report was adopted, and ordered to be printed and circulated amongst the Proprietors and,Depositors.
Mr. George Alexander Murray was re-elected as Shareholders’ Director, and the lion. F. Jago Smith, M.L.C., was
re-elected as Shareholders’ Auditor.
At the Annual Meeting of Depositors, the Chairman (Hon. James Watson, M.L.C.) presided. The retiring
Director (Mr. Philip H. Morton) and retiring Auditor (Mr. T. F. 11. Mackenzie, F.S.I. A.) were re-elected.
A vote of thanks was accorded to the Chairman, Directors and Officers of the Bank for their good management
of the affairs of the Bank.

The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing- ballads and verses of sailor-men’s
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Brady has contributed
to The Budletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing; and the price is ss.
post-free.
FLOWERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Blumen dir zu streuen.
Dies soil mich erfreuen.”
Lilies for thine innocence
With their whiteness vying,
Boses sweet with redolence
Of the summer dying ;
Sprays of rue for lovers’ grief,
And my song's one laurel leaf < • •
Purple of anemone
Starred in chaplets royal,
With a sprig of rosemary
For remembrance loyal ;
Pansies strewn in velvet plots
And the kind forget-me-nots . . .
Be the path with fragrance spread
Where thy feet are resting,
Farther back the leaves lie dead,
And Decay is nesting ;
Let my sighing rhyme be laid
Gently with the things decayed . . .
Ah, but in the Night’s great gloom
Where our dreams are mustered,
Lie old wreaths of pallid bloom
Still with cunning clustered ;
And the gods, in magic hours,
Deck with gold their withered flowers . . .
Traumes blumen dir zu streuen
Soil auf immer mich erfreuen.

Cootamundra’s abortive effort to raise a
fund for the entertainment of Gov. Beauchamp,
and the Governor’s announcement that for the
present he doesn’t intend to go there, are remin-
ders that in the early years N.S.W. Governors
paid all their own expenses when visiting inland
towns. And then the expenses were considerable,
as there were no Vice-regal trains. Up to Fitz-
roy’s time the Governors regarded it as a duty to
visit every large centre at least once in their term,
and to do this they hired the necessary horses,
provisioned their party, and paid their hotel bills
with spot cash.
Reid seems to have got out of tho N.S.W.
Treasurership just at the right time, and left his
successor, Carruthers, in a lovely hole. Reid got
out on 30th June, 1800. A few days later the
much-delayed Auditor-General's report for the
year ended 30th June, 1808, came to hand. It
was full of unpleasant remarks about Reid’s
financing during ’OB, and Carruthers, as Treasurer
now, has to stand the storm. Apparently Reid
saw the document coming, and stepped out of the
. way (into a better billet, too) at the psychological
moment;, and let the big report hit Carruthers in
the stomach.
Treasurer Braddon estimates Tasmania’s
present year’s surplus at .£OO,lBO. Customs duties
are to be reduced by £37,000, thus paving the way
for “ that more moderate tariff which must come
with Federation.” Which prompt levelling down
is a bad knock for the old 225. Gd. lie of the
N.S.W. anti-Federal gang, said lie being based on
the other lie that Tasmania could not do with less
customs revenue than it has already and therefore
that the N.S.W. tariff must be levelled up to meet
its case. There is to be a boom in public works,
no less than £227,740 being on the estimates.
This is a mighty lot for Tasmania, though not
nearly so much per head as N.S.W. is spending.
General-manager Stewart, of Chillagoe, is
probably at present in receipt of the biggest Aus-
tralian income from personal exertion. The big
Chillagoe co. is said to pay him £7500 a year, other
Chillagoe co.’s add £2500, and from the B.H.
Proprietary, whose general management he lately
resigned, he draws £lOOO a year as consulting ex-
pert. Stewart is, nevertheless, admittedly not in
the first flight of metallurgists, and there are no
doubt better miners; it was as an organiser
that he instantly impressed the B.H. Prop. men.
That is how intelligent private enterprise rewards
its servants. On the other hand, G. H. Reid has
for years been N.S.W. Premier at £1370 per
per annum—which, when he has paid all official
allowances; and subscriptions, probably means
£BOO or £ooo—less than is often received by a first-
class civil servant entitled to a pension.
The tragedy of poverty ! Five small
creatures were recently brought before the N.
Melb. police-court as neglected children. Ages
from 2 to 13 and scarcely sufficient clothing
amongst them for one. The eldest—a girl—
bugging her shivering baby brother to her breast,
told the tab*: —“ We were living in Arden-street,
N. Melb. Mother’s been in the madhouse for a
long time, and I’ve had to look after my brothers
and sisters. On Tuesday we were put out because
father, who is a piano-tuner, could not pay the
rent. Father got a van and put us and the bit of
furniture in it, and we were driven towards
Spencer-street. Father left the van and after a
bit came back with some food. Then he left us
again, arid we waited, and waited, and waited, but
be never came back and we’ve got no friends
and no relations.” The tragedy of it !

Continuous supplies of New Records keep
arriving, and amongst the latest are reproductions
of VIOLIN performances.
The Patentees have devoted a great deal of
trouble and attention to the production of a
perfect VIOLIN record, and I am glad to
say that, after repeated disappointments, their
efforts have been crowned with success.
No sound re-producing machine has till now
been able to register a perfect Violin record, and
it is difficult to appreciate, without actual experi-
ence, the many technical and practical difficulties
that have been successfully overcome.
I have the pleasure to introduce to your notice
a series of VIOLIN records, executed by violinists
of repute, and I think it will be readily conceded
that for brilliancy of execution, and for delicacy
of tone, they have never been surpassed by any
records hitherto produced. The most delicate
cadenza, the most intricate bravoura passage, is
faith ully rendered. All the beauties of touch
and style, and the masterly technique of the
artist, will be readily appreciated by all music
lovers, and by violin players in particular.

OBJECTS OF THE COMPANY.
THIS COMPANY is being formed for the purpose of acquiring the right, title, and interest in three
gold leases issued by the Mines Department of Victoria, numbered respectively 33G9, 3370, and
3377, containing an area of about 120 acres, situated at Glasgow Range, nine miles from Ballarat, in
the Parish of Yarrowee, County of Grenville, and working same on the hydraulic dredging principle.
The property offered is right in the heart of one of the great gold fields of this Colony, and does
not require much said about it to recommend it to the public. The mere fact of it being in the Balla-
rat district, which has been so famous for its marvellous returns in gold, obtained from the innumer-
able mines that have been worked in that justly famed golden city, is sufficient in itself to warrant its
bond-tides.
The method proposed to be applied in working this property will be on the hydraulic dredging
principle, similar to that now in operation at Staghorn Flat, Yackandandah, owned by the Hon. J. A.
Wallace, M.L.C., the profits from which amounted to .£20,000 for 8 months of last year, as publicly
stated by that gentleman (vide Argus, September 17th, 1898).
The success attending hydraulic sluicing is obtained in the fact of the capacity for treating large
quantities of material at a nominal cost by this class of machinery, and its efficiency in saving gold,
which altogether removes the element of speculation or uncertainty.
Following is a comparative table of results which may be obtained from an estimated treatment of
57,000 tons per month, which is a very moderate computation, as Mr. Wallace has manipulated IGO
per hour, being equivalent to over 100,000 monthly allowing for all stoppages :

The auriferous nature of the property it is proposed to work, and its adaptability for dredging,
will be apparent from the perusal of the attached reports from Messrs. Reginald A. F. Murray,
F.G.S., London, late Government Geologist of Victoria ; George A. Wilherforee, M.E., late engineer
to the Hon. J. A. Wallace ; «T. B. King, and Clias. Trevena, all of whom speak highly of the pro-
perty, and are unanimous that good results will be obtained.
The vendors show their confidence in the mine by taking the whole of their consideration in
shares, allowing all the cash realised by the sale of shares to go to the credit of the Company, to put it
into working order, thus demonstrating that they have no doubt of the mine being a highly payable
one.
The property presents advantageous features as a dredging proposition, and the promoters, in
placing it before the public, do so with every assurance as to its being a thorough, sound and legiti-
mate mining venture, and one that has every prospect of being placed on the dividend-paying list at
an early date.
A large number of shares have already been applied for, and as priority of date will be strictly
observed in allotting the shares, early application is necessary to prevent disappointment.

Messrs. Fewcofski AND Hill, GO to 70 Queen-street, Melbourne.
Dear Sirs, —
The property indicated by you to me on the Parisli Plan of Yarrowee—portions of allotments
131° and 131 N. are shown on the geological map of the Ballarat goldfield, prepared from lr.y personal
survey, as containing in area of “ oldest gold drift,’’ resting on a silurian bedrock. I have a good recol-
lection of the place, and know that the gravel was worked for gold with success in the early days and
with crude appliances, but only to a limited extent.
There is a vast amount of gravel which, in all probability, contains gold generally diseminated
throughout its whole mass, and which, with adequate water supply, should pay well if dealt with on a
largo soale with proper appliances.—l am, Dear Sirs, Yours most respectfully,
(Signed) REGINALD A. F. MURRAY.
Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill, Melbourne. Ballarat East, June 14th, 1899.
Dear Sirs, —
I have pleasure in stating that in company with Mr. Trevena I visited your Leases, and
styled the “Jason,” bn 3rd inst. 1 found them to be composed of a fine quartz alluvial, and well
adapted for hydraulic sluicing. There are unmistakable signs of immense work having been done,
and no doubt large amounts of gold were taken out in the early days. There is abundant catchment
area for water, and many old races cut which could also be utilised to augment the supply. There
is also abundance of firewood, and taking these items into consideration, I have no hesitation in
stating that the material can be treated for 3d. per ton, which, according to the accounts of those
who are in a position to know the quality of the ground, should leave a very handsome margin for
X>rofit. —1 am, Dear Sirs, Yours faithfully, (Signed) GEO. A. WILBERFORCE M.E.
Late Engineer to Hon. J. A. Wallace, Woolshed Valley G.M. Co.
Spec. Cons. Eng. to Australian Mines Developt. Co.
Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill, GO to 70 Queen-street, Melbourne. Melbourne, June 4th, 1899.
Dear Sirs, —
As requested, I furnish you with a report on your property called tho “Jason,” situated
about eleven miles south of Ballarat.
I visited your mine twice, and was very much pleased with the prospects of it, especially on my
second visit. There is, I should say, from 15 to 25 feet of gravel wash, containing gold from surface
to bottom, and is splendidly situated for hydraulic sluicing. I gave it a good trial with dish prospects
from top to bottom with very satisfactory results. Bulking all the prospects together, I should say it
would go at least £ a d wt. per load from the surface as deep down as I cquld get, and I feel siire that the
wash under foot that I could not get at would average more thaii double the prospects I have obtained.
What I tried has been driven out in the early days, and from what I could learn had been remarkably
rich ; in fact, it must have been to induce them to cut races and construct a puddling machine in those
days. There has been very little ground worked on your property; in fact, your property is virgin
ground so to speak. I traced the same run of wash on surface for over a quarter of a mile, and you
could go over as much bottom in a month with present appliances as has been worked altogether on
your property. It is the only ground I have seen similar to what we used to do so well out of in New
Zealand in the early days. Your ground has exceptional advantages for working, very little timber
to obstruct operations, no big stones, except, perhaps, on the bottom, and continues level-made ground
of the same character of wash as far as I could see all through your property, which will take a good
many years to work out.
In conclusion, I think I can with confidence recommend your property as likely (as far as I have
seen, with plenty of water and the way you intend working it) to be a dividend-paying mine for many
years to come.—Yours faithfully, (Signed) J. B. KING.
Messrs. Fewcofski and Hill, GO Queen Street, Melbourne. June 2nd, 1899.
Dear Sirs, —
As requested I furnish you with a report on your Mining property called the “Jason,” within
nine miles south-west of Ballarat.
It consists of about 120 acres of quartz-alluvial wash with a depth from 30 to 40 feet, very well
adapted for hydraulic sluicing. There has been a considerable amount of work done on it in the early
days, and, from what information I could get, with very good results; even with the primitive
appliances used at the time, I washed a considerable quantity of stuff by dish, which gave very
satisfactory returns. I should think it would yield from 1G to 18 grains to the ton, and, with the
quantity of wash and easy mode of working, it should give excellent returns.
You have very good catchments for water, as there are two partially-constructed dams, and also
some three miles of race, which could be made available with very little outlay. You have plenty of
timber for all purposes.
I think, gentlemen, with oareful and judioious management, you have a splendid property.
Yours faithfully, (Signed) CHAS. TREVENA.

The Case of
MISS EDITH A, BARKER.
(From the “Leichhardt Standard.”)
Another astonishing recovery having
come to our ears, a reporter was despatched
to the residence of Mrs. Barker, of Carlisle-
street, Leichhardt, Sydney, whose daughter,
jJliss Barker, had only lately regained her
health after a three years’ spell of suffering
from acute neuralgia. This young lady
showed no reluctance to be interviewed.
“ Pain,” said Miss Barker, in answer to
the newspaper man’s questions, “ pain isn’t
the word for it; why, the agony of it nearly
drove me mad. For three long and miser-
able years not a day passed but I was seized
with an acute attack of neuralgia. The
pain jumped round my face and head till I
was quite beside myself, and it would often
dart down into my shoulder as well. I
really hate to think, even now, how I
suffered, and I only wonder that I was able
to stand it as well as I did. I congratulate,
myself 100 times a day on my freedom from
that terrible neuralgia—then the bane of
my life. ”
“ Was your general health affected, Miss
Barker ?”
“ I should say so. I was deplorably thin,
and I got exceedingly weak. The excessive
strain on my nerves began to tell on my
whole nervous system. I always felt heavy
and weary. All my vital force disappeared,
and I got quite limp and helpless. I could
neither sleep nor eat. The miserable pam
in my head and jaws kept me awake all
night, and, as for eating, I was fairly
frightened to put any food in my mouth, for
this would frequently start; the neuralgia
again, just, perhaps, when I was recovering
from an attack of it. Besides, I had
no real appetite, and only at.) some-
thing when absolutely driven to do; so
by sheer weakness for lack of food.
I was run down frightfully, and was
but a mere shadow of what I once was.
I was a source of very great anxiety to my
mother, who used to give me all sorts of
medicine to try and get me cured. But
neither the doctor’s prescriptions nor the
preparations supplied me by the chemists
were able to cure me. Once or twice I
fancied I obtained some slight relief, but if
I did so it was of no service to me, for the
neuralgia came on with double force after-
wards. At last I was recommended to take
Clements Tonic. That is, a lady told my
mother it would cure me outright, and so
it did. I never came across such a remedy
in my life before. After three doses there
was a sort of slacking off in the awful pangs
in my face. I felt easier somehow, and
more able to bear the pain. After I had
taken a bottle I was ever so much better.
I improved all over. There was a good
colour in my cheeks. I had quite a healthy
craving for my meals once more, and was
ple.ased, you may be sure, when meal time
came. I began to get plumper and heavier
every day, and slept—well, I made up for
lost time, and no mistake. It was such a
relief to be able to sleep without being
roused half-a-dozen times in a night by
neuralgia. Now I just put mydiead on my
pillow, and slept right away till the morn-
ing. . I bought another bottle of Clements
Tonic, and that did all that was required.
My neuralgia has gone for ever—that is, I
have every reason to believe so, for it’s
never showed itself since. ”
Our reporter then put a few questions to
Mrs. Barker, the mother of the young lady.
“ I had a great deal of trouble with her,”
replied that lady. “ The months passed on
and on, and I couldn’t find anything to do
her good, till at length I adopted a recom-
mendation of a neighbour, and tried
Clements Tonic; and that remedy acted
just like magic. It pulled her round, and
made her bright and healthy again in no
time. It was quite a surprise to me to see
her so quickly cured, for I had had so many
failures with other medicines. lam sure it
will be a duty as well as a pleasure to my
daughter to make her case public.”
Our reporter then asked Miss Barkov's
permission to publish all that had pas~e
“Oh, yes, with pleasure.”

The Case of
Mr. Joseph Meyers.
(From the “ Windsor Australian”)
(by our special reporter.)
What is still called Old Government House,
siiuate in George-street, Windsor, is now
the residence of
Rflr. Joseph Meyers, Painter and
Contractor.
It was here that a reporter from the Austra-
lian was fortunate enough to find that
gentleman.
“ I come to learn something of
Your Present Good Health,”
said the reporter.
“Concerning my illness, you mean,” re-
plied Mr. Meyers. “I shill be pleased to
tell you all about it.” Our reporter seated
himself opposite Mr. Meyers, who said—
“ My energy began to wane away. I used
to wonder what was the matter with me, for
my work, in which I once took a pleasure,
now became a burden to me.
I Was Always Tired,
and was just as weary at the commence-
ment of the day as at the finish. A queer
pain started in the upper poitioii of my
back. It felt like a sharp stab between the
shoulders. There was a
Bitterish, Sour Taste -r
in my mouth in the mornings, and I couldn't
tackle my breakfast, which was, up to then,
my best meal. Indeed, my appetite began
to fail altogether. I was very sleepy and
dull all day, arid hardly cared to move
unless I was absolutely compelled.”
“ How did you sleep at night time?”
“Badly—very ; I kept on waking up
In Fits and Starts
the night through, and the pain in my back
v»j very often severe enough to keep me
wide awake for most of the time,”
“ Did you guess what was the matter
with you ?”
“Oh, yes! I knew very well my liver
was out of order, and I frequently took
strong purgatives and other medicines to
make it work properly again, but nothing I
then took could check the progress of the
complaint, and since then I’ve come to the
conclusion that the regular taking of
cathartic medicine is a great mistake. Then
terrible
Headaches Afflicted Me.
I couldn’t eat a mouthful of food now with-
out suffering great discomfort afterwards,
such as wind and distension arid pain in the
chest, and every few days X should have a
bad bilious turn, when the very thought of
food would almost turn me up. ”
“ Did your liver troubles give rise to any
secondary symptoms?”
“Yes, most serious ones. My nervous
system got completely unstrung. The.’
merest trifles would give me a painful
shock. I lost my interest in things in
general, and began to get thin and pale. I
thought I was going to become a confirmed
invalid. I felt
Completely Worn Out,
and was just about to try a change of air
when my eye fell Upon a paragraph in a
newspaper telling the result of using
Ch merits Tonic in a severe case of liver
complaint. It was through reading this
that I was induced to obtain a couple of
bottles, and ! ,
I Was Fairly Astonished
at the celerity with which it stopped the
pain in my back. Clements Tonic braced
me up splendidly. I began to lift up my
head again instead of dragging about all
day in that heavy, drowsy way. 1 was able
to take some exercise now, and could eat
and sleep again properly.
No More Headaches
or nervousness troubled me, and I was at
the fourth bottle when I was quite well,
and getting as stout and strong as ever, and
considered it no longer necessary to take
Clements Tonic regularly, though I took a
dose now and then to clinch the cure, as it
were.”
“How is your health now?”
“ Perfect, and my liver must have;been
completely cured, for never since have I:felt*;
it.”
“ And you think Clements Tonic eradi-
cated the disease ?”
“I’m quite sure of that, for it was the
remedy I should have taken at'first if I had
known of it.”
“ With your consent this iriterview will
appear in the Australian.”
“ Certainly, I should like it to.”
Declared to be true,

, Windsor, this 19th day of July, 1898.

How It Is.
In the streets of the town where I live I some-
times meet a poor fellow who is so badly off that
his appeal for a penny or two is hardly to be re-
sisted. He has lost both his legs above the knees
and punts himself along the pavement with his
hands, like a loaded barge in shallow water.
Thank Mercy, one doesn’t often see human hulks
like him. Where there is a single instance of a
man having lost both legs or both arms there are
a dozen where only one limb of the pair is missing.
And where there is a single case of the latter sort
there are a hundred cases of people who are lame,
or more or less disabled, by disease or minor in-
juries which are scarcely noticeable, yet in the
long run very serious to those so afflicted.
Consequently, when we sum up both classes, we
perceive that it isn’t the total wrecks and the in-
curables that are most expensive to society, but
the prodigious host which must work, and does
work, yet always under difficulties and against
hindrances. Men and women regularly em-
ployed, but who are continually breaking down in
a small way, thus losing fragments of time and
fractions of wages, are of the kind I mean. The
amount of income lost in this way in one year in
England is immense. And so far as the cause of
all this is disease, and not accident or born bodily
imperfection, it is almost always preventible and
generally curable. Look at this, for example, and
take heart.
“In the spring of this year (1897),” the writer
says, “my health began to fail me. My appetite
was poor, and after meals I had pain and weight
at the ehest. I could not sleep owing to the pain,
and I got weaker every day. I had so much pain
that I dared not eat, and rapidly lost flesh.
“ I was in agony night and day, and often sat
by the fire at night as-1 could not rest in bed. I
had a deal of muscular pain, particularly in the
arms. I gradually got worse and worse, and in
two months lost two score pounds weight.
“ I saw a doctor who gave me medicines and in-
jected morphia to ease the pain; but I was no
better for it. Then I met with a friend who told
me of the great benefit he had derived from the use
of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup. I gob
a bottle of it from Mr. S. Richkrdson, chemist,
Bridgman-street, and in a week I could eat well
and food no longer distressed me. Therefore, I
kept on with the medicine, and soon was strong
and well. I am now in the best of health, and
recommend this remedy to all I meet. You are at
liberty to publish this letter as you like. ” —(Signed)
William Bridge, Grocer and Baker, 65, Bridg-
man-street, Bolton, October sth, 1897.
Here we have an illustration of the proposition
with which this article sets out.', From Mr.
Bridge’s account of his own case we see that he
lost a considerable time from his business. How
much that represents in money he does not say ;
nor is it important to the argument. For two
months or more he lost from his business practi-
cally all he was worth to it; and what that situa-
tion would have signified, had it been indefinitely
continued, any intelligent person can imagine.
Men frequently become stricken with poverty as
with illness in that way. However well any
business may be managed in an emergency by
others, it is. not to be supposed that it gets on as
prosperously as when the proprietor is himself at
the helm. And he cannot be there while he is
suffering agonies from disease. This is true even
if we make no calculation of the direct expenses
created by illness, nor of the suffering experienced
the latter not computable in terms of money.
Now, please remark how quickly Mr. Bridge
was cured of his ailment—bad as it seemed and
really was. Dating from the time he began using
Mother Seigel’s Syrup, he says: —“ In a week I
could eat well, and the food no longer distressed
me.” His trouble was of the digestion only (acute
dyspepsia), for which this preparation long ago
proved itself a specific. Had he known of and
employed it when the attack began he would have
lost no time, felt no pain.
The lesson of the case is this: —As indigestion
is a common complaint, and dangerous also when
neglected, the remedy should be at hand for imme-
diate use when needed. The more valuable the
treasure the more strict should be the guard over
it. And health is a jewel compared with which
rubies are as the glass beads of savages.

FOXS’ PURR INDIGO.
As the result of many years of experience, FOXS’ PURE INDIGO DYE is recognised as the best in the Market. The superiority of Pure Indigo over other
dyes may be briefly summed up as follows :
FASTNESS OF THE DYE.—lndigotine, the colouring principle of Indigo, is insoluble in water, alcohol, ether, and dilute acids, and alkalies either hot or
cold, hence its extreme fastness for all conditions of ordinary wear and tear. It is also quite fast to Sunlight, and for an all-round dye there is nothing superior to
it- There is no need to point out the advantage there is in a perfectly fast dye, as compared with one which may be fast to light, but will not stand perspiration, or
washing, or vice versa.
STRENGTH AND HANDLE.—CIoth dyed Pure Indigo is stronger and tougher than the same cloth dyed in other ways, owing to the dye-bath being at
a comparatively low temperature, and the absence of any injurious chemicals or mordaunts. In almost all other dyes, the cloth is first boiled in the mordaunt and
then in the dye for some hours altogether. This always impoverishes the wool fibre, and destroys much of its natural lustre and softness, whereas the Indigo Vat
with its soft, mellow liquor, adds to the fulness and richness of the fabric. Indigo Dyeing being a mechanical process rather than a chemical one, actually feeds the cloth.
All their standard cloths are stamped every two yards on the back,
FOX PURE XCTXSXG-O,
• • and without this stamp are not Genuine*
These Celebrated Serges can be obtained from the leading Tailors and Drapers throughout the Australian Colonies.
See for yourselves that the Serge is stamped—FOX PURE INDIGO,-as many dealers for the sake of an
extra profit try to sell inferior-dyed cloths, remarking “that they are just as good as Pure Indigo.”

Grand Old Man Geo. Gladstone, father of Rev. Nathalia,
is a mine of anecdote, and one of his favorites is about
Charles Mathews. “When Charles appeared at Melb.
Royal in the morning to rehearse the ‘ Game of Specula-
tion,’ with his inimitable Affable Hawk, Daddy Rogers,
Jim Bartlett, Jimmy Milne, and the rest, paid Their
respects. Charley picked me out and said ‘ Dear me, I
think we have met before.’ ‘ Yes, sir,' said I, • I was the
original Frederick Noble with you in the “Game of Specu-
lation,” at London Lyceum, in 1851.’ ‘Well, well,’ says
Mathews, ‘ how the time flies ! And it is now 1871.’ You
fhall be Frederick Noble again.’ ‘ But, sir, lam too old.’
Old ! Damy ! You’re nothing like so old as I am.’ And
so I was once more Frederick Noble to the great
comedian.”
Fletcher McAdoo, brother to Orpheus, late of Sydney
Palace Theatre, had a joyful experience in Rarotonga,
where his company was playing. Part of the show con-
sisted of a friendly game of cards between McAdoo and
his trained dog, in which the dog usually won. Card-
playing is an abomination in missionary-ridden Raro-
tonga, and a native Judge, who daily risks death and
tempts Providence by calling himself Meringatamd,
promptly proceeded against showman and doc and im-
posed a line of lOdols.
Years ago, the clown and columbine of a panto, were
pals—were man and wife, in fact, by a theatrical arrange-
ment usually admitted by mummers to be just as good as
the clerical knot, especially when, as in this case the
clown’s own old original “Dutch” had left him for
reasons too numerous to mention. These two and several
others of the co. were invited to dinner one Sunday by
an_ ex-pro., who had “shaken” the “ profesh ’’and was
doing well in the vegetable line. The host had a bright
little girl of seven who had been to the show the night
before, and was in raptures about the dancing, sinking
and “ the lovely people dressed only in skin.” " Then the
clown said, “ Which part did you like best, dear?” “Oh
it was all lovely,” said the child ; “ but I think I liked
the clown and the concubine best.”
Handsome monuments cover the remains of Frank
Bates, “Johnny” Hall, Mrs. George Darrell, and others
which lie pretty close together in Melb. general cemetery’
but it would be hard to find the spot where lie the bones
of old “Billy” Hoskins. Years ago, attention was called
to the fact that the grave was unmarked, but
was done. By the way, the name on the gravestone of
Fanny Oathcart that was is Frances Darrell Price the
last being the real surname of our friend George Darrell.
“ For snobbery, side, and quiet damnation, the foreign
settlements in China,” says a Bulletin correspondent,
“ take the belt. At a Shanghai race-meeting I saw some
curious sights. Demi-monde here are mostty Americans
and very gorgeous. They are not allowed in the theatre
dress-circle, and on the course they are debarred from
entering the grand-stand part. Their carriages have to
avoid certain principal streets, but they thrive in spite of
it. The immorality of the elite would stagger even a
Westralian, and yet 20 people left the theatre last night
when Marcus Superbus made his attempt on Mercia in
the palace scene, in the ‘ Sign the Cross.’ Theatrical
business booms here, but is mostly in the hands of very
cronk companies.’’ J
’Murka has a famous woman agent for the placing of
stage plays, and all the choicest theatrical literary goods
are said to pass through her hands. Miss Marbury, the
expert in question, scored her first successful deal ’with
“ Little Lord Faunt.leroy.” She found the various mar-
kets for Mrs.’ Burnett’s comedy, and since then she has
come to be the recognised go-between for authors and
managers. The.big book in Miss Marbury’s registry-office
contains particulars of hundreds of desirable four-act
domestic dramas for sale, and the authors sit in rows in
the ante-room waiting to be engaged.
An anecdote of Paderewski credits the pampered
pianist with a horribly snobbish reply to a ’Murkan
woman who besought by letter “ a lock of his hair.” The
letter came to hand whilst Paddy and co. were on the
train-car, and his secretary wrote as follows: “ Dear
Madame,—M. Paderewski directs me to say that it affords
him much pleasure to comply with your" request. You
fail to specify whose hair you desire, and, to avoid error
he has secured a sample from each of the staff en voyage
—to wit, his manager, his secretary, his valet, his two
cooks, and his waiter, together with a small portion from
a mattress belonging to M. Pullman, propriataire of the
coach de luxe which we occupy.” Bloated piano-puncher !
By the way, Paderewski got married the other day.
THE USHER NEAR THE DOOR.
(With Banjo Accompaniment.)
When the show’s an awful duffer
