Melb.
On the Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Talk of cookies’ hurapies and farm-huts as miser-
able hovels—why, they’re perfect palaces compared
to places I’ve been in. I’ve dossed in such cabooses
out-back that old ’Tilda has nearly crawled away
from me. Bush-rhymesters of early days loved to
sing of “ Old Bark Huts,” and the joy of jogging
from one to t’other. 0 Lord !
Four bare walls and a leaky roof are often the
only home of a dozen men. No furniture, no
cleanliness, no anything. Often there are no
bunks. The naps are spread in the dust, and
everyone lies on his bed smoking, and spitting at
random. It’s this way : When there's a job to do,
a man is put on ; when it’s done, he’s sacked, so
none will trouble to rig a bunk that would mean
comfort for the next man.
On most stations there is a lonely little place,
away by itself, like a dead-house, called the
“Travellers’ Hut.” Squatters built them years
ago, when men wero scarce and not so given to
camping-out. It kept them away from sheds and
stables, too, and aloof from the regular hands.
The only difference between the two huts is that
each is worse than the other.
The men’s huts are generally thick to the roof
with tobacco-smoke, and at times half the hands
are drunk, and the abominable fumes of back-
block grog pervade the place; sometimes three
or four are sick, and then you have the smell of
drugs in addition.
The men are mostly illiterate bores. There’s
Bullocky Bill—blatherskiting for hours about what
Strawberry and Rattler can do ; and Sam, the
blacksmith, lecturing on the mending of cracked
saucepans; and the boundary.rider telling you
how long the water will last in this or that tank,
and if there was a sheep bogged in one he’ll be a
solid hour relating how he pulled it out.
“ Ever been to Sydney? ” I once asked by way
of a change.
“ Wal, no,” he said ; “but I was dashed close
ter Eurongella onct. Pulled up at Tally-Ho pub.
an’ got paralytic drunk in two nips.” That’s the
Waybacker’s ambition, to get “ paralytic drunk ”
occasionally, and make history of it in those
loathsome huts on Skeleton Creek and Dead Man’s
Hole.
A reasonably civilised and educated man can
only find disgust in such places. He can’t write
or enjoy a book for the continual swearing
and filthy yarn - telling going on around
him. There’s always some yahoo wanting to get
a pitch on, or to sing the “Old Bark Hut.”
Some want the door shut, some want it open ;
and then there’s always a row over the slush-
lamp. Some can’t sleep with the light burning,
others can’t smoke in the dark. Some one blows
it out; a chorus of voices calls him things un-
printable; and then someone lights itagain. . . .
Most travellers nowadays prefer to camp out.
Gfivon a tree, a bit of grass for “hipper,” a
blanket and a fire, and they’re at home.
The aborigine has taught Bushy a wrinkle in
fire-making. “White man big fool; make um
too big—can’t get close. By’n’bye burn down—
too cold. B'ackfeller make um little fire. All
night se-leep close up—budgeree warm ebery
time.”
By following this advice, however, I once burnt
my blankets.
Camping-out also has other drawbacks. Beetles
and crickets rustle the dead leaves, and have you
every ten minutes peering round with a fire-stick
to see if it’s a snake, or you spot a scorpion or a
centipede crawling over your pillow. I’ve had the
tucker-bag dragged from under my head by
’possums, and have been awakened by crows
pecking at my feet. But the greatest fright I
ever got was on Taabinga Creek, Q. I was dozing
under a tree, when two ’possums, which were
fighting overhead, dropped with a tremendous
thump on to my chest. It was then I uttered my
record yell.
Picked up half a tent once and rigged it like a
fly, blocking the end with two old shirts. Fixed
up a slush-lamp and settled down for a read, and
in five minutes my palace was ablaze. After that
I pegged along with gnnyahs of boughs, bark, old
bags, and sheet-iron. One wet night found me
with an old fellow under a lean-to. We were on
a slope, and towards midnight he complained of
water running in. “Ain’t you gettin’ wet?” he
asked, his teeth chattering. No; I was quite dry.
“Jumpin’ Jerusalem !’1 lie gasped, “the whole
blazin’ Murrumbidgee’s. agin me! ” He raised
himself, the water rushed through, and lin turn
was swamped. “You darned old cuss !” I said ;
“you couldn’t have got any wetter, so what was
the use o’ wettin’ me ! ” “ What yer givin’ us ! ”
he returned, “d’yer take me for a blanky dam ! ”
We sat the rest of the night leaning against one
another.
E.S.S.
The Psychology of a Punch on
the Nose.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The following was told to me by a long- faced
man who abominates fighting.
It happened last Boxing Day, at Olontarf
—a word a man used to my wife caused me
to shove him aside. There was a big crowd,
and we peeled. Dressed, my opponent ap-
peared an insignificant mannikin with little,
wet eyes; unrobed, he was a muscular fury,
with hands like knobs of quartz. He smelt,
ugh ! like a lion. I never saw such a fiend—he
crouched before me a bunch of horrible muscles.
The look in his eyes was sickening.
“ Go on ! ” yelled the crowd.
“Kill him, Harry ! ” whispered my wife. “ Kill
him, kill him ! ”
My hands went up mechanically as he ad-
vanced—sparring, dodging, feinting ; his lips
opened and shut; I saw the veins in his eyes
thicken, smelt the lion-like effluvium that exuded
from his pores. “Stop,” I whispered, “for
mercy’s sake ! ”
“ Yah ! ” His left fist smashed on the point of
my nose. I thought a star-shell had burst in
mid-air above me ; hot blood seemed to spring
from my eyes ; I felt that an army had trampled
on my face. Somebody propped me up. “Kill
him, Harry!” screamed my wife; “kill him,
kill him ! ”
My antagonist was now a mere gesticulating
impression before me—arms stabbing the air,
bubbling with human hatred. Again some invis-
ible power held up my hands, but I saw him
through an alembic of fear : it was his lion-smell
that murdered my courage. It seemed to me that
we had met aeons ago in a dark, wet jungle when
he was the stronger beast, and my physical cosmos
quaked with an instinctive knowledge of defeat.
“ Kill him, Harry ; kill him ! ”
He was stroking the air as a panther dabs at a
mouse; he grinned and talked a ferocious gib-
berish, and I thought that his teeth were alive ;
his ears seemed to flatten against his head. I
tried to speak to him, for I saw that he was pre-
paring to hit me in the same horrible place. I
wanted to tell him to strike .me on the breast,
throat or heart, anywhere save the point of the
nose. I felt that another such punch would jar
both eyes into my brain. And yet I wanted to
appear well before my wife ; I could never live-
down defeat with her.
My enemy drew towards me—a red imp from
Top’net, snarling, feinting, his face livid with
hate.
“ Don’t, don’t; for God’s sake, don’t! ” I whis-
pered. I saw the grin on his face, the sudden
animal curve of his throat as his arm swung back
murderously. “Whoo!” But I dropped howl-
ing at his feet, covering my nose with both hands.
_ “ Coward ! ” It was my wife who kicked my
ribs : the jeers of the crowd didn’t matter.

The Garden of Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All in a garden fair
Love dwelleth, far apart ;
Each rose that bloometh there
Is some true lover’s heart.
Over the mystic wall
That in the dawning gleams
Float odors magical
To fill the world with dreams.
One golden morning-tide
When birds began their song
“ Open the gate,” I cried,
For I have waited long.”
Then, pale as Eve’s white star,
Came Love, and low spake he—
“ Ere I the gate unbar,
Give thou thy heart to me ! ”
My heart from me was drawn
By this sweet wooing tale—
And he grew like the Dawn,
But I grew passion-pale.
Then earth and sky above
Bloomed glorious in mine eyes ;
And, hand in hand with Love,
I walked in Paradise.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The disrespect with which parents treat
their children in this benighted land is harrowing
to the soul. A youth of 40 was recently running
round Brighton (Vic.) in search of police to arrest
his mother, and when a constable accompanied
the tender innocent home, he found the old lady
lying on the floor with a bashed head, adminis-
tered by her pet lamb of a son, who was
righteously indignant when the constable walked
him off, instead of his ferocious parent.
The adven: of the self-driving bike is
ithin measurable distance if Tripler’s Liquid Air
turns out a success. The trouble with the power
driven bike has been the furnace. Petroleum
simply stinks out every street the thing passes
through, and besides, the bike becomes an “auto-
car,’’..that is, a truck or cab, or carriage—anything
in the wide world but a bike. But with liquid
air a'man could carry in his tubes enough motive
power to drive him a hundred miles at top speed.
They begin to quarrel over the “ first
discovery” of gold in Westralia, just as they do
with regard to the event in N.S.W. and Yic.
Mr. R. Austin, a surveyor, now on the Hodgkin-
son (Q.), has lately been trying to get his claim
recognised. Austin was sent out in the early
fifties to explore the N.E. of the province, and he
described some of the geological features of the
Murchison and wrote that it would turn out to be
“ probably one of the finest goldfields in the
world.” But he stopped at that, and claimed
afterwards that his instructions did not cover a
search for minerals. The fact seems to be that
until the pastoral country had been secured “ the
families ” put a heavy, flat foot down on mining
prospects.
Bando’s Son.
A STORY FROM A VILLAGE SETTLEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bando had his peculiarities, verging on eccen-
tricity. His utter inability to recognise a knock-
out seemed to argue a mild species of incipient
insanity. The Settlement had figuratively
jumped on him with both feet and pounded him
into chronic bankruptcy. For all that, he smiled
and faced Fate with a clean shave, a curled
moustache, and a somewhat faded Chesterfield.
Further, he spoke hopefully of the future, and
was full of schemes for the advancement of his
co-workers. There was some mental kink.
He had three sons. The eldest, a steady young
fellow, well-read on many subjects, a civil engineer
by profession, was a fine specimen of manhood—a
handsome, strong-haired, well-set man, with
womanish hands, and the cat-like quality of
muscle so often found in conjunction therewith.
Burnt to a brick, and hardened in the long, use-
less struggle through frosty winter and hell-
suggesting summer, he fought on silently, uncom-
plainingly, against Fate and his sand-patch. So
quietly that no one suspected the grim despera-
tion that prompted his tigerish effort, his search
after taproots deep as Hades, his delving for
water, the rattling pace of the bullock-handled
axe in spite of bleeding, blistered hands. None of
us knew that a girl, and one worth it, lay at the
bottom of it all till Herbert lay beyond the reach of
human help, a pitiful object for human sympathy.
He rode away, leading a horse. The horses
returned, and the insensible body of the man,
with a deep wound in the head, told what story
there was. He put-in a fortnight in the local
hospital, and was discharged cured.
Then he stripped his 15 acres—three bags to the
acre—and sold at Is. 6d. a bushel, to satisfy a
rapacious grocer. With what money he had left
he made a bee-line for Melbourne, and tramped
the streets for weeks in the hopeless quest of
work. Then he bought a horse and cart, and
started for the Settlement, to make another
attempt. The horse bolted, and smashed the
cart. He rode the horse 240 miles, and again
became an inmate of the hospital. A note to his
father, requesting his removal, was the first inti-
mation of trouble. The doctor reported signs of
progressive mental disorder. He came home in
charge of the old man, and was watched care-
fully.
His watchers agreed that he was “balmy.” When
he turned on them with a razor, and five of them
had spent themselves in rendering him powerless,
they agreed that he was a dangerous lunatic. But
Bando could not, or would not, believe that he
was past hope. “ Time would cure him,” he said.
Search through his private papers revealed
“ the girl,’’and the breaking-off of the engagement
some three months previously. Disregarding all
other possible causes, the oid man sent for the
girl, and, brick that she was, she came.
She was a fair, slight, straight-bodied little lady,
with sad eyes and a caress in her voice. I, one of
the watching shift for a certain Sunday, saw her
roam round the paddocks with that madman who
had assured me that Mrs. Yubagang possessed
the Secret of Life and that he’d cut her throat if
she did not yield it up to him. He had also said
that his race had been defiled by a Mongol, and
the injection of pure blood from a rooster would
be the only brake to still the dynamos that were
continually being applied to him. I watched
them, keeping behind, for he was jealous and no-
thing could save her if he turned crusty. She
was talking earnestly to him, striving to recall the
past to him, putting gentle womanly touches to
his collar and tie, and listening gravely, with
steady gray eyes fixed on him, to the peculiarly
keen, mystical madness that blended with the
coarsest of utterances as they fell from his lips.
Every man of us knew, and she knew too, that
the width of a hair only lay between life and
death in his company.
No use ! He grew worse. We subscribed for
his transfer to the Kew Asylum. The watch had
numbered five of late, and at times there was a dis-
tinctly homicidal tendency. The last night all
five of us were in his humpy; a dismal moaning in
the scrub outside, a steady patter of rain on the
roof. He lay on a bunk at one end. His father,
worn out with watching, slept restlessly in the
other bunk. Steel, Mason, and Sevika were
stretched out on the floor in front of the fire. I
sat on a kerosene-case and smoked. Drops of rain
sizzled in the ashes, and presently the fire died
out. I wanted wood, but at the first stir two wild
eyes glared from the bunk at me. In the gloom
of that hut the sensation was paralysing. I
wanted light, and prayed for a fat-lamp ; and still
it moaned and rained, and —I touched the old man.
He turned and groaned. The figure in the far
bunk sprang to a sitting posture and listened in-
tently. Then fell back, and tucking the clothes
viciously around itself, left nothing visible but a
white forehead and two burning eyes. I couldn’t
face it ; so I kicked Steel.
He’d be damned if he’d get up for any con-
founded cocky. “ Take the sack first thing in the
morning. ” The figure sprang out of bed. I col-
lared the poker and kicked Steel viciously. He
got up, and Mason, Sevika, and the old man also.
We watched the process of dressing, and,
strange, there was a faint gleam of insanity
about the lunatic as he carefully arranged tie and
collar and brushed his coat and pants. I asked
him, jocularly, was he going to see a girl. “ Yes,”
he answered, and we knew that our hands would
be full, till 8 o’clock at any rate. Three hours
and a half ahead ! —and what to do to amuse him
till then we didn’t know.
We coaxed and persuaded. He got away once,
but at a call from me came back like a disobedient
boy and expressed contrition.
There was an old fiddle hanging on the wall.
None of us could play except the old man. He
knew one tune, “John Brown’s Body,” and he
played it. His son joined us as we sang—sang
louder and more musically than any of us, laughed
heartily and took it up again. We stuck to it till
our throats were dry, our tongues parched—and
what a picture it made ! The old man in his
shabby gentility, sitting on the edge of the bunk,
with a half-smile on his worn old face, sawing out
“John Brown’s Body” over and over again on a
crotchety fiddle, while his mad son sang it with
the preoccupation of a child in his game. The
grey of the morning found us there, and never did
I see a more weirdly-pathetic sight.
At eight we escorted him to see “ the girl.” At
ten he was on the road to Kew, and there he is
now. Also, there you will be, if you fall in love
and attempt to make a home for your wife on a
Village Settlement.
Vic.
L.M.D.
One American iron-working concern turns
out annually nearly 2,000,000 tons of pig-iron,
besides about 1,000,000 tons of steel ingots and
650,000 tons of rails ; 8,176,000,0001 b. of metal in
a year!
A troublous question for Queensland (or
three-fourths of that province) in the future will
be the scarcity of building timber. The South
and the centre are becoming rapidly denuded of
pine and hardwood, and it is no unusual thing to
see American timber brought into Q. ports. On
the other hand, the Northern coast districts
(Cairns in particular) are wonderfully rich in
building material, and it is no uncommon thing
to see houses, h 'tels, and churches almost entirely
built of cedar—the cheapest timber obtainable
out Mareeba way.
S.A. Govt, keeps pegging away at its
artesian bores in the Far North. ITp from Her-
gott there are 16 of them completed or in progress
—some, as at Hergott, under 400 ft. deep. Others,
as at Kopperamanna, over 3000 ft. Just at pre-
sent, the track from Queensland’s S.W. border is
having most attention. By the way, though the
Federal Govt, will not take over the Mines Depts.
it ought, at an early date, to promote a geological
survey on a national scale for the purpose of more
accurately defining the artesian-water areas. So
far, Q., N.S.W., and S.A. have done much, but
have done it disjointedly. Thousands of pounds
have been wasted ; but where success has been
struck, such bright spots have been made in the
droughty country that many more of them will be
welcomed with great joy. And when that much
has happened—perhaps before—there will be need
to get a better use made of the flows than is
mostly the case nowadays.
As long as Chinese sailors and stewards are
allowed to land without proper check, so long will
Australia’s anti-alien Acts be evaded. A dozen
dungaree-clad heathen go ashore, make for Lilly-
buck or Lower George-street, and in some slum of
Melbourne or Sydney simply swop their garments
with other Chows desirous of leaving their bones
in their native land. The bogus nauticals go on
board, at night if possible, and pass count by the
customs officer. Sometimes they are well at sea
ere the dodge is disclosed. If they are discovered
sooner why should the shipping eo. do anything
except kick them privately? The liability of the
co. for Chows left behind is supposedly sufficient
guarantee, but if the co. can’t find its own China-
men, and some other Chinamen who will pass
muster just as well in the eye of the law are willing
to personate them, why should it inform on itself
and ask to be severely jumped upon ?
